THE RED PAGE.
A POET AND A POET
TO Gilbert Murray’s work one looks with
peculiar interest, for his first breath drew
Australian air. Even one may argue an
Australian taste in his preference of Eu-
ripides to iEschylus. The “ Hippolytus ” and
“ Bacchte ” he gave us last year in a rhyming
translation ; the “ Trojan Women ” v -is just to
hand ; and “Electra ”is coming. His version of
the “ Trojan Women ” is a noble piece of work, a
book shining with beauty. In this play Euripides
gave grief a voice, and Murray rises and matches
him in a new tongue. Of a great poem he has
made a great poem.
How much and how many the loss of Troy
has inspired ! how art has ennobled the myth !
Euripides, one of the first, has seen and shaped
his subject as only genius can. Troy has
fallen; Priam is dead, and the mighty Hector;
and the Greeks, their evil mission accom-
plished, are about to embark for their own
land, bearing with them the Trojan women
as captives. There is a period of suspense when
tbe Greeks are making ready to sail, and the
women wait their pleasure beneath the ruined
walls of the city. In the dusk of early dawn the
gods of fallen Troy appear and introduce the play.
Then, as the day comes, Hecuba wakes, the widow
of the slain monarch. Her and the others Murray
interprets, following his master in felicitous mea-
sures (which one thinks, but for Swinburne, would
not have been precisely what they are). Beautiful
lines and verses abound :
I that was Queen in Troy i
He loved her for that same strange holiness.
For tears are music too, and keep
A song unheard in hearts that weep.
... No wife came
With gentle arms to shroud the limbs of them
For burial, in a strange and angry earth
Laid dead.
0,1 will think of things gene long ago
And weave them to a song, like one more tea.-
In the heart of misery.
The lovely word Troy, that B-ossetti appre-
ciated, gives help. Occasionally Murray uses one
of Tennyson’s familiar devices: the overflow on
a strong word.
... and there at the altar Priam fell
Murdered, and round him all his citadel
Sacked.
J*
In the play Hecuba’s splendid wail—
Take this slave that once trod high
In Ilion ; cast her on her bed of clay
Rock-pillowed, to lie down, and pass away
Wasted with tears. And what so man they call
Happy, believe not ere the last day fall l
—is followed by a finely-planned version of the
chant of the chorus. Presently Andromeda, wife
of Hector, mingles her tears with those of Hecuba,
his mother :
Hf.c. Troy, Troy is gone !
And. Yea, and her treasure parted.
Hro. Gone, gone, mine own
Children, the noble-hearted !
And. Sing, sorrow...
Hko. For me, for me t
And. Sing for the Great City,
That falleth, falleth to be
A shadow, a fire departed.
And. Come to me, omy lover l
Hsc. The dark shroudeth him over,
My flesh, woman, not thine, not thine !
And. Make of thine arms my cover.
And Hecuba sings :
0 thou whose wound was deepest.
Thou that my children keepest,
Priam, Priam, 0 age-worn King,
Gather me where thou sleepest.
At p. 47 the speech of Andromache —
Forgive met It is not easy—
should surely be oredited to Talthybius ? He, the
herald, comes to take from Andromache her little
child Astyanax, whom the Greeks have ordered to
be thrown from the wall of Troy. Says Andro-
mache :
Weepest thou ?
Nay, why, my little one t Thou canst not know.
And Father will not come ; he will not come ;
Not once, the great spear flashing, and the tomb
Riven to set thee free !
Soon the chorus gives Murray an opportunity to
ose the singing measure which he handles so ad-
mirably. Then comes Menelaus seeking Helen, the
origin of evil. ‘‘He is a prey to violent and conflict-
ing emotions,” says Murray in a phrase that has
been made impossible in Australia since the egre-
gious Meagher, “ coming from Bondi in the tram,
was torn asunder with conflicting emotions.” Helen
defends herself, and Hecuba attacks her, in a
Seat scene finely rendered. It is curious to find
ecuba urging that Helen was a willing prey,
with the argument that appeals to modern police-
courts :
It was by force my son
Took thee, thou sayst, and striving... Yet not one
In Sparta knew ! No cry, no sudden prayer
Rang from thy rooms that night. .. Castor was there
To hear thee, and his brother : both true men,
Not yet among the stars !
That is, their evidence was still available if Helen
pressed a defence. The chorus takes up the tale ;
Talthybius returns with the body of the child
Astyanax ; and Hecuba utters immemorial grief :
Ah, what a death hath found thee, little one !
Hadst thou but fallen fighting, hadst thou known
Strong youth and love and all the majesty
Of godlike kings, then had toe spoken of thee
As of one blessed.. .could, in any wise
These days know blessedness. But now thine eyes
Have seen, thy lips have tasted, but thy soul
No kncnvledge had nor usage of the whole
Rich life that lapt thee roy/nd Poor little child !
Was it our ancient wall, the circuit piled
By loving Gods, so savagely hath rent
Thy curls, these little flowers innocent
That were thy mother's garden, where she laid
Her kisses ; here, just where the bone-edge f rayed
Grins white above—Ah heaven, I will not see !
Ye tender arms, the same dear mould have ye
As his ; haw from the shoulder loose ye drop
And weak! And dear proud tips, so full of hope
And closed for ever ! ' What false words ye said
At daybreak, when he crept into my bed,,
Called me kind names, and promised : ‘ Grandmother,
When thou art dead, I will cut close my hair,
And lead out all the captains to ride by
Thy tomb.' Why didst thou cheat, me so ? 'T is /,
Old, homeless, childless, that for thee must shed
Cold, tears, so young, so miserably dead.
Dear God, the pattering welcomes of thy feet,
The nursing in my lap ; and O, the sweet
Falling asleep together! All is gone.
Then comes one of the characteristic touches of
“our Euripides the human.” Hecuba shrouds
the small body in the garments which she had
treasured, as women will, for a wedding-gift.
Glory of Phrygian raiment, which my thought
Kept for thy bridal day with some far-sought
Queen of the East, folds thee for evermore.
A crash is heard, and the wall of great Troy
falls. The trumpet sounds again, and the women
go out in the darkness to the ships.
A ad forth, 10, the women go,
The crown of War, the crown of Woe,
To bear the children of the foe
And weep, weep, for Ilion !

N.B.W. PUBLIC LIBRARY
THE perfunctory and ignorant enquiry of
N.S.W. Public Works Committee, re-
garding the site and building of a new
Public Library, was designed apparently
to give the semblance of official sanction to a cut-
and-dried conclusion. The action of Premier
Carruthers in procuring Parliamentary approval
of the Committee’s conclusion, after some hours’
perfunctory and ignorant debate, wore the sinister
air that has attached to other public actions of
Premier Carruthers. It appears from the news-
paper reports, which alone are available at this
writing, that he gave the Assembly no definite in-
formation regarding the character and conditions
of the Mitchell gift. As regards its value, he is
reported to have said that this would approxi-
mate 200,000?. In the absence of other evidence,
one must suppose that he believes this. In the
absence of other evidence, one must suppose that
he is foolish to believe it.
The value of the Mitchell gift can be tested in
two ways : by ascertaining its cost to the donor,
or by the estimate of experts. So far as the
public knows, neither test has been applied. If
one assumes from the limited information avail-
able that the collection cost 20,000?., then its cash
value is possibly 20,000?. If it were sold under
the best conditions, one is extremely doubtful
whether it would realise more. If the donor paid
20,000?. within the last twenty years, one is
extremely doubtful whether the cash value will
double during the next twenty years. For the
items of the collection apparently have a varying
commercial value and little intrinsic value.
J*
Apparently they have for Australians a senti-
mental value due to their curios 'ty and rarity. That
value, though it may be considerable, is still
limited ; and certainly does not add 180,000?. to
the value of the gift. “It is said” that the
donor purposes adding a money endowment. In
that case, particulars should be given. Why
should Parliament be asked to vote public money
on false or imaginary or insufficient data ? Why
should a transaction honourable and profitable be
completed furtively and hurriedly ?
The Assembly appears vaguely to have believed
that it was voting for the public benefit; and the
bait of a financial bargain was attached —as one
believes, falsely. For a building adequate to con-
tain the Mitchell gift could be erected (according
to the estimate of an authoritative Telegraph
contributor) for 10,000?. ; and the building
authorised may cost 125,000?. or more. 10,000?.
for 20,000?. is a good investment; 125,000?. for
20,000?. is not. And if this hypothetical criticism
blunder for lack of data, how much more serious
a blunder was the Assembly vote, similarly lack-
ing data ?
Jt
The public benefit from the Mitchell gift, apart
from its cash value, is a doubtful thing. The gift
is spoken of as a library : it is rather a museum of
antiquities. Public expenditure on antiquities
should be governed by their value in relation to
national life, and this really is very small. For
hundreds of years to come the material develop-
ment of Australia will be far more important than
the accumulation of literary curiosities: it will
always be more important. Literary curiosities
are proper subjects of private pleasure, but their
national value is slight. One grudges the expen-
diture upon them of public money badly needed
for the promotion of industry and enlightenment.
F. M. Bladen, a N.S.W. public librarian, re-
cently printed a laborious essay devoted to ascer-
taining upon what precise rock in Botany Bay
Captain Cook first set foot. The riddle is a pro-
per amusement for learned leisure, but it has no
public importance whatever. It does not matter
a scrap where Captain Cook landed, or whether
he never landed at all. What does matter is
that there is a ragged woman sweltering under an
iron shed at Bourke, whose children are sickening
for want of wholesome food. Similarly, the
MS. of Captain Cook’s journal is an interesting
curiosity; but if he had never kept a journal Aus-
tralia would suffer no loss whatever. Loss comes
when a despairing farmer loses his year’s labour
for want of an artificial water supply to supple-
ment insufficient rainfall.
Some members of Parliament and others are
apt to regard books as fetishes. Just as the
devout of the Dark Ages worshipped alleged
feathers from the wing of the Archangel Michael,
so the devout of the Dark Ages worship first folios
of Shakespear. Such things have private interest,
but no public value unless they exist for enlighten-
ment. Books, in particular, have several uses : as
a drug for life’s pains, or as an addition to
life’s pleasures, or as an inspiration to life’s
battle for life. It is only in the last mode
that they become of serious importance in
the scale of national values. The Mitchell museum,
it may be assumed, is no fountain of inspiration.
It may fairly be doubted whether it will help
one citizen to live more nobly or to die more
bravely. That it contains material valuable
for the study of Australian history is undoubted,
and the study of Australian history is of the very
least importance in national life. Boys and girls
can be taught in a month all they need to know
of it: all that will be of the least help in making
them useful citizens. For strong bodies and
enlightened minds we depend upon lessons in
far different fields. Not to museums, but to the
common schools of the country, should public
money be devoted.
Jf
The Mitchell gift is a splendid gift, to be ac-
cepted gratefully and housed fitly. But the pre-
cise nature, value, and conditions of the gift
should be ascertained before Parliament votes
(borrowed !) money on apparently a dispropor-
tionate scale, for expenditure on a plan and a site
which are not plainly the best available.
RECENT BOOKS
Wild Wings, by H. K. Job (Lond., Constable;
10s. 6d.), describes “the adventures of a camera-hunter
among' the larger wild birds of North America on sea and
land,” and makes a handsome volume in small 4to, with
160 photographic illustrations. One does not suppose
the book will appeal to more Australians than a few, but
these are likely to delight in it. The author’s enthusiasm
is transparently sincere, and sincerity is essential nowa-
days, when the vogue of “ nature-study ” brings to light
so much deliberate enthusiasm, such a flood of senti-
mentality laboriously pumped. The photographs are
especially charming. Such a book of Australian birds and
animals would be welcome.
A. & H. Constable, London, publish several
Dovels transferred from the U.S.A. The Colonel’s
Dream, by C. W. Chesnutt (2s. 6 d.), is the story of a man
who tried to enlighten and benefit an Amer. township,
and was beaten by the forces of meanness, ignorance, and
prejudice, rampant in small Amer. townships as else-
where. The interest is especially American, and the book
has no remarkable value.
In Rose o’ The River (2s. 6 d.) K. D. Wiggin writes a con-
ventional love story, without any arresting quality. This
is another book with a particular Amer. appeal unfelt
elsewhere.
It seems as if in The Little Shepherd John Pox, jr.,
wrote the only story he has to tell. A Cumberland
Vendetta (3s. 6rf.) recurs to the idea of the family feud in
which hero and heroine are on opposite sides. The slight
story is sufficiently well told, but has no particular im-
portance. , , ,
The Reckoning, by R. W. Chambers (2s. 6 d.), has a form
now too stereotyped to enchant us. The period is that
of the Amer. War of Independence, with the lady and her
lover thrown again into hostile camps, and a new shibbo-
leth, Thendara ! used as Lorraine ! was used in a former
story. Mechanically the book is well handled, and the
epoch affords ample colour. The heroine has the pleasant
individuality of Chambers’s heroines : she is like the others,
with a formal difference. On the whole, however, there is
a loss of gaiety and novelty, and the book does not enthrall.
A fresh story and good, to be commended highly, is The
Comihg of the Tide, by Margaret Sherwood (25. 6d.) The
author does not acknowledge previous work, and if this
be her beginning it is a beginning worth while. The atti-
tude is new, the style excellent; and the story holds one
until the last chapter, where, alas, it is wrecked upon an
unconvincing crisis. “0 ! ” says the author, “if Swin-
burne had not been Swinburne what sealike poems he
might have written ! ”
The Toll of the Bush, by W. Satehell (Lond.,
Macmillan ; 2s. 6 d.), is a graphic story of Maoriland, with
some powerful dramatic passages ; in particular, the story
of the fire. As a novel, the book fails for want of a strong
and shapely plot.
The Last Chance, by “ Rolf Boldrewood ”
(Lond., Macmillan ; 2s. 6 d.), is an extraordinary concoc-
tion of Australian and English impressions—a cinemato-
graph of the author’s mind up to the date of this year’s
war, with Bully Hayes elbowing selected verses from
ordsworth, and Australian mining experience salted by
criticism of Sir Walter Scott. All the surplusage
trimmed from previous books seems to have found its
way into the mixture, which is not without excellent
irrelevancies.
The late Anna Swan wick’s version of Faust is
republished in the “ York Library ” (Lond., Bell; 25. 6 d.
net)- The translator was not heaven-born, and work like
Gilbert Murray’s puts us out of conceit with her. Faust,
indeed, still wants an English interpreter, and is worth
him : one fears it will not be “ her.” After all argument
the law remains : you must not make of a good poem a
bad one ; which amounts to saying that in metrical trans-
lation literality must yield to beauty. People who must
be literal may have prose, but in verse the most beautifu
paraphrase is the best version. Miss Swan wick is solic
and conscientious, but... she is so sadly uninspired I
What can one say of this?—
And, as waned my strength, no longer
Rolled the sea upon the shore :
Prudent lords, bold serfs directing,.
It with trench and dyke restrained ;
Or this :
Baucis, to my lips half-dying,
Art thou, who refreshment gave t
Thou Philemon, strength who plying,
Snatched my treasure from the wave i
The translation swarms with such clumsy inversions.
The famous ending of the second part appears thus :
All of mere transient date,
As symbol showeth :
Here, the inadequate
To fulness groweth ;
Here the ineffable
Wrought is in love ;
The ever-womanly
Draws us above.
How is it possible for a reader, ignorant of the original,
to gather from this the original spirit ? Yet one must add
that, brooded upon, Miss Swanwick’s dogged consci-
entiousness does yield a certain flavour ; and through her
glass darkly Goethe can be seen. The present edition is
prefaced by Karl Breul with advantage.
Walter Jerrold’s Charles Lamb (in Bell’s
“Miniature Series,” Is. net) is succinct and adequate on
its scale. Between too-much and nob-enough Jerrold has
hit the happy mean. His account of Lamb’s life is read-
able : his criticism, without being remarkable, is just;
and he gives many hints for readers’ own extension.
The little book is bettered by the illustrations, including
two portraits and a reproduction of a letter in Lamb’s
handwriting. A chronological list of works is added, and
a reference to the chief books to consult.
The Physical Configuration of the Australian
Continent, by Ernest Favenc (Syd.; Brooks), is a short
essay adequately represented by its title, and published
as a booklet. As the pn face states, “the feature is the
maps,” which are interesting and useful. The relief-n.aps
suggest the question whether the original models are
covered by the Commonwealth Copyright Bill; and the
answer is doubtful.
Hubert Bland, author of With the Eyes of a Man
(Lond., Laurie ; 3s. 6 d.), earns affinity with Australia
through his wife, E. Ncsbit, a distinguished writer of
verse. This volume contains a series of essays, apparently
reprinted from Lond. Sunday Chronicle. The subjects
are modern and interesting : sex and heredity, beauty
and G. B. Shaw ; and the author’s ideas are modern,
though here not novel. His style, however, is too
smooth, its pace too slow for an Australian taste: he
shoots his arrows dexterously, but they lack points and
barbs to pierce and hold.
The House by the River, by Florence Warden
(Lond., Unwin, 3s. 6ef.), shows what may happen to a
typewriting girl if she is pretty enough and good enough.
A handsome young man will fall in love to her, according
to the Law of Gravitation ; but that is not all. She may
walk suddenly into the middle of the most exciting melo-
drama, where strange scoundrels scutter about like cock-
roaches, and stimulating outrages rise like coffee-fumes
from elegant suburban “grounds.” One supposes that
the emotions and colours are violent, but one supposes
that many people will not mind. Tim pretty typewriter
is almost persuaded to come to Australia, when she must
come with a convicted villain of the piece ; but when the
handsome, tall, noble, intellectual young man embraces
his own on the last page they go to. ..Canada. Here is a
nice adjustment of colonial valuations.
In Helena (Edin., Blackwood; 2s. 6 d.) Mrs.
H. O. Forbes writes a story of Maoriland which may
interest Christchurch people. Our interest was lost in
the scrub of insignificant observations that crowd the
MEMORANDA

In The Westminster Gazette were recently
printed twelve chapters on education, bv
£ Kappa,” under the title “ If Youth But Knew ”
The matter is so good that surely it will be re-
printed, and should then be bought by all i n .'
terested in education.
“Why should not youth know?” asks the writer.
“Why should its best years be portioned outbatween
dead task-work and idle child’s-play, both seeming deliber-
ately calculated to conceal from it the splendour and the
mystery of this strange adventure of life, on which, for a
little space, it is embarked ? We are given some two or
three score years to enjoy the pageant of the universe
and contemplate the miracle of existence ; and we let our
ingenuous youth waste their intelligence on dismal
pedantries and their admiration on despicable trifles.*
And he goes on: 1
“ I believe that the efficiency, the morality, and above
all the happiness of coming generations might be inoal.
eulably promoted by a radical change in our system of
education. I believe that the conscious and deliberate
aim of education should be to enable a man, in the fullest
sense of the word, to enjoy life ; and that the enjoyment
of life, for anyone who has time and means to cultivate
his intelligence, is partly, no doubt, dependent on the
gratification of physical appetite and the exertion of
physical energy, but far more essentially and permanently
on the intellectual apprehension of the sublimities and
jn.vsteries of the universe and of the human lot.
“ What, then, is the fundamental task of a liberal edu-
cation ? What should be its constant endeavour ? Surely
to awaken and to keep ever alert the faculty of wonder in
the human sou 1 . To take life as a matter of course—
whether painful or pleasurable—that is the true spiritual
death. From the body of that death it is the task of .edu-
cation to deliver us.” \
Longman's Magazine expires formally of mani-
tion and Andrew Lang : it died spiritually years ago.
The editor’s farewell attributes decease to his high
literary standard ; but in our view it is precisely because
his standard was not high that his magazine died. His
policy was partly a policy of “ names,” and even the
English public seems learning to disregard names and ask
for values. Because Andrew Lang was a bright writer
twenty years ago, he has been allowed to be tedious
month after month, and to lead a faded cohort to a battle
where they always fell before they saw the enemy. Ail
the vitality having been fogied out of the magazine, its
obvious course was to lie down.
Maori land correspondence prophesies more
power to Seddon’s elbow. In one aspect we do not
grudge it, for he has given us two good moments. One
came when he closed a robust oratorical period with this
phrase :
“ The Mother Country will always find New Zealand her
ihs-a-vis, following close behind her.” -jit.
The other flashed when he clinched an argument with
this simile unparalleled :
“It is like a man who fancies he is riding, a nors-ae-
combat when he is astride on a miserable nag.”
To the fifth number of Art and Architecture,
G. Sydney Jones contributes both the best article and
the best drawing. He pleads for an original note in Aus-
tralian architecture, and urges his brethren not to be
content with copying the work of the past :
“We have a fine and costly building, for instance, in
the Art Gallery [N.S.W.] Its general form and composi-
tion are good, and its detail is refined, but the whole might
have been done vastly better, both in form and detail,
centuries since bv a Greek or a Roman architect ; for it is
designed almost wholly in the spirit of Greek and Roman
work. Even the detail in parts— e.g., ihe main entrance
doorway, is as nearly as possible the 'opy of a Grees
doorway. Do not misunderstand me, not for one moment
would I decry the work, which as a design is admirable,
hut as a piece of modern architecture I submit that it
fails, simply because it is not modern.
A fragment of.Char’es Lamb, quoted by a
reviewer of the last biography ;
“ I have made a little scale,” says Charles, “ supposing
myself to receive the following various, accessions of dig-
nity from the King, who is the fountain of honour:—As
at first, 1, Mr. C. Lamb ; 2, 0, Lamb, Esq. ; 3, Sir C. Lamb,
Bart. ; 4, Baron Lamb of Stamford, where my family came
from —I have chosen that if ever I should have my °hoice ,
5, Viscount Lamb ; 6, Earl Lamb ; 7, Marquis Lamb; 6,

Model 1905 Self-Loading Rifle
This rifle is a six shot, hammerless take-down, made in .32 and .35 calibers,
the cartridges being of the modern smokeless powder type. The recoil of
the exploded cartridge ejects the empty shell, cocks the hammer and feeds
a fresh cartridge from the magazine into the chamber, leaving the rifle ready
to shoot upon the operator’s pulling the trigger. The self-loading system
adds much to the pleasure of rifle shooting, either at target or game. The
list price of this model is $28.00.
These Rifles For Sale By All Dealers.

LEICHHARDT TESTIMONY.
EVERY LOCALITY SENDS ITS TRIBUTE.
The Case of Mrs. A. YOUNC.
(By a Sydney Reporter.)
The principal feature for us of the
stories of adversity which have .been
the lot of others is that they g iv ? »
clue to the manner in which similar
experiences can be effectively dealt with
if they come our way. Here is a nar-
rative which will assuredly put many a
reader on the right track—one supplied
to us by Mrs. Annie Young, ot Myrtle-
street, Leichhardt. It was at Mrs.
Young’s present address, where she has
been living for the past eleven yeais,
that our special . correspondent was
granted the interview which appears
prpnnupr *——
“I’m in search of news,’’ said the
newspaper man. “Do you mind letting
me know something’ about tne altijc-
tions you encountered whilst living in
Bay-street, Glebe? where .you
were living at the time, I’m told !
“Yes,'*’ answered Mrs. Young ; ‘and
it is thirteen years since I had the ill-
ness you refer to. Truly, the time does
My. It doesn’t seem half so long ago,
tor everything is as fresh in my me-
mory as though it was only yester-
day. Oh ! .1. was unwell for a good
while. Hardly knew what to do from
one (lay to another owing to the leeble
condition . I was in. Times being so
bad I could not afford to keep a ser-
vant, yet I was quite unable to get
about and do the work myself, and the
consequence was that things had to
take their chance.’ 7
“Were you experiencing any physical
pain ?’’
“Oh ! don’t talk ab<put it. Have you
ever had neuralgia in the head ? I
hope not, for it was something dread-
ful. Right across the forehead,
through ,from one temple to the other,
and up to the top of mv head, the
most acute pain imaginable harassed
me. The agony was so great that of-
ten 1 imagined something was pressing-
on my brain, and I really used to
think I .would go mad with it. It had
such an effect upon me, indeed, that
even my sight went bad, and as for the
feeling in my eyes—well, they felt as if
they were double the size they should
have been. Ah ! what desolate, me-
lancholy days they were ! Km sure 1
felt myself to be about the most un-
happy woman in tho world, and I
think I had occasion to be in view of
the fact that the outlook was so
black.’’
“Do you mean that your prospects
of getting strong again seemed re-
mote ?’’
‘■They did, indeed, for I had been at-
tended to by several doctors, and the
most they could do for me was to give
just a little relief now and then ; but
there was nothing lasting about it.
and if I felt a trifie better one day I
was sure to feel ten times worse the
next, and the medicine, besides being
so ineffective, was always disagreeable
to take. A strange thing was that I
felt always so worn out, drowsy and
exhausted—as if I was only lit to go
somewhere and lie down and die—yet
when .1 made an effort to sleep, do you
think I could? Not a bit of it. I’d
twist and turn nearly the whole night
through, and when at last T dozed off
the dreams I had were fearful. T al-
ways appeared to be struggling against
something that was holding me in its
clutches, hideous sights flitted through
my imaginative brain, and when I
woke up, which was usually with a
s t ar . t ’, L felt terribly agitated and
a raid Really, I used to dislike tho
idea of going t,o sleep on account of
those dreams ; besides. I never felt
rested afterwards, as T got up feeling
more weary than ever. No wonder [
thought I would never get stroncr
again, for I got absolutely no benefit
whatever from any food T took. Soon
after eating J suffered dreadfully t ro m
flatulence, the wind rising* from the
stomach, and leaving a sour taste that
was similar to the disagreeable flavour
I had in my mouth each morning ;
then, a little later, my chest becama
painful and uncomfortable, with a feel-
ing as it a stone had got inside, and
had closed up the breathing passages,
for when I tried to take a long breath
it hurt me."
"And you couldn’t get permanent re-
lief from these symptoms through the
medicine prescribed for you Y"
".No, unfortunately. But linally I got
something to do me good. Clements
Tonic it was, and the best remedy I
ever had in all my life, too. Good-
ness, what a show it made of every-
thing else 1 had taken, for instead of
only easing me a little occasionally, it
went right to the base of my iniirmi-
ties, and lifted them out by the roots !
In addition to what I’ve already told
you, 1 must state that I was highly
nervous—nobody could be more so, for
1 was actually afraid to stay in my
own house by myself, and was con-
tinually worrying myself about things
that 1 imagined were going to happen.
Trouble was always hanging over me,
and I couldn’t get the idea out of my
head—not until I had been taking Cle-
ments Tonic for a space of time, when,
quite mysteriously, I ceased brooding
over such silly things, and my mind
became as happy as the day. Oh ! it
was wonderful what an altered woman
Clements Tonic made me. Instead of
being anxious and timid all day, I got
as happy and cheerful as possible—no-
thing frightened me, and my nerves
were steady. It is because I know of
so many nervous women that I deem
it my duty to say what a splendid re-
medy Clements Tonic is for that com-
plaint, though, for that matter, it was
equally beneficial to me in other ways.
I used to get pains down the small of
my back and about my shoulders, al-
so palpitation and pains in the region
of the heart, which made me think
that something very serious was
wrong with me there : but Clements
Tonic showed itself to be such a pain-
removing medium that I can faithfully
recommend it to all sufferers. Its re-
gulating influences on the heart were
extraordinary, and for making one’s
sleep sound and peaceful, the same re-
medy was the only one to meet with
my requirements. I wish I had used it
months before, for plenty of people
told me what a grand medicine Clef,
ments Tonic was, and—"
"How did you come to use it at
last ?’’
"I was laid up at the time, thin,
pale, and despairing, when I suddenly
thought of it,'and I asked a friend to
get some. She did ; I regained my
health and strength through its in-
fluence over me, and thus my worldly
happiness was restored. JN'ever as long
as T live shall I forget what a good,
regular appetite Clements Tonic gave
me ; how it stopped the giddy sensa-
tions that had troubled me from com-
ing back ; and how effectively it ended
my tormenting head and body pains.
The least I can do is to assist my
neighbours to throw off their afflic-
tions, so you may publish my experi-
ences in any manner you like."
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ANNIE YOUNG. of Myrtle-street,
Leichhardt, in the State of New-
South Wales. Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing- of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from odo to five, and that it contains and
is a true, and faithful account, of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without, receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisiohs of the Oaths Act. 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Petersham this
filth day of Sententber, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me.
H. EVANS, J.P.

Various Verses.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Paudheen’s Fairy.
Paudlieen took leave of
His comrades gay,
Upon the eve of
The first of May ;
With heart undaunted
He trod the path
Unto the haunted
Green Fairy Rath.
Sore wept his mother.
“ Avic ! Machree !
Where was another
Son dear as he ?
He’s gone for ever !
Too well I know
The fairies never
Will let him go.”
The wind went soughin’
Across the land;
A branch of rowan
Was in her hand ;
Witch-ha/.els bended
Their shadows lean.
Her cry ascended —
“Paudlieen ! Paudlieen !”
It was the night, and
The charmed hour,
When elf and sprite and
Queen Meeve have power.
Was it, perchance, heard,
That cry so keen ?
The lone hill answered,
“Paudlieen ! Paudlieen !”
But Paudlieen, lying
On magic ground.
Of that sore crying
Heard not a sound—-
For, through the springing
Green grass, rose clear
A sound of singing
Most sweet to hear.
No wild, marsli-firisli,
Witch-chant he heard,
But kindly Irish
Was every word.
The strain rose reeling—-
He heard, the rogue,
The song, heart-stealing,
Of Tir-nan-oyc.
The corn was springing
Where once was loam —
When, softly singing,
Paudheen came home
His step was airy,
His lips apart—
The Singing Fairy
Was in Ins heart.
N.S. W.
VIOIOR J. SALEY.
Aden.
She joined the ship at Aden, whence the mad-
boats march phalanxed,
With the standards of all nations trailing from
their spurning spars;
She joined the ship at Aden, and a thrill ran
through each crank’s head
And went throbbing, throbbing, throbbing up
toward the steadfast stars !
She came aboard at sunset when the dust hung
over Aden,
She was Egypt writ in woman —splendid Sheba’s
vivid charms;
She was frankincense incarnate —she was fire-and
passion laden —
An 1 the great red moon of Ismail saw her smiling
in my arms !
The Indian Ocean opened—its wide bosom spread
before us,
But we'd lost our souls at Aden, by the gates of
Pharaoh s Sea.
They were lost and gone like Isis and Osiris and
old Idorus,
But man needs no soul at Aden where it fries
eternally.
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
Excelsior.
I stood in an archway dimly vast, in the court of
the Halls of Day ;
And I saw the night on star-fringed wings, pass
swift to the west away ;
And the infant morn, like an opening flowi r, its
petals fair unfold,
Flushed with the light of a rosy dawn, and ba'hed
in a sea of gold.
And I thought of the men who slept and rose,
who toiled and worked and prayed,
And laid their spoils at the day’s decl.ne in the
fires their hands had made.
And I saw myself, with my petty aims, my puny
joys and fears,
Only one among millions more, in the mist of a
million years.
But I knew there were some with souls of fire, and
mighty hearts of love,
Who climbed from the plains of the commonplace
to the heights which tower above;
And there was a way and a place for each, though
the powers opposed were great,
But I was a child with empty hands, and a path-
way desolate.

By sending direct to Tiif, Bulletin Office Twenty-Six

Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
H out to A ddress Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Aof ice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short 'Talcs or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clipping 's.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood, that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

WANTED-A GUN
At time of writing the Australian atmosphere is
full of reports about a despatch in which the
British Government has “ peremptorily ordered ”
Westralia to repeal the anti-Chinese clauses of
its Factories Act, on the ground that they are
“repugnant to the feelings of nations with which
Britain is at peace.” Exactly how peremptory
the despatch is no one save Premier Rason knows
just yet, but that is a matter of small import-
ance ; if the British attitude is not yet quite as
peremptory as it might be, it will doubtless expand
later on. To the unthinking there is something
wildly grotesque about the British view of things,
seeing that for months British subjects in the
Transvaal have been flogging and torturing thou-
sands of Chinese —the subjects of a nation with
which Britain is at peace —and have injured their
backs as well as their feelings; that they have
done this in a colony which is under direct
British rule, so that, as far as the responsi-
bility goes, the British Government was, and still
is, itself the illegal flogger and torturer ; and that
the British Government only gave up denying the
atrocities perpetrated on the subjects of an alleged
friendly nation when it found itself liable to be
drowned in a sea of popular execration if it con-
tinued to lie on behalf of the mine-owners of
Jo’burg. It doesn’t seem to be, even now, one-
tenth as peremptory over the illegal tortures of
Chinese in Africa as it is over the factory legis-
lation of Westralia, for the tortures and unlawful
punishments still continue. But the Chow-flog-
ging in Africa is intended to extract larger divi-
dends for England’s beloved German Jew, while
the Chinese factory legislation of Westralia is
only intended to prevent the white artisan of pure
British descent being starved out of his job.
Therefore, to those who know England, the posi-
tion is understandable enough. England exists
nowadays mostly for the German Jew.
* ♦ * *
But what Australia has to consider now is not
Britain’s attitude—at present, the German Jew
disease there seems to be incurable —but its own.
It has got a clear, broad, well-defined hint
that its White Australia policy is a house of cards
which may collapse at any moment. There isn’t
a gun behind it, and no great policy is worth any-
thing without a guti. The community which, situ-
ated as Australia is, declares in the teeth of Asia
that it will make its land a white man’s land needs
to have a fast-growing population to begin with.
It needs to be attracting desirable white immi-
grants wholesale—and seeing that Britain alone
sends out over 250,000 annually of its own race,
and that this country has also Western Canada
and the Western part of the United States to
draw from, there is no lack of material. It
needs to stop importing, and manufacture for it-
self under an ironclad tariff, and to cut up
the great coastal estates remorselessly, so as to
provide work and land for its own people and for
hordes of desirable white immigrants. It needs
to be able to put into the field at the shortest
notice at least 200,000 men, all well-armed and
reasonably well-trained ; and to possess a fleet at
least as good as that of Holland, with its 17 ser-
viceable fighting ships—a .fleet of its own which
will be here when it is wanted. It needs to pay
off its debt in time of peace, so that when war
comes it will have good credit to fall back upon,
and it requires to have itsjown arms factories, and
to build its two transcontinental railways, so that
it can shift its army—when it gets one—to the
point of danger, remote that may be. If
these things were done there would be a gun
behind the White Australia policy. At present
there is nothing behind it, and it commands no
respect accordingly.
* * * *
The “Mother Country” is a depressed old
parent these times. It knows that its army is
a mockery and a wreck, and it doesn’t know
how to make it better. It has a vague idea that
its navy, large as it is, is not too large to protect
the British isles themselves. Under its Freetrade
system and its nefarious land system it feels the
stress of foreign competition very keenly. It has
made one Asiatic alliance in its own defence, and
is probably fishing for another. It has no sym-
pathy worth mentioning with Australia’s desire to
preserve the White Race which has made the
empire; the land that the race sprang from
seems to be the one which values it least. It is
quite willing that the Englishman should be
ousted by Chinese in South Africa, by Chinese
and Japanese in Australia, &nd by Japanese in
Canada ; till the Anglo-Saxon supremacy shifts
definitely to the United States and the expansion
of the white Briton in his own empire becomes
impossible for want of space. It values India much
more than Australia because it directly governs
India and it doesn’t directly govern Australia.
It probably values the Japanese alliance more
than it values Australia because it thinks that
Japan will fight for it, and if Japan does fight for
it, that means a strong fleet and a million brown
soldiers. On the other hand, it feels sure that
Australia Would fight for it—with a handful of
soldiers and the superannuated gunboat Protector.
This country can’t hope for any cordial sympathy
with its White Australia policy merely because
that policy means the expansion and elevation
and strengthening of the British race ; the British
race counts for less in England now than the
dividends of the German Hebrews whose beaks
make a cool shade over Jo’burg. Australia must
needs win respect for its policy itself, and to
do that it wants people—2o,ooo,ooo of people—
and factories and ships, and close settlement,
and wealth of its own—and its own gun.
If the alliance of White Australia were worth
as much for defensive purposes as that of Japan,
Britain would have a respect for White Australia,
and would see new merits in its policy. If our
anti-Chinese laws were backed by a force which
could do any necessary argument with China
single-handed, these laws would wear a new aspect
of reasonableness in the opinion of the ruling
classes in London. To Britain just now any
policy is reasonable if it is backed by a big
friendly gun; John Bull even hangs a garland
of admiration and a halo of respect round the
horrible immorality of Japan for the sake of the
Jap’s fighting power. If Australia had a service-
able fleet and army, Britain would be ready and
willing to fight for Australia, and probably even
for the White Australia policy. It is one thing
to fight for a country which can do at least half
the work of suppressing the joint enemy, and
quite another to fight for one which can do little
or nothing, except, perhaps, supply the cause
of the quarrel. The White Australia policy,
if it had its own gun, would be as good an
excuse as any other on which to extinguish a
dangerous rival, even if Britain had no real
sympathy with the aspirations that lie behind
that policy. But men, money, and fighting
capacity are the only things that count. Australia
has sacrificed its industries, and consequently its
chance of raising a great industrial population,
because it wouldn’t be so beastly ungrateful as to
raise a High Tariff barrier against Britain, and, in
proportion to its population, it has been the best
customer Britain has on earth. It has also sacri-
ficed its chance of raising a great agricultural
population rather than compulsorily break up its
big estates —many of them English-owned. And,
in return, it is scoffed at in England for not having
a population. It pays a subsidy to the British
navy, as Britain asked it to do, instead of raising
a navy of its own, and it is only jeered at for its
defencelessness, and supplied in a grudging way
with a dead-beat squadron which is regarded as
good enough for such a place—a place that is
s icrificing its future rather than seriously offend
Britain’s commercial susceptibilities.
* * * *
The White Australia policy is just about the
greatest political idtal in the world’s history,
it aims at raising the only pure white nation to be
found out of Europe. It proposes to show the
world, for the first time since the days of the
primeval ape, a whole continent under one flag,
one people, and one Government. It grasps at
the last chance on earth to build up a new white
people ; there is no other place left where such a
people can be built up. It makes Australia the
vanguard of the white race and its most advanced
entrenchment the one that absolutely looks
into Shem’s greatest encampment, and which is
the furthest of all from possibility of white
assistance. And this policy, with all its vast
aspirations, and with its huge possibilities of
glory and of danger, is being worked out quite
casually, as if it were a small thing that might
• happen any day. The political party which is
mainly responsible for it refuses even to see that
it is necessary to raise industries and accumulate
population with feverish haste, and to heap
up guns in the white man’s most advanced
entrenchment, and to have something more than
a smattering of people, and an antiquated rifle or
two, and a few hired tin-kettle cruisers behind
the biggest enterprise in history. There is
nothing behind that enterprise save a few paper
statutes ; there isn’t even the cordial support of
our British suzerain. So any day it may happen
that Britain will job away our White Australia
ideal for the sake of buying the support of an
Asiatic power. Britain may not be strong enough
to do without a Japanese alliance, but it is quite
strong enough, as things stand, to wipe out the
White Australia ideal as the price of a Japanese
alliance ; and unless we build up a great manu-
facturing and agricultural population, and put
our finances in order, and arm our people, and
build our own navy, and get our own gun, our
alliance may not seem to commercial England of
such value as to make it worth while aggravating
an important nigger on our account. England,
these days, is horribly and pathetically commer-
cial. It speaks gently—very gently, in fact it
hardly speaks at all—to the men who flog China-
men almost to death in South Africa, but then they
represent the money power of Europe. It speaks
severely to Australia over a petty Chinese factory
regulation; but then Australia doesn’t represent
anything in particular to commercial England just
now—not even its own gun.
SOUTH AUSTRALIA’S PROBLEM.
This is the sort of desert that most of S. Aus-
tralia is. The State’s total area, exclusive of the
Northern Territory, is 243,244,800 acres ; and, of
that, 141,014,152 acres, or much more than half, is
absolutely unoccupied ; it is under no form of
purchase, lease, or reserve whatever. About one-
third of the whole State is under pastoral lease ;
and how much even that third is worth can be
sized up from the fact that the average rental is a
penny a year for a little more than 13 acres. In
other words, one can rent a square mile of it for
four years for less than £l. This includes the
best with the worst; so it looks as though one can
rent a mile of the worst of it for 20 years for as
many shillings. And still 141,014,152 acres, or
more than one-half of the whole State, isn’t
reckoned worth looking at on even those terms.
Moreover, S. Australia has spent over a million
(£1,066,500, to be exact) either in making improve-
ments on alleged pastoral country hi the hope of
getting somebody to drive his jumbucks upon it,
and to occasionally send along a twopenny stamp
by way of rent, or in buying up the jmprovements
of people who have gone “on tlie land” and
then been starved off it. It'couldn’t get
people to make dams and tanks unless it under-
took to buy the alleged improvements in case the
owners were starved off; and now, besides having
sunk a few hundred thousands of Cohen’s money
in dams that are mostly silted up by dust, or fences
that are only useful to divide the east from the
west, a considerable annual sum goes out to sup-
port caretakers, who see that the fence does its
work properly. Anybody could have the use of
these “improvements” as well as the land, if he
wanted them and looked like making use of them;
but even that inducement isn’t enough. Only one
acre in every 33 in the State has been sold out-
right, though another one in every 4S is held with
the right of purchase ; therefore the freeholds are
comparatively small patches of 'coastal lands.
There isn’t even a fringe of freehold. And yet so
bare of good public lands is the State that the
average price received by the Treasury during
the past few years has tumbled in this way :—■
Acres. Average Price.
1901 .. .. 11,920 £lB 3
1902 .. .. 19,832 105
1903 .. .. 27,716 0 15 4
1904 .. .. 27,452 0 16 0
1905 .. .. 53,817 0 12 6
There has been a bit of extra demand for land, or
there has been a bit of -extra demand for “re-
venue,” and to supply even 53,817 acres S.
Australia has had to throw in desert which brings
down the average paid for the freehold to 12s. 6d.
per acre.
* * * *
Nevertheless, where land is fertile in S. Aus-
tralia, some of it is amazingly fertile. The little
scrap of volcanic soil in the south-east is equal to
almost anything in Australia. On the Adelaide
plains, again, and on parts of the hill-slopes, there
are scraps of land of great productiveness. But
practically the whole of these were grabbed in the
first days of the State ; and some of them are used
as sheep-walks still. BucKland Park, owned, like
many of the best areas, by an absentee, is within
20 miles of Adelaide. The chances are that it
would have gone on being a sheep-run, just as it
has been for the past 60 years, if Premier Price
hadn’t come along with his more stiffly graduated
land-tax. Every State in the Commonwealth
needs such a tax, but probably no other State needs
it so badly as S. Australia—certainly none has
greater need of it. The fact that the State can only
now supply twelve-and-sixpenny land goes a long
way to prove that; and there is also proof of the
same thing in the fact that in the past 10 years
the State has spent £470,000 in the repurchase of
land, and yet hasn’t been able to check the out-
ward flow of population. It can’t develop the
“great natural resources” by running out cock-
spur railway lines ; it knows that it hasn’t any
remote agricultural resources to develop. The
fact that even the squatter won’t look at most of
the country, and that what he does look at he gets
for less than ss. per square mile per annum, proves
that. S. Australia’s one hope, therefore, is to
make the very best use of ’such fertile scraps as it
has; and it has been proved that, left to thern-
selvs, the early grabbers won’t make the best use
of it. Therefore a stiff graduated land-tax becomes
absolutely indispensable to force them to do
what they have proved that they won’t do other-
wise. Even then there seems to be no great future
before the State in the matter of “the great
natural producing industries.” It is too poor a
country, anyhow, to stick to those industries, and
let others do for it all the other services it needs.
If it had vast areas of immensely rich soil it might
be a fool to trouble about making its own boots or
clothes or machinery ; but it clearly isn’t that sort
of country. To make any progress it must
hang on to every bit of work it has to do ; in
other words, it can’t afford to believe in anything
but High Protection,
.* * * *
There is a good deal of “local patriotism ” in
S. Australia ; but, unfortunately, it is the sort of
“patriotism” that shows itself in a sweep of the arm
and a vague remark concerning “ our vast inherit-
ance in the Hills,” and another sweep of the
arm and a further vague remark concerning
“ the vast mineral resources of the interior.” Yet
“ the Hills ” are yielding less now than they did
30 years ago, and “the mineral resources of the
interior ” are mostly represented by a long pro-
cession of wild-cats and small tucker-fields. S.
Australia had better get down, then, to hard facts,
For the past 20 years it has this ghastly record :
In every year but two—lB92 and 1893—it lost
people by excess of emigration over immigration ;
and the gain in even those two years was due to
the mineral developments in another State. It
has been living on expectations concerning “our
inheritance in the Hills,” and waiting for some-
thing to turn up; and nothing has turned up.
But there are some things that it knows it has ;
and it is up to it now to turn-to and make the
best use of them—by cordially accepting Price’s
graduated land-tax, with regrets that it doesn’t
go further; and by ruling out every Foreign-trader,
and thus stopping the insane policy of compelling
the Australian to become a foreigner in order to
supply Australia with the things it needs. A loss
of population in 18 years out of 20 surely ought to
be enough to make any country think hard and
act sharply.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Postage Stamp of Secesh.
The N.S.W. Secession party in the State Assem-
bly, last week unfurled a tuppenny stamp as its
banner, and cried out loudly for “some action,”
because the Commonwealth had, it was said,
decided to establish its stamp-printing business at
the Adelaide printing office, which it owns.
There are two reasons for this : The Adelaide
printing office is the only one which the Australian
Government has got; and the records show that
the Adelaide office has always produced its stamps
at a lower price than any other office. But though
the Secession party, which is mostly the old
Foreign-trade party, is always willing to approve
of the “economy” effected by making engines,
boots, cloths, &c., for Australia in England, or
Germany, or Japan, it bitterly resents secuiing
economy by centralising at Adelaide the Coramon-
inonwealth’s tiny little stamp-printing business.
It is filled with fury at the idea of taking away
from Sydney an industry which employs four men
in order to establish it in another Australian city;
and over this trifle it raged, as it rages when it is
proposed to fulfil the implied purposes of Federa-
tion and do away with the State Governors,
second Chambers, Agents-Gener&l, and other
extravagant superfluities. These extracts from
Hansard report of the debate should be read
carefully, with frequent mental note Or the fact
that the matter at issue was that of milk nig an
obviously desirable economy by centralising the
stamp-printing businesses of the Commonwealth :
Mr. VV. Anderson (Balmain): The unconstitutional
aspect of it, in my judgment, shows itself in the fact that
before the Federal capital site has been settled by the
Federal Parliament, we have the introduction of a new
printing establishment in one of the lesser States, thus
centralising another very large part of the busi-
ness of the State in that centre... It is felt
that instead of the Commonwealth being a benefit
to Australia, it is quite the reverse.

when we shall have to cease discussing these injustices
which are being perpetrated on N.S.W. The time is arri\-

Government know that we have rights, and that w e
intend to assert those rights, and not to submit to a long
series of continuous and persistent injustices which were

compact.

State to take some de.inite action, and I feel assured that

than which I know of no more important for many a long

proposed to take everything from us, and the sooner we

protest that is proposed to be made, and I think the time

enter our protest; we must not forget that these protests
—protest after protest—are only leading up to the great

And all these speakers were grown men! Not
children deprived of their lolly-sticks for the good
of their little stomachs ! On the other side there
was only one voice lifted, that of a man with
whom Tiik Bulletin is seldom in agreement of

the sense to satirise the childish complainings of

incident. It should serve to show to the Aus-

people have federated, the Australian politicians
have not. A certain proportion of Bumbles will
always get to the State Parliaments by promising
bridges, subscribing to cricket clubs, and helping
along land-agency transactions; and these men
will never be anything but anti-I ederal, never
anything but hostile to the power which saps their
petty dignity. And the buzzing of these Bum-
bles, outraged in their dearest feelings as they
seethe inevitable dwindling of their importance,
will always be loud. It will rage and shriek
“ unconstitutional ” on any pretext or on no pre-
text at all. It is as ready to chew soap and froth
at the mouth over a twopenny stamp as over a
Federal capital. That being so, it is absurd to
give any consideration to its foam. Since it will
talk secession for any reason, or without any
reason, it should be left to mumble and chatter in
its corner. In the past the mistake has been made
of listening seriously to the murmurs of the State
Frights crowd. The stamp debate should make
that mistake impossible for the future. As usual,
by the way, N.S.W. Assembly is the one great
blubbering infant of the Commonwealth. Other
States also lose their stamp-printing jobs through
the transfer of the whole business to one office,
but they don’t regard the matter as anything out
of the ordinary run of events. It is fortunate that
there is only one such State Parliament as that of
N.S.-W. in the Commonwealth. If the weeping
infant were twins ! ! !
A Quack Prevention Law.
The Bent Government has circulated a Bill which
proposes to make it very warm indeed for the
quack doctors who loaf on the Victorian com-
munity. The Victorian medical law, as it now
stands, forbids anyone not on the official
register to call himself a doctor, and also pro-
hibits such persons charging fees, or at any
rate recovering them at law. But both these
provisions have been evaded by the quacks.
They call themselves “professors,” “.herbalists,”
“American healers,” “medical electricians,” and
so forth ; and, instead of asking for a consul-
tation fee, they charge outrageous prices for their
mixtures, and announce that they give “advice
gratis.” The new Act contains a clause pro-
hibiting anyone but a doctor or a chemist
dispensing medicine, or rendering medical or
surgical aid for a fee; and any trans-
gression carries a fine of up to £5O,
so unless the “ Timbuctoo healer” gives his
advice and medicine free, and “gets there”
by charging 18s. fid. for a pot plant, his day
will be over. Victoria, just now, is a shark-
haunted community. There are “ Chinese
doctors,” “Hindu eye-doctors,” “herbalists” of
various nationalities, “Indian healers,” Egyptian
dodgers, and others. If the Bill flattens out only
half these impostors it will do a lot of good. It
might be worth the while of the Assembly to
consider the advisability of applying the White
Australia principle to the measure, by means of a
ciausi.f.. Aking it a penal offence, or something
near it", y >.j’ v Asiatic to do anything at all in a
medical wayv 'This would do away, at one fell
sweep, with the whole tribe of Fatty Shams and
Stink Hings that at present get an easy living
as Indian doctors and Chinese herbalists, and so
on. Also, it might be well, in view of the
possible pot-plant dodge herein hinted at,
to add a clausa providing that, when a
person professes to pursue the healing art, any-
thing he may sell to a person whom he has sup-
plied with medical advice—even a geranium or a
bookcase—shall be regarded as medicine, and
must not be charged for. Victoria might come in
for some British scorn, as the only country on
earth which regarded a wheelbarrow or an old
book as medicine, but that need not deter it from
taking reasonable precautions.
Backing Down to the Jap.
Prime Minister Deakin’s proposed amendments
of the Immigration Act read in places like the
compromise of a man who has made compromise
a fine art and the search for the line of least
resistance a science. In some respects they are
unobjectionable; in others, they look like the
beginning of a dangerous course of apology. One
of the first results will be that domestic servants
and personal attendants who are accompanying
their employers will be exempt from the anti-
contract labor laws —a proviso which can’t do any
visible harm, and which will prevent Horace
Tozer making a fool of himself again about his
wife’s maid. Other contract labor is allowed to
be imported if the contract seems to the Minister
to be a fair one according to Australian wage
rates and the Australian cost of living, if the labor
isn’t imported with a view to affecting an
industrial dispute, and if there is a difficulty
in obtaining within the Commonwealth a suffi-
ciency of equally skilled workers. Though
George Reid objects from force of habit there is
nothing objectionable about any of these items.
But Deakin’s proposal to vary the existing law as
regards colored aliens is a different affair. He
proposes to provide that the Education Test for
immigrants, instead of being in some European
language, may be in any prescribed language—
Chinese, Japanese, or any other Asiatic variety—
and this makes the present large and objection-
able hole still larger. Under the present law the
application of the test to Asiatics is, unfortu-
nately, not compulsory, and if ever the very worst
kind of Ministry happens in Australia, that
Ministry may let in Asiatics wholesale by declin-
ing to apply the test at all. Under the new law
it may admit Asiatics wholesale while professing
a busy and anxious desire to enforce the law—by
the simple process of testing them in their own
language. Therefore, there are to be two orifices
instead of one. Japan has already demanded
that its language shall be one of those which may
be used in a test. It will probably next demand
that as regards its own brown subjects its own
language shall be used as a test every time, and if
it carries that point also, the barrier against
Jap immigration will be about as completely gone
as if it had never existed. Then Deakin’s foolish
Bill provides that arrangements may be made to
exempt the people of any country from any and
every kind of test, and the first voice heard at
this country’s keyhole demanding to be exempt
will almost certainly lie that of Japan. And it
must be remembered that, though a contract labor
clause is very good for keeping out desirable white
workers and some of the more remote colored
races, it is very little good in the way of keeping
out the Jap and the Chinaman, therefore it
isn’t safe to relax the clauses which do serve
to keep them out. The skilled white artisan,
who is worth high wages, often wants
an agreement as a guarantee that work in his
line will be provided at adequate rates; the
yellow man, who is willing to undersell everybody,
and to economise by living eight in a room, needs
no agreement. That kind of man can always get
a job at somebody’s expense. The barrier was too
weak before, and Deakin, in his lamentable desire
to be affable, makes it weaker. It had holes in it
before ; the new device makes it liable to become
all hole. Instead of trying to placate the Jap,
who won’t be placated by anything short of the
free run of the country, Australia should hasten to
arm itself and to fill the country up with strong
white men so as to leave no room for the Jap. Of
course it can’t do that until it supplies work for
men (and the tariff is in the way), also till it
finds land for them (and the big landgrabber is
in the way). But if Alfred Deakin were a real
whole man, he would put his head down and
butt straight at those two obstacles as the
most honest w'ay out of the Jap trouble.
He could only lose his billet at the worst, and
better lose it honorably than keep it dishonorably,
But he couldn’t lose unless the Labor party failed
to support him, and it is his duty to dump his
High Tariff Bill, his Federal Graduated Land-
Tax Bill and his White Immigration Bill on the
table of the House, and tell the Labor party that
if it doesn’t help him with all its strength to
carry them, the blood of Australia will be on its
head, and that, like Pilate, he will wash his
hands of all responsibility. Britain is un-
doubtedly dissatisfied with the present position
of affairs, but there are two ways to make things
square with Britain. One is to back down to its
bumptious little brown friend. The other is to
build up such a nation in Australia and such an
army and navy that, when they are thrown into
the scale in any just quarrel, Britain can do with-
out its brown friend ; and, having done that, to
ask Britain which friend it will have.
The Lobbying Importer Again.
The precious importer, the agent of foreign traders
to whom Australia is merely a dumping ground,
has been defendant in an interesting matter known
as the “ Balata ” belting case. The matter, which
had the attention of the Customs authorities
since ’O3, amounted to a charge that a brassy
attempt was made to “ rook ” the Customs by the
Melbourne importers on the false pretence that
their principals—a Scottish firm —had allowed them
an extra discount, and this alleged extra dis-
count, being deducted from the invoice, naturally
reduced the duty. At first the Commonwealth
Parliament put this belting on the free list, but
on March 6th, 1902, it was made dutiable at 20
per cent. In August, 1902, the duty was lowered
to 15 per cent, as a result of the difference of
opinion between the Senate and Representatives.
The prosecution put in some really choice
letters from the Australian importers to
their Scottish principals showing the why
and wherefore of this “difference of opinion.”
On April 2nd these lovely lobbyists wrote : “We
are again this week personally working the Senators
in regard to this belting duty ... we approach
you for a further concession in the way of dis-
count...” On April 23rd a third epistle
informed the canny Scots that “we
are doing our best to induce the Senators
to put on a merely revenue duty instead of 20 per
cent.” On July 25th, further correspondence
notified that “ ... we have been working very hard
interviewing both members of the Representatives
and Senators, and in the latter, where there is a
slight majority of Freetraders, we were able to
get them to advise a reduction of the duty to 10
per cent.” All this hard toil, though it injured
Australian industry by getting the duty lowered
from 20 to 15 per cent., apparently failed to soften
the hearts of the canny Scots, and they declined
to allow any further discount, so these “com-
mercial men” are said to have got what they
could out of the Customs by representing the dis-
count as 40, instead of the actual 20 per cent. This
was the case as put by the Customs authorities.
But the part of the tale that is most interesting to
Australians is that about the Senators who were
“ interviewed ” and “ worked ” to induce them to
damage an Australian industry for the benefit of
those remarkable importers. How do you “work”
a Senator when you want him to wreck an Aus-
tralian industry for the benefit of a rich importer ?
Will someone get a Royal Commission appointed
to find out ?
A Sample of British Snuffle.
The British Government, in raising objections to
the anti-Asiatic clauses of the Westralian Fac-
tories Act, appears as a shocking hypocrite as well
as a muddle-headed meddler. Those clauses limit
the number of hours during which an Asiatic
may work in factories; and especially prohibit
Asiatics from carrying on business as owners of
factories, or from being employed in factories,
unless in business or so employed prior to Novem-
ber, 1903. The Act is remarkable in the sense that
it cuts direct at the root of an acknowledged evil
in plain terms and with effectual provisions. The
British objection is apparently based on the appli-
cation of special restrictions to Asiatic races
which do not apply to European races. It
is instructive, therefore, to turn for precedents to
the Transvaal Labor Ordinance (1904) that master-
piece of Conservative legislation under which the
Witwatersrand is being flooded with Chinese
slaves for the benefit of Bull-Ooiien, and of which
the present British Government is particularly
proud. In that Act “ laborer ”is defined as
a male person Belonging to a non-European race other
than one of the races indigenous to Africa south of 12
degrees north of the Equator, introduced to this colony
under contract of service.
A far more sweeping racial differentiation than
any in the W.A. Act, as it bundles together in
one careless lot Japs, Chinese, British Indians,
Pacific Island cannibals and most African savages
as “non-European races”! For special restric-
tions, that do not apply to the European, the
Transvaal Act provides that—
It shall not be lawful for any laborer (as defined above)
to enter, be, or reside, or to be introduced into this
colony unless he shall previously have entered into the
contract referred to in section eight.
Unless, in fact, he has first become a chattel,
known by a registered number, liable to be
passed from hand to hand by transfer in writing
at the pleasure of his owner, forbidden to leave
his stockade without a pass, forbidden to hold,
purchase, lease, or own property, to do skilled
labor, or to carry on any trade or business. While
such drastic provisions are enforced in the Trans-
vaal against all “laborers” of non-European
origin (Japanese, Indians, Chinese, etc.) with
the high approval of the British Govern-
ment, it seems a most malevolent variety of
hypocrisy and mere premeditated snuffle and
insult to take objection to the very moderate
restrictions to Asiatic employment provided by
the Westralian Act. To put British Chadband-
ism to a test, how would it be for Aus-
tralia to copy Britain’s own law as applied to
the Transvaal, and pass an Act forbidding the
entry into Australia of all Asiatic races except
under contract with some company authorised by
Act of Parliament to import (and flog) Asiatics ?
Then, by refusing to grant any company the right
of-contract (and flogging), Britain’s own law as
applied to the Transvaal would be a complete
prohibition of the Asiatic influx—a far better one
than Australia has ever yet had. The spectacle
of Britain violently putting a veto on its own law
as insulting to its Jap ally would be interesting.
Only a Million a Year.
Australia gets a wonderful amount of advice
from benevolent persons, who mean well, but who,
apparently, don’t know. A recent sample is Mr.
T. A. Dibbs, manager of the Commercial Banking
Co. of S dney, who has just come back from Eng-
land, and finds that N.S. W. isn’t popular there-
in fact it is so unpopular that it would be difficult
to raise a loan in London just now on anything
like “fair terms.” And Mr. Dibbs’ remedy for
this sad condition of things is that N.S.W. should,
for the time at least, “limit its borrowings to
£1,000,000 a year, and find the rest of the money
out of revenue.” He reckons that if this were
done N.S.W. would, in a few years, “reinstate
itself in the financial world.” Some people might
rather imagine that in a few years it would go to
perdition, and would deserve its destination. Mr.
Dibbs’ proposal is that N.S.W. should, in a time
of peace, and with no defence expenditure worth
mentioning, borrow a sum which would amount to
the same thing in proportion to population, as if the
United Kingdom borrowed about £30,000,000 a
year regularly. He proposes that N.S.W. should
add to its debt every' two years as much per
inhabitant as the whole debt of Switzer-
land. The State has, in proportion to its popula-
tion, a magnificent land revenue such as no other
country on earth ever enjoyed, half of it being
acquired by the sale of the public assets ; but as
the sale of assets can’t go on for ever, N.S.W. will
have to learn some day to do without a lot of this
revenue. Mr. Dibbs doesn’t propose that it
should learn to do so now, before the interest bill
grows larger, by the simple process of putting the
land revenue into a public works account and
swearing off the loan-and-cadge habit. His
idea seems to be that the State should
go on adding £35,000 each year to its in-
terest bill, so that when the land-sale re-
venue at last dies out there will be perhaps
£700,000 a year more interest to pay, and
about £1,000,000 a year less revenue to pay it with,
while the borrowing habit will have become even
more confirmed. There is no mention in Mr.
Dibbs’ philosophy of any possible future day when
borrowing will cease, nor of any period when re-
payment will start. In fact, N.S.W., having got
into trouble by borrowing, the great and brilliant
idea of Bank Manager Dibbs is that it should go
on borrowing till it gets out of trouble. In a
general way Manager Dibbs appears to regard
borrowing as our natural condition—like eating,
or being born, or receiving Christian burial.
There is no country on earth in a better position,
not only to leave off borrowing, but also to com-
mence a systematic reduction of its public debt,
than N.S.W., for its land revenue gives it a chance
such as no other country ever possessed and such
as N.S.W. itself is never likely to possess again.
But the community is bogged by the well-meant
advice of so many elderly gentlemen, who think
that we are unlike any other people on earth—
that we are the Chosen Borrowers, and that we
were never meant to endeavor, like other races, to
be perpendicular and self-reliant, and owe no Jew
anything.
Things that Need Explanation.
A little group of Australian and ex-Australian
financial men has been putting some tens of
thousands of Australian money into Japanese
concerns. After a successful coup in tramways,
these people formed the British and Japanese
Finance Corporation, in 120,000 £5 shares and
20,000 £1 deferred shares. Malcolm McEacharn
put in £30,000 ; his partner, Mcllwraith, £20,000 ;
Mcllwraith, McEacharn & Co., £10,000; and
James Burns, of Burns, Philp & Co., £12,000.
And the S. M. Herald, noting that Australians
are largely interested in this new general trading
and development concern, remarks—
How it is that Japan should he regarded as a desirable
country for the investment of Australian capital, while
Australia still remains only partially developed, requires
a good deal of explanation.
The Herald leaves it at that ; it doesn’t
give or attempt to give the great deal of
explanation that it says the case calls for.
It doesn’t point out that, in conformity with the
Herald s own foolish policy, Australia is a low-
tariff country, and that a man would, generally
speaking, be a fool to put his money into Aus-
tralian industrial business, seeing that the busi-
ness would be almost sure to be kiiocke i out bv
the products of cheaper labor countries. It doesn’t
point out that a man would be a fool to put his
money into factories in Australia and pay high
wages, when he can supply the same market by
putting up factories in Japan and getting the
work done at low wages. It doesn’t point out to
the Labor party the utter absurdity, in the
light of these things, of thinking that high
wages and low tariff can run together.
It doesn’t point out that Japan, even
though it is a cheap - labor country, protects
itself against any possible cheaper labor country
by a Customs tariff running up to 150 per cent.
ad valorem. It doesn’t point out that, as the
result of dropping the S.M. Herald’s own policy
overboard with great emphasis, Japan has in-
creased its cotton manufactures 40-fold in 15
years, and other industries almost as much.
Likewise che S.M. Herald doesn’t explain that
Japan imposes one of the heaviest land-taxes in
the world, and derives close upon £7,000,000 of
revenue from this source alone ; that it has prac-
tically wiped out the big landlord ; and that over
three-fifths of the whole of the arable land in the
country is cultivated by peasant proprietors.
Also, it doesn’t point out that Japan hasn’t
been afraid of the bogey-cry of Socialism.
That country wanted iron and steel works,
just as Australia does, and knew that
it couldn’t be great without them ; and
though it had no good available iron ore to speak
of, and had to import most of the raw material
from China, and though its coal is not nearly as
good as that of N.S.W., it established iron and
steel works. Further, it set to work to pay its
way. It never got into the deficit habit. It
didn’t assume that the only way to do anything
was to borrow money to do it with. It wanted to
own things itself instead of paying a foreigner for
owning them ; and to do that it made a State
monopoly of the tobacco business, among other
things. It wasn’t perpetually talking about any
Mother Country ; it didn’t feel called upon to be
foolish because any other country was making an
ass of itself, and allowing its trade to slip
out of its hands. It thought about itself
for itself; that was its patriotism. It
therefore never worshipped foreign capital
it knew that foreign capital was foreign
debt, and it wanted to work for itself and not for
any foreigner. And so it grew, and became, in a
measure, prosperous—marvellously prosperous,
anyhow, compared with what it had been, and,
considering the materials, human and otherwise,
that it had to work with. And it accomplished
all this by doing almost everything that the S.M.
Herald says a country shouldn’t do—by high
Protection, by a stiff land-tax, by abolishing the
great landlord, by State Socialism, by paying for
public works out of taxation (land-tax, income-
tax, business-tax, Customs duties, &c.), and by
treating the man who talks reverently about “ the
influx of foreign capital ” with the disrespect he
deserves. Now, as usual, the outsider is seizing a
chance to jump in and share in the advantages
that these things have brought the country (though
in a year or two Japan will probably take him by
the neck and throw him out again, as it usually
does); and the Herald says that the circumstance
“ requires a good deal of explanation.”
