The Stinking Fish Campaign.
The old Conservative party in Australia —the one
which lost its hold so badly a few years ago—has
now spent two or three years in a struggle to
recover its old position by outside influences, and
has met with very little success. In Australia
the party was too well known for its utterances to
carry any weight, except within its own narrow
circle. The community knows its members, and
doesn’t know them favorably. It has seen their
signatures on lying balance-sheets and swindling
prospectuses away back in the boom time. _ It
remembers their heartless robberies of the savings
of the poor. It knows their great estates —also
the dummying, forgery and fraud by which some
of these estates were got together. It has just
been inspecting their agency doings, and has
seen the amazing wealth of lies that they would
descend to for a very small consideration. It
has just heard it explained with engaging
frankness how land-owners and commercial
men profess to have combined together to
bribe a Department to dissipate the public
estate for their benefit. Australia is acquainted
with its landed Tories, and its rich com-
mercial Tories, and the handful of fashionable
parsons who fetch and carry for them in return
for contributions to the tabernacle, which is orna-
mented neither by the presence of the preacher
who is sold nor by that of the people who bought
him. Being thus so badly discredited in his own
country, the Australian Tory of the worst type set
up a propaganda outside. He sought, by a tre-
mendous process of organised lying throughout
the world, to excite, in every English-speaking
country, a cry of horror at the imaginary doings
of the democratic party in Australia,, and he suc-
ceeded pretty well. Wherever an Australian Tory
of the bad old type went, the world was informed of
bogus horrors from this side—the total prohibition
of immigrants, the throwing overboard and drown-
ing of colored sailors shipwrecked on the coast,
the imprisonment of British tradesmen for coming
here to earn an honest living, the vanishing of
population, the disappearance of capital, the
general closing up of everything. Things which
Maoriland, Canada, the United States, and Eng-
land do, and have done for years, without any-
body regarding them as in the least degree
unusual, excite consternation when Australia even
proposes to do them in a modified form ; and
these things, combined with the things which
Australia never did, or even proposed to do, have
made this country, for the time, a bye-word.
It was expected that the democratic party would
be staggered by the storm of obloquy
thu3 created—that in the face of the wide
execration which was supposed to have been
roused by its policy, but which was really
roused by the lies told about its policy, so
many people would fall away from the party that
it would lose its influence, and the old Tory gang
of land-dummies and bank-robbers and commercial
thieves would come back to power on a wave of
popular feeling. It didn’t matter to these Tories
if they made Australia, by their lies and slanders,
a thing of loathing all the world over, provided the
outside pressure thus created brought about a re-
vulsion that would restore them to political office,
and enable them to fill the country with nigger
labor, and steal more land, and avert the
threatened breaking up of their great estates to
make room for settlement. It is pleasing to know
that in this respect they have failed. They have
taught the people to loathe the very names of the
traitors who are willing to blacken the good name of
their own country for the sake of getting back to the
jobs and the salaries and the perquisites of office,
and making more secure the profits of old-time
steals. They have made many good Australians hate
the ve-y name of England by reason of the slanders
which the English press, partly at the instigation
of Austv: lian Tories, has heaped on this country.
They have made many more Australians hate the
church and its mmistei s because so many
of them have lent themsehes to the anti-

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Boomerang and Karrakatta, late cruisers on the
Australian station, have been sold in England for about
£lBOO each. —Statement in the Australian Parliament.
Let the council stop the traffic ;
Make the concertina wail;
For we want to do a Maffick
On a transcendental scale ;
That our grat'tude may show in
Such a fashion as is meet
To our good old Uncle Cohen
With his pretty cardboard fleet.
For he sends it o’er the water
With a tearful tenderness,
And he only asks a quarter
Of a million, more or less.
While our gain, if we behave, is,
As his praise we loudly bleat,
That he’ll let us play at navies
With his pretty cardboard fleet.
Though the armor plates be dented
Where the cook has thrown the crust,
We should always be contented
If the boilers haven’t bust.
Though no gun is safe to blow in
And the engine’s obsolete,
We must grateful be to Cohen
For his little cardboard fleet.
They have plenty flags and pennants,
Polished brass work, bright as gold ;
They have handsome young lieutenants
Mashing gii'ls with glances bold ;
AH the boats were reckoned charming,
As a rabbit clean and neat,
And quite equally alarming
Is Australia’s cardboard fleet.
So let money freely flow in
As it flowed in heretofore,
To the safe of Uncle Cohen,
Who protects Australia’s shore.
And the only boon we crave is,
When the yearly bill we meet,
That he’ll let us play at navies
With his little cardboard fleet.

slanderers. are really no nearer to power. The
dream of a return to the ways of the period before
the boom burst and before the present democratic
party appeared in Parliament—when among the
front-rank men of politics could be counted
lialf-a-score whose bankruptcies, bogus com-
pany swindles, and miscellaneous commer-

when, though occasional public frauds do come to
light, they are mostly the work of a few Tories
who have been left over from the wreck. They
don’t know that they have become impossible,
but that is merely through their inability to
realise what they were and what kind of a smellful
era they belonged to.

The S.M. Herald’s Implied Falsehood.
The Sydney Morning Herald, for a little while
of late, threatened to become a decent, fair-minded
and reasonably truthful paper, and showed some
unexpected gleams of patriotic Australian spirit.
Apparently it has got over that, and become the
same narrow-minded, hypocritical, parochial rag
as of old. Witness this extract from its leader
(10/11/’O5) on “The Federal Capital ” :
This matter of the Federal capital is one upon which
New South Wales may well found its case against the
Commonwealth. Whatever may be said now, it is un-
deniably true that when the decisive vote teas cast in favor
of Federation it teas in the belief that the capital ivould
as soon as possible be established in New South Wales, and
that as nearly as possible to the hundred miles’ radius
from Sydney. Everybody knows that ; but now the rep-
resentatives of other States in the Federal Parliament
profess to believe that they can play fast and loose with the
clausein the Constitution Act. These representatives, aided
and abetted, we regret to say, by members returned by
New South Wales and by the iate SEE Ministry, claim that
they can select any site they choose. They have chosen
a site which, whilst very convenient to Victoria, is alto-
gether unacceptable to New South Wales. They have
chosen a site which is without railway communication,
which is distant hundreds of miles from Sydney, whose
soil is as unproductive as its position is inaccessible. It
is as if a private person consented to give to another a
camping-place on his land, and the recipient of this offer
assumed the right of establishing himself wherever he
pleased. New South Wales is bound to give the Common-
wealth a site for the Federal capital, but it is not bound
to accept the dictation of the Federal Parliament as to
the exact whereabouts of this site. In the circumstances,
the people of New South Wales will agree with Mr.
Carruthers when he asserts that faith has not been kept
with New South Wales in the matter, and they will back
him in every legitimate effort he may use to secure the
fulfilment of the compact.
According to the first italicised sentence, N.S.
Wales wouldn’t have joined the Common-
wealth unless the other States gave way and
agreed that the Parliament should camp on
its ground. (The statement is false, because
N.S. W. gave a majority for Federation when
there was nothing in the Constitution about the
capital being in N.S.W., but the State Parlia-
ment disgracefully broke its agreement with the
other States and annulled the acceptance. How-
ever, that is a side issue.) According to the
second italicised sentence N.S.W., as a favor,
consented to the Federal Parliament camping on
its ground, and because of this concession, made
by N.S.W., it has a right to say whereabouts the
Federal Parliament shall camp. Now, has the
Herald the assurance and the phenomenal lying
power to say that Australia in general asked
N.S.W. to be allowed to place the capital in
N.S.W. territory, and that the act was a conces-
sion made by N.S.W. to the other States? It
distinctly implies that when it speaks of “con-
senting to give to another a camping ground,” but
does it feel equal to telling the lie straight out ?

A Proposed Tramway Steal.
Premier Bent is sending through Vic. Parliamen t
a Bill to enable the Railway Department to run
motor buses and cars along suburban routes,
as feeders to the railway traffic. The
Bill has met with the hot opposition of
shareholders in the Tramway Co.. and
the lobbying done by representatives of that
temporary monopoly (it is supposed to hand over
its business to a municipal trust in 1916, but it
intends to get a renewal if possible) was a magni-
ficent spectacle. The Vie. Legislative Council
consists largely of shareholders in the monopoly,
and their friends. Consequently, when the Bill
came before the House of Shareholders, this new
clause was inserted
No motor carriage or car shall ply for hire in any street
in which a line of tramway is laid, or use such street,
except for the purpose of crossing the same, or for a dis-
tance of 20 chains in order to leave or approach a railway
station.
The large shareholders declared that the clause
was needed to save the municipalities, when they
took control of the tramways, from ruinous State
competition. The Assembly cast the new clause
out with scorn. Then the House of Rich Land-
lords went one better, and altered the clause to
make it provide simply that
No motor carriage or car of the Victorian Railway Com-
missioners, hereby authorised, shall ply for hire in any
street in which a line of tramway is laid.
That is to say, the veil was dropped, and the
amendment was framed frankly in the interests of
the dividends of the present tramway co. and
future capitalist undertakings. This clause was
flung out by the Assembly without a word of
debate. The fact that the Tramway 00. is at pre-
sent a private monopoly is due greatly to the efforts
of the Fatmen’s Council of the past. Only the
tremendous efforts of that sick old lion, Fitz-
gibbon, induced the formation of the Trust and
the 1916 compromise. The first line of motor-
buses proposed by the Railway Department—
Prahran to Malvern—was bitterly opposed by an
M.L.C. who headed a syndicate with a rival
private scheme. There seems little chance
of the Council winning this time, but it
may. Bent has a habit of falling heavily from
a democratic position, when a certain section of
his old pals in commerce oppose him. The Vic.
Assembly should make certain that Bent is prop-
ped up this time, or allowed to fall for ever.
+
Besides sinking the fiscal question, N.S.W. Labor
party also appears to have sunk its platform.
That platform declares that the party is opposed
to any further borrowing except to carry out
undertakings which will pay interest and sinking
fund. To that every member of the party is
pledged ; it is his contract with his country and
his constituents. Yet the other night, when
Solemn Lee, Carruthers’ Works Minister, pro-
posed that the new Public Library should be
built with borrowed money, only three members
of the Labor party — Griffith, Meehan, and
Miller— thought it worth while to respect
their pledge or live up to their contract.
It isn’t suggested that a public library building
will ever pay interest, let alone sinking fund ; it
is well known, in fact, that it won’t earn anything
whatever, and every farthing of the interest on
the cost will have to be made up out of revenue.
But in spite of its pledge, in spite of its platform,
in spite of everything, the shiftless State Labor
party says “ Hear, hear ” to a shiftless Premier
and a shiftless Ministry ; and, instead of raising
the money to pay the bill, passes on the document
to posterity—another lump laid on to the load of
unproductive debt with which the community is
cursed. It might easily get the money and pay
the bill, and be done with it; it prefers, instead,
to open up an endless vista of interest-paying,
with the unpaid bill still always dangling in the
distance.

There is great excitement amongst Victorian
mayors and municipal councillors just at pre-
sent, in connection with the Union Label Bill,
now before the Australian Parliament. The re-
cently “consolidated” anti-Socialistic League
started the crusade ; but its howl was such a feeble
one that the Tory party fell back on its old device
and fled for support to the alderman. And the
alderman, true to his instincts, has responded to
the call. A circular, inviting the municipalities
to denounce the accursed label, has been distri-
buted broadcast throughout Victoria. In two or
three rare instances a council has simply “re-
ceived” the document, and has taken no action,
on the wise and sensible ground that municipal
bodies are elected to attend to the streets, the
bridges, the thistles, and the vagrant dog. But
in the vast majority of instances the councils
have arisen in their might, and have passed,
as a rule unanimously, a fierce condemna-
tion of the Bill, and many of them have
added a clause to the effect “that the local
Parliamentary representative be asked to vote
against the measure.” Of course the Australian
Parliament knows all about the unimportance of
the alderman’s opinion on any legislative question.
The alderman had his vote, along with others,
on election day, and if he had any manners or
taste he would accept the position. But the
alderman is for ever trying to ape the legislator,
and half the time of Vic. council meetings is
occupied with the consideration of matters that
are utterly foreign to them. If State Premier
Bent wants to further distinguish himself, he will
pass a law limiting the municipal subsidy to those
councils which, in the preceding twelve
months, have not discussed any political
question extraneous to their functions. The sub-
sidy would then be cut up amongst about six
shires, and the subsequent stunned silence of the
others would be something memorable.
Atlek Hunt, Commonwealth Secretary for
External Affairs, comes back from New Guinea
with a report that devotes itself anxiously to the
best means of making the black brother work for
the profit of the white. In happy New Guinea,
about two days a week spent in labor keep the
black boy in the means of subsistence, and he
shows a growing disinclination to work six or
seven days a week for the benefit of his employer.
Moreover, being entitled (under an ordinance of
1888) to refuse' to sell his land if he guarantees to
work it himself, he is disinclined to part with it at
the rate of a tomahawk and a glass bottle
per acre, but prefers to linger on it in
his own lazy style. Hunt proposes that
a Lands Acquisition Ordinance should be
passed, in order to shift the selfish savage
from his ancestral home, and that there-
after the land should be given to white settlers on
terms that “practically amount to giving it for
nothing.” Then Hunt says it is impossible for the
white man to work the land himself, and he there-
fore sets out to consider how “in the interests of
the native” the labor problem is to be faced. In
the interests of the native, his term of indenture
on a plantation, says Hunt, should be increased
from 12 months to three years, at the end of which
time the pernicious habit of laziness will have gone
from him. In the interests of the native, he should
be taught the necessity of labor by being taxed.
Then he’ll have to work, or go to gaol for not pay-
ing his taxes. The tax which Hunt prefers, in the
interests of the native, will take the shape of forced
labor on plantations. Altogether, the interests of
the native premise to be kept very steadily in view.
It isn’t easy to spe how anything can be done with
the black brother without being unpleasant to him,
yet it seems a pity that so much should be said
about the interests of the native. Couldn’t the
element of pious and moral sludge be left out of
public documents for once ?
*.
One end of the Tariff Commission found its lower
jaw hanging down like an executed criminal
during the sittings in Perth. The boss of the
State’s biggest timber concern was giving evidence,
and said he had bought second-hand locomotives
from the contractors for the Coolgardie railway,
and they had been hauling ever since. The loads
were heavy and so were the grades and curves, but
these locomotives stood the wear and tear as well
as, if not better than, any English or American
engines. They were manufactured in S. Australia,
and some stationary engines turned out by the
Austral Otis Co. were equally satisfactory—quality
as good and price as low as anything imported.
Railway waggons, made of timber cut and dressed
by themselves, with locally-made ironwork, gave
better results than imported waggons ; in fact,
witness expressed his opinion that every truck
or waggon required for railway purposes should be
manufactured in the State. This from the general
manager of a big English concern fairly broke the
assembled Foreign Trade crowd into fragments.
Slowly Gropers are learning that jarrah has other
uses besides that of being sawn into railway
sleepers for foreign countries. Immense quantities
of splendid jarrah are destroyed because it will
not cut up for sleepers. A factory for working up
this waste into hubs, spokes, and felloes for drays
and waggons, flooring boards and even furniture,
ought to be a valuable business proposition.

“Merely Mary Ann,” at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), is an interesting study from several
points of view. With its help, for instance, you
can see the different sorts of treatment which so
good a judge as Zangwill reckons necessary for a
book and for the theatre. The comparison isn’t to
the advantage of the theatre. Zangwill’s book
knocks off at the departure of Mary A. from Mrs.
Leadbatter’s premises—and that is right, for
Launcelot’s “mash” would, in 999 cases out of
1000, have ended just there. But the conven-
tion-ridden theatre wouldn’t stand that. It must
hear wedding-bells, and catch the panting of The
Voice as it reaches for its hat. So for the
theatre Zangwill had to weave the flagrantly
improbable into a fourth act, and keep the un-
certain flame of affection alive through a vast
stretch of years, during which the pair never see
one another, and each lives quite another sort of
life. The theatre hasn’t, in fact, got further
ahead than the Emma Jane Worboise novel, in
which they always lived happily ever afterwards ;
and yet it calls for situations in the opening acts
which makes the living-’appily-ever-afterwards
just about impossible. There are playwrights, of
course, who kick ; but—well, they don’t, as a rule,
go strong to the end of a fourth week, as “ Merely
Mary Ann ”is doing. And then think of what sort
of a blaze (at the back of a grate) a book would make
if it spent pages on such circus-clowning as Peter’s
pouring of the tea on to Mrs. Leadbatter’s carpet
instead of into the cups. The audience screams
at this nightly ; and Peter, as he does the clown-
ing, must wonder, if they like that sort of thing,
why they don’t buy circus tickets instead of
orchestra stalls. On the whole, they are rather
sad reflections that you see in the fireplace when
you go home from “Merely-Mary Ann” in a
philosophic humor.

Sometimes a theatre programme tells fibs
in its description of the piece presented. But
when the programme for “The Gondoliers”
describes the opera as Charming, Tuneful and
Witty, it tells the truth—not the whole truth, and
yet nothing but the truth. The Repertoire Com-
pany opened with the opera of the Venetian boat-
men on Saturday, at Sydney Criterion, and
carried the piece to its conclusion with the perfec-
tion that comes of practice and voices in capital
condition. The cast is, with one exception, the
same as that which left the city some months ago.
Messrs. Vernon, Kenningham and Wilson, Misses
Dolly Castles, Aggie Thorne, and Vinia D’Loitte
(as her name appears on the programme) are
at home in their gondolas and songs, and
H. H. Wallace, who was so lately an amateur,
looks and acts a hardened old prof, as the
famous Duke, Limited, and is delightfully hen-
pecked by Celia Ghiloni as his duchess. Next
Saturday will be produced “The Sorcerer” and
“ Trial by Jury.”
* sfc *
The weeping old “East Lynne” gives
Miss Lily Dampier her star show as Lady Isabel.
On Saturday she started a season at Sydney
Palace, and presented the Mrs. Henry Wood
heroine in all her tearfulness, to a sympathetic
house. Some audiences delight in a scene
between a mother and her dying child more
than in anything else on this earth, and Miss
Dampier gave Saturday’s gathering its heart’s
desire in the Little Willie variety of misery.
The Bulletin thinks that Lady Isabel does her
late husband too much honor when she dies at his
feet. He may be her “ wronged husband,” but
he has consoled himself. A newer version of “East
Lynne” ought to be written—a version in which
the second wife turns out to be the first one, shehav-
ing deceived her husband by borrowing Barbara’s
transformations and pin-curls, Barbara in the
meantime having gone off with the wicked Francis
Levison. Miss Dampier’s version is, however,
warmly received by the audience, and the piece
will weep bitterly till Friday, and a bucket will
stand in the orchestra to catch the tears. On
Saturday “ The Postmistress of the Czar ” is to be
produced for the first time in Australia.
* * * -X
The extra-special programme at Sydney
Tivoli is now packing that house eight times a
week, and the house, under the circumstances, de-
serves the treatment; for Leroy, Talma, and
Bosco’s feats of spookery and sleight-of-hand are
served up in a fashion that Australia hasn’t had
before. To them and the other good turns—in-
cluding Crimmins and Gore in some more fan-
tastic foolery—has now been added Arthur Albert,
a descriptive singer, who doesn’t depend so en-
tirely on the limelight for the descriptiveness as
some singers who have come along with the same
label upon their brassy countenances.

On Saturday night there were not enough
seats in Melbourne Princess’ to hold all the people
who wanted to see Nellie Stewart in the new
character of a fugitive thief seek-
ing sanctuary “In the Bishop’s
Carriage.” Readers of advance
notices of said “comedy-drama”
(alias common melodrama) had
been stimulated to an interest in
the novelty of the girl-and-
gaiters situation. Soon after the
curtain rose, however, the situa-
tion sank to commonplace, as
it became apparent that th<>
Bishop had pretty
girl in the <pjw;>Awloak (de-
signed by Wo. on expressly for
this production), in ignorance of
her crime. A golden-haired,
expensively-clad young thing,
understood to be a parson’s
daughter, suffering from the
effects of overstudy at college,
is not such a very tough camel to
swallow. Even a saint of high
degree may stoop to the assist-
ance of Innocence in distress
without detriment to the Cloth.
And this particular saint, being
a doddering old thing with
crotchets in his voice and qua-
vers in his legs, accepted the
brazen, slangy lady-burglar at
her own valuation. Hence, the
inferiority of this ecclesiastic
(who does good inadvertently and
entertains a sinner unawares) to
Hugo’s bishop in “Les Miser-
ables.”
* * *
When the Bishop (Harry
Hill) is hugged in his carriage by
hysterical Nance Olden (Nellie
Stewart), who, bounding in with
a stolen watch in her pocket,
feigns to mistake him for her
clerical papa, he carries her off
to the protection of his friend,
Mrs. Ramsay (Miss Singleton).
Mrs. Ramsay is a fat, comic
matron, bent on marrying her
daughter (Nannie Bennett) to
one Latimer. Latimer (Harcourt Beatty) is
a tired young billionaire, too bored to
love the Ramsay girl, yet too lazy to take himself
off ; so he dawdles round, twaddling galore to the
afflicted spinster on threadbare old texts, less or
more to the effect that burglars are better than
bakers, and thieves more precious than tailors.
He’s all ready to fall in love with the lady-burglar
when the Bishop brings her along. She has a
lover already, a fine upstanding burglar who has
trained her m her profession; but Latimer’s
broad-minded philanthropy falls short of he-
robbers, so Tom gets hunted and threatened
with the police. Nance herself is for a time uncer-
tain as to whether she will become a pure and
blameless actress under the Latimer auspices,
or just go on burgling in the firm of Dorgan and
Co., house-breakers. While undecided, she brings
her house-breaking beau to the rooms of her new
admirer, and, hiding the Stousher on the premises,
departs in a whirl of regret, remorse, and general
hysteria. In the end, Latimer routs Dorgan with
a revolver and sends him to gaol, but it is no thanks
to the ladies that there isn’t a dead philanthropist
on hand in the morning! One writes “ladies”
advisedly, for the Ramsay spinster, Latimer’s high-
class admirer, smarting under the conscious-
ness of having been cut out, is a party to the
burglar scheme.
* * * #
Relieved of her cracksman chum, “Nance”
develops into a theatrical star respectfully
adored by her philanthropist, and persecuted by
the attentions of Ramsay senior (Dudley Clinton).
The latter is an inebriate ancient, built on lines
popular with American playwrights, who are fond
of depicting paterfamilias as bold as brass in
chasing scrapes, while all the time quaking in
maudlin dread of his wife’s wrath. In her pros-
perity, the ex-burglaress remains faithful to Convict
Tom, refusing Benefactor Latimer for his strong
sake. But her mind changes when Tom breaks
gaol, and arrives, looking hideous, to command
her to cross the ocean with him next morning.
The lady refuses, and then the escapee chases
her round the table. Sometimes he catches her
and shakes her hard enough to toss down her
lovely auburn hair, and cause her diamonds to
heave on her snowy decolletage. But consider-
ing his desperate reputation, one feels a


little surprised that Thomas should be kept
at bay by one small woman sprinting
round a dining table. Then Latimer rushes in with
a revolver and casts the gaol-bird into outer
darkness (i.e., foreign parts) with two dollars
in his pocket. In the act of farewell, however,
the baffled miscreant hands over a poc-
ket-book with nasty reflections on Nance’s
character, plus slanderous data re the lady’s
past connection with his own burglarious
self. The philanthropist casts Nance off for
evah. Of course, he repents, but too late!
The wronged Innocent has disappeared. At
last, she arrives at the Ramsays’ lawn-party in
the uniform of a “Sister” in the Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to Children. Harcourt
Beatty, whose grief at her absence has caused
him to look and dress exactly like chief-mourner
at a funeral, falls figuratively at her feet,
and all is cleared up at last. Then Nance, as a
celebrated actress, is persuaded to favor the com-
pany with a taste of her professional quality. The
ex-pickpocket, in pure, womanly tones, tells a
stupid little fable about a pipkin and a pot.
Nance, herself, stands for the pipkin, and benefac-
tor Latimer represents the philanthropic pot.
When they are married and living happily ever
afterwards, “Pipkin” and “Pot” must come
in conveniently for pet-names.
* * * *
In the earlier scenes of the play Nellie
Stewart gets some scope as a dare-devil baggage
of a girl. When the goody-goody stage sets in the
whole thing becomes too absurd to treat convinc-
ingly. _ Harcourt Beatty fails to do much with his
mawkish part. Always lacking in strength and
force of feeling, his present role has no use for his
intelligence. Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis is a lenient
stage-mother, while Miss Nannie Bennett and
Miss Willard present somewhat angular studies of
New York society girls.
* * * *•
Australia got its first impression of
“Veronique” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) last
Saturday. The French opera is a straight-going
work in three acts, a modernised opera bouffe
rather than a new departure on musical-comedy
lines. It has a well-defined form, and a style of
its own —a dainty style calculated to grow in
"pspnkwtw and take some of the rough edge off .
the public fetsjte. The music and the story will
not immediately Fite the dull ear and tickle the
fancy of the multitude at large ; nevertheless,
the effective dressing and pretty backgrounds in
“Veronique” should win admiration until the
merits of the show as a whole are fully appreciated.
The libretto is seemingly a mild translation of a
naughty French original. Lauri and Miss Young
are a married pair who conduct an amazing
Parisian flower shop (a.d. 1840) with a chorus of
girl attendants. The lady has a Florestan (John
Doran) who formerly shared her endearments,
but he is about to make the usual marriage of
convenience on his own account. He and the
bride elect (Miss Thomas) meet for the first
time when she happens into the flower shop
(incognito) with her massive mama (Miss Clifton)
and overhears an incriminating conversation
between the young man and his giddy Madame.
Then she decides not to reject Florestan, but to
reform him, which —as she remarks to Mania—is
“much worse.” The two unknown ladies get
themselves taken on as extra hands at the crazy
•hop, and Florestan, who travels with a bailiff
(Bantock) in attendance, takes the whole establish-
ment to a picnic where he is going to marry. The
3rd act takes them to the Tuilleries to sign the
marriage contract, and then Florestan discovers
that his appointed girl is one with the other girl.
Therefore his matrimonial intentions become
strictly virtuous, and nothing is left for Miss
Young but to find another Florestan or live happily
ever afterwards with her lawful ridiculous Lauri.
* * * *
Andre Messager’s music is more success-
fully treated by the orchestra under MacCann
than by the principal voices. Some of the choruses
were brightly sung on Saturday night; the solos,
duets, &c., lacked tune-color as a rule. The
singers sounded out of sympathy with their songs.
There was too much effort and not enough melody.
In the performance, apart from the singing, Lauri,
of the flower shop, took the honors as usual.
Miss Young and Miss Clifton did very well. Miss
Thomas was a rather magnificent sentimental
angel. Bantock, Percy, and Miss Milne met the
requirements, and John Doran was a tame
Lothario in strawberry continuations.

At Melbourne Bijou another Lauri —-
Edward, the strenuous endeavorer—is resorting
to his original character of Pineapple in “ A
Chinese Honeymoon.” Also, Miss May Beatty
is the same smudgy, tangle-headed Fi Fi as of
yore, and the other people are an improvement,
in some cases, on the co. that appeared at the
Princess’s when the “ Honeymoon ” was young
in the heavens, and the capers of Pineapple were
fresh cut.
At Melbourne Royal a number of little
tin gee-gees continue to rock themselves across
the distant landscape under the pretence of being
some actual live ’osses that clear a hurdle way
down by the orchestra. Bland Holt’s wondrous
Australian moraldrama is booked for another
week or so, and in the meantime Walter Baker is
an object of especial interest owing to a recent
performance in which he made a decided hit
before he hastily withdrew round the corner.
Facing the enemy’s gun at the Royal is a very
different matter to doing it in Bourke-street.
** * *
None of the other recent arrivals at
Melbourne Opera House are quite so well ap-
plauded as the handsome ’Murkan lady, briefly
described as Whitaker. The she-partner in
the Hill and Whitaker firm comes presum-
ably from the Soutli where de cotton grows,
and her humorous imitation of a niggah
man in a descriptive coon song is new to these
parts. Moreover, she has a beautiful rich voice.
Her ditty and her joyous smile are a comforting
reminder that a white gal can surpass our colored
brudder in his particular line of musical comedy.
** * *
The circuses are still inviting comparison
within easy smell of the Yarra; also Princes
Court stands where it did, and a Wild West Show
happens at the Cyclorama building. But Father
Robinson’s bazaar has run its course at the Town
Hall (where the Lyric Orchestral Society concerts
to-night, Thursday), prior to closing its art union
with a “draw ’’ on the 29th. The Bio-Tableau is
likewise out of Melbourne by this time, and the
Athenaeum Hall will be the home of the Gorriek
Family for one week, beginning on Saturday.
** # *
Adelaide Royal (swept and varnished, and
made fire-and-burglar proof) has been re-opened
by Charles Holloway, who unkindly depicts “The
Rogues of the Turf” for the edification of the
sports now returning from the loss of their money
at Flemington. Harry Rickards, in a speech
from the stage at his own little house, the Tivoli,
has expressed a wish to keep it open all the time,
instead of only in spasms. His present co. in-
cludes the musical Jacksons and Payen, the
automaton. It is rather a pity that the latter
gives his show away at the end, instead of leaving
the audience doubting whether it isn’t really a
machine after all, as the best Paris and London
figures mostly do. Miss Florence Challis works
hard to make a success of a very moderate London
ballad of the Old Bull and Busli. Someone ought
to write an Australian version and call it the “Old
Cow and Scrub” ; but the true inner significance of
a lady’s drinking “ port wine ” would be lost on a
local audience, anyway.
EXPLAINED.
“ Gran?papa, wot makes you get up so late?”
“ Faith, Terence, Oim an ould man now, an' Oi
shlape very slow.”
A little while ago, the N.S.W. Railway
Department wanted 10,000 chromo-lithographs
printed, and, though there is a splendid Govern-
ment printing-office in Sydney, it sent the work
to Switzerland. There was not a word of objec-
tion from the Freetrade section in N.S.W. State
Parliament about this. But when the Australian
Government proposed to send a stamp-printing
job to Adelaide, for the sake of economy, that
•ame section foamed with rage, and talked of
secession and other wild animals. The Carruthers
crowd is absolutely pleased to see employment
taken away from N.S.W. workers so long as it is
given to some foreigner, but when it hears of a job
going to S. Australia it raves like a lunatic who is
full of beer. Its one”consistent Federal principle
consists of a carefully cultivated hatred of every
part of Australia except N.S.W., and it hasn’t
any practical love for N.S.W. except when it
hears of some other State getting a job.

“VERONIQUE.”
There are lots of nice girls trimly skirted in green,
There are men in most various guise,
There’s a new sort of charm in each “ Veronique”
scene
Which appeals to the merry and wise.
There are heroines twain very gone on a man,
The first of three giddy gallants;
They gently dote on his lavender coat,
And adore his crushed-strawberry pants.
Many heroes are “dreams” on the opera stage,
For they wear irresistible togs,
To girls whose affections they freely engage
They are ever the gayest of dogs.
All the figures are gay in the musical play,
But the fellow who chiefly enchants
The susceptible fair sports a tight-fitting pair
Of the sweetest crushed-strawberry pants.

AT POVERTY POINT.
Grant Hervey, a writer whose ringing verses are well-
known to every reader of The Bulletin, helped to
provide Melbourne with a sensation last week, when he
shot a public-house in Bourke-street. Hervey is a very
large and powerful poet, and he was escorting Mrs. Baker
home. Walter Baker, who has suffered from unjust
suspicion for about 16 years in Bland Holt’s dramatic
company (mostly through the machinations of Albert
Norman whom Holt pays to bring Baker into trouble),
sighted them. Baker is a very large actor, and he seen s
to have plugged Hervey on the jaw before the latter had
any idea that hostilities had broken out. Baker next
seized his wife and started to run, while the half-stunned
writer reached for the atmosphere. Then recovering
himself he snatched out a “gun,” and as Baker
emerged from behind his wife and fled round a
corner, Hervey fired and hit a perfectly innocent public-
house. A detective fell on him just then, and as
Hervey pulled himself together and offered no
resistance, his intentions don’t seem to have been
generally ferocious. When Hervey had surrendered and
given up the revolver, the large figure of Baker re-
appeared round the corner and said things till the police-
man had to tell him not to “rub it in.” There was lan-
guage on both sides, but the professional actor had at
command all the lofty remarks he has been throwing at
Albert Norman in the drama for 16 years, and so had a
great advantage over the journalist. The charge was
“ shooting with intent to murder”, and bail was fixed at
£2OO. The public-house was not much damaged.
“S.”: Grant Hervey was fearfully excited after the
shooting case in Melbourne last week, and if he had let
himself go and shown that he was not himself he would
have been doing a wise thing. Instead he made a supreme
effort, gathered up the ends of his dancing nerves, pulled
himself together, and assumed an air of nonchalance that
will do him the greatest possible harm when the* case
comes before the court. Mrs. Baker, whose scream min-
gled with the report of Hervey’s revolver, disappeared as
soon as Hervey was arrested. She attended at the Police
Court next day, however, and smiled graciously on the
accused, who—perfectly composed and irreproachably
dressed, though he had spent the night in a cell in Little
Bourke-street—-bowed gracefully and smiled back at her.
They were allowed a few minutes’ conversation after the
proceedings were over, whereat Walter Baker must have
ground his teeth.
Walter Baker, who retreated in so masterly a way when
Grant Hervey fired a revolver at him, in Bo'urke-st., Mel-
bourne, last week, has a rather unusual record for bravery.
His first start was capturing an armed highwayman with
his naked hands, about 25 years ago. Baker was then an
assistant grocer in a baekblock town in N.S.W. He
chased his man four miles, and secured a Government
reward of £250. Later on, at Mount Bischoff, in Tasmania,
he rushed into a burning house and rescued a child, at
the peril of his life. His hair was all burnt off, and he
fell insensible after stumbling into safety with the young-
ster. He has also done some life-saving- by water.

Musg-rove has engaged a strong support for Nellie
Stewart through the American tour—capable performers
and striking as to appearance. It is understood that the
two Rignold sisters—Dora and Marie—will be divinely
tall members of the co., and the balance of style will have
to be maintained by at least a couple of heroic-looking
young men players. Musgrove’s idea seemingly is to give
Yankee journalism a great chance to boom “ Sweet Nell ”
as a beauty show, with the most decorative all-round
actress in the world as the star.
Miss Unie Russell was induced to sign on for another
six months with the Knight-Jeffries Go., after she had
given notice of her intention to quit. This re-engagement
seems to have been the outcome of Miss Unie’s sudden
appointment to the leading lady part in “ David Garrick,”
which she undertook at three days’ notice and played
very prettily.
“L.”: It seems just about time for the idea worked
out in “ Davy Garrick ” to take a long rest. Sixty years
ago Theophile Gautier complained that the French
theatres were frequently occupied by -‘the everlasting-
Story of Garrick, Talma, or Kean curing some foolish girl
of a passion for them as actors by exhibiting themselves
in private life under the most repulsive conditions.” At
about that time eight Paris playwrights almost simul-
taneously produced dramas founded on one of these
Garrick myths. Versions of most of their versions,
besides others derived from the German and from the
Kean plays referred to by Gautier, have since been in-
flicted on the English-speaking public when it wasn’t
strong enough to defend itself. Charles Reade and half-
a-dozen others have rung the chang-es on the story by
the leading- character an actress—usually Mrs.
Bracegirdle. If the tired old legend is not to be allowed
to totter toothless to its tomb just yet, a revival-of one
of these last-mentiohed.plays, in which the.limelight is
not all intended for.a beauty actof with- incredible legs,
would be an acceptable change.
“ F.C.” : I wish to vent a grievance against the manage ■
inent of Princes’ Court, Melbourne. The chute chutes
all right, and the Jap girls, who aren’t Jap, are nice
enough, also the helter-skelter has no tacks where they
shouldn’t be ; but I reckon “Dastro” has deceived me.
D., aforesaid, a firm-faced gentleman of Australian ex-
traction, rides a bike down a chute 60ft long with a 35ft.
drop, then shoots up a strip, rising 2ft. in 9ft., then
slithers across a vacant space to where the tail end of his
“chute” is, and so to the ground. This gap, or chasm,
measured just 27ft., and Dastro found by experience
that he landed about 10ft. over it, so one night he
shifted the tail end of his chute further away,
making the gap 40ft. Perhaps this was too large
a shift; anyhow, when he shot down in his usual
hurry, the planks he expected to laud on weren’t
there, and a surprised Dastro collided with
cinders and gravel. Now my grievance is that I have
witnessed the performance several times without experi-
encing any thrill—it seems quite easy when .you see it
done —and when there was a chance of a sensation, the
management failed to properly advertise it. And to think
that for a wretched sprat I might have had the chance of
seeing a man killed ! Certainly he wasn’t killed, nor even
seriously damaged, hut that wasn’t his fault. If he is
going to lengthen his chasm and attempt suicide again I
want to be there. It’s the cheapest thrill I know of, and
I’m a whale for cheap thrills.
The other week when Horace Lingard produced “Our
Boys ” in Melbourne, he stated that H. J. Byron’s widow
is badly in need of any copyright fees she can get from
such occasional performances of her late husband’s plays.
11. J. Byron’s work continues to provide sport for
amateurs, who never recognise the widow’s claim in the
matter. Amateur theatrical managements have always
been audaciously piratical, except when they produced,
say, a Gilbert and Sullivan opera by arrangement with
J.C.W., and he, by the way, is no longer willing to let
his goods out on hire to rival speculators. A move
against the pirates was lately made by J. A. Miller, of
Melbourne, acting as attorney for Samuel French and Co.,
of London. Thusfar, Miller has got a promise of co-
operation from none of the amateur competition promo-
ters except the A.N.A. National Fete Committee. In
reply to his letter stating the case for French and Coi’s
copyrights, the A.N.A. committee promised that amateur
companies entering for competitions will be required to
show the consent of the author’s agents before they
can produce any comedy on which a fee is chargeable.
Dan Barry lately won a country editor’s heart, and
the Areas Express (Gladstone, S.A.) printed the
following “ The drama ‘ The Prisoner of War,’ or ‘ The
Russian Spy,’ which Dan Barry’s company is to play on
Wednesday night next, in the Institute Hall, Glad-
stone, is likely to draw a record house, especially
as the combination is headed by the genial and gifted
actor-manager himself. Incidents connected with the
momentous Russo-Japanese war are the main features
of the play, so that it can be understood as being
up-to-date. It is now the reigning dramatic furore in
the Old Country, and such has been its success that the
author is said to have netted £6OOO for his share of the
profits out of the first two months of its production in
London. From its initial performance in that city it
made a profound impression ; all classes of playgoers have
rushed to see it in thousands., Even clergymen and
others who do not as a rule visit theatres have not been
able to resist the fascination which the play seems to
exercise.”
In a recent comedy, “ What the Butler Saw,” a giddy
husband sums up marriage as “ one of those blessings a
man gets so accustomed to that they are apt to slip his
memory.”
“ Skull ” : Among Australian actors Walter Baker is
easily first as a pedestrian. A long experience of Walter
leaves in my mind an excellent impression of him as a
footracer. He is always starting from scratch. You have
a mental picture of him posing in the wings toeing the
mark, impatiently waiting the starter’s pistol. Crack !
and on bounds the hero. After that throughout the piece
W.B. is continually waiting for the word “go,” and then
going like a sprinter out after records. He jumps from
mark at all sorts of situations. He runs on, he runs off,
he makes false runs and goes through preliminary
canters, and it is not his fault if every piece has not a long
run. No wonder there is a popular idea that Walter is a
dashing actor. He makes more dashes in the course of
five acts than half-a-dozen ordinarily energetic heroes.
“F.”: Anent “Oxo” and stars with mothers in the,
chorus (B. 12/10/’O5). In all stage crowds, in pieces in
which G. V. Brooke appeared at old Melbourne Royal,
“Mrs. G. V. Brooke” —not Avonia Jones —always made
one.
Early in her career Ellen Terry was known as Nellie
Terry, but decided that her formal name was better
suited to the parts she undertook. Perhaps Australia’s
leading Nellie will make the same change if she takes
altogether to tragedy.

fessional, pa Hambourg, the other night. Mark is
away shekel-making in S’Africa. The brothers will pro-
bably revisit Australia in the near future, and may then
take their father with them. He is a fine, handsome,
shaggy old Continental, with greying chin and lip adorn-
ments on Napoleon the Little lines. Mark and Boris
declare, however, that they won’t take the old man with

“ O K.” : Satisfactory to hear that when Julius Knight
was making a farewell speech, at Melbourne Princess’s,
he merely destroyed a bit of blue silk sash by twisting it
nervously round his fingers. When he attempted a first-
night speech, at Her Majesty’s, some two or three years
ago, Julius hung on to the hand of Miss Jeffries and
twisted that round his finger until the lady’s countenance
expressed agony and scorn.
Bernard Shaw’s latest, “Passion, Poison, and Petri-
faction, or the Fatal Gazogene,” is a quaint satire on
various styles of bellowdrama. The play, announced as
“ by the Chelsea Shakspeare, George Bernard Shaw,” was
performed in a tent at a recent garden party in aid of the
English Actors’ Orphanage fund. G. P. Huntley and
other leading mummers were in the cast. Early in the
play Lady Magnesia FitzTollemaehe goes to bed on the
stage, still retaining her hat and parasol—evidently a slap
at bed scenes in “ Camille,” “ Pretty Peggy,” etc. She is
awakened by her husband,trying to stab her with a dagger,
which he weakly attempts to pass off as a new fish-slice.
Enter Adolphus Bastable, arrayed in magenta and puce,
instead of the ordinary black and white evening-dress of
Sassiety, so that nobody will ever again be able to take
him for a waiter. The villainous FitzTollemaehe, enraged
at being foiled in the attempt on his wife, treats Adol-
phus to whisky diluted from a poisoned gazogene. Fitz-
Tollemache suddenly repents, and gives Adolphus some
lime from the ceiling as an antidote, but an -over-dose of
it solidifies within him, and turns him to stone. Various
minor murders and suicides happen in between, and
throughout the play a “Chorus of Invisible Angels ” gives
assistance. The closing scenes consist of a series of
sudden deaths, accompanied by the squawking- of Zion’s
white-robed choir. Altogether, the drama ought to be a
howling success with the connoisseurs who appreciate
“The Booziest Bounder in Bendigo ” and “The Leg at
the Loophole.”
Melbourne Johnnydom is dying or dead. Father
Robinson, prince of promoters, wishful of making his
bazaar in Melbourne Town Hall rake in large masses of
shekels, induced the Royal Comic Opera Company to run
the show for him one afternoon. From past experience
of such things he figured out that straw-hatted, high-
collared, tan-booted Johnnydom, in its new Cup suit,
would roll up in hundreds, mournfully mouth its silver-
headed stick, and hand over its wealth for pin-cushions
and flowers and bottles of scent and raffle-tickets, sold
with a smile and a word by a footlight charmer. But,
alas ! the fowler’s snare caught almost nothing. The
brightest of the chorus, and all the principals, turned out
in swell frocks and witching smiles—but Johnnydom
came not. About 150 people wandered into the Town
Hall and listened to the singing of the company stars.
The chorus girls ivere disconsolate, and early in the after-
noon gave up the job of trying to sell dear nothings to
dowdy women and green-grocery-looking men. The
cleric’s little ruse had failed, and his faith in human
nature was shaken to the plinth. There is no more
Johnnydom.
An Australian contralto who promises to get a tangible
hold on the British market is Miss Maud Dalrymple, of
Sydney, who has just made her first London appearance
at Henry Wood’s promenade concerts. Her voice,
although shaken a little through nervousness, gave evi-
dence of such quality that she bad to repeat her bow
four times after her first song. The good-looking Maud
has booked several good London -engagements for the
winter season, including one or two of the Chappell
ballad concerts.
“ Pro.”: Even slang words have their fine distinc-
tions. To “pong” is not the same as to “gag,” which
means the introduction of extraneous stuff. Ponging is
paraphrasing the text, when; imperfectly acquainted
with it.

Ministry getting on the right fiscal track. The

Wise, is clearly assisting itself to a wise policy.

The oldest house but one on the North
Shore of Sydney Harbor, “The Hangers,” at
Mosman, is about to come under the hammer.
The picturesque and quaint structure, with its
belfry, was built about the year 1840 by Oswald
Bloxsome, who had a grazing property in the
Liverpool Ranges. In or about the year 1846
Oswald Brierley, the marine artist, was a guest,
in ill health, and as a pastime he painted on the
dining-room wall a picture of the Rattlesnake in a

* * * *
Any more casual person than the smal
boy has yet to be found. Witness the experience
of a Camperdown (Vic. ) parson, as recorded in the
Argus the other day. Parson, driving past a deep
waterhole, saw a youngster playing on the bank,
and pulled up to warn him that he might fall in.
“My brother’s in already,” the nipper replied
brightly, and went on scraping mud. ihe parson
was lust in time to hsh out a youngster who
seemed to be going down for the last time. The
kid on the bank hadn’t thought the incident worth
mentioning until the parson interfered.
In other days, the ghost of Michael O’Byrne
would have walked William-street, Sydney. Mick,
when in the flesh and flush of cash, willed the
Bible Hall, standing half-way up that street, to
the R.C. Church authorities, and fixed a sum to
be wholly devoted to Bible exposition. After a
time, the interest in Bible exposition fell off, and,
as it was no good expounding without an audience,
the authorities decided to, if allowed, devote the
money, or part of it, to secular education. The
Equity Court has had a long wrestle with Mick’s
ghost, but, last week, Justice A. H. Simpson pro-
nounced that the ghost should fall into line with
modern ideas, and £5O a-year of the late rum-
seller’s hoardings will consequently go to the
secular education of children. Somehow the
ghosts of the dead, meaning thereby their wills,
are losing their grip of the world, and their threat
to walk about with a sheet around them if their
directions about the cash should be neglected are
scorned. Nowadays, a tram-car would as soon
run over a ghost as not.

“ Jonah Jeremiah” writes
Am a Yank, just starting business here. Bought your
journal as I stepped off the boat. Been here one week.
Beckon this country wants you badly, and it can do with
me, not having seen any great natural resources so far,
except those I brought with me.
Oct. 17th : Sent from Haymarket (9 a.m.) to Central Post
Office for telephone. “ Switch me on punctually at
2 p.m.” Punctually paid fees.
Oct. 25th : A sad stranger appeared. He had a gimlet
and a length of wire. He made a mess generally in an
unbusinesslike manner which might have had connection
with the carved front of your Central Post Office, but
none with telephones.
Oct. 27th : Found two gimlets and more wire and
carving, but nothing else worth mentioning.
Used half-a-dollar’s worth of stamps on the Depart-
ment. Only result a still and mysterious development of
the higher courtesy on the part of polite officials, appa-
rently anxious to help a ballooning stranger down to real
ground. Finally told, “No telephones in the State until
a shipment arrived.” My suffocated gosh !
Afterwards found that everything arrives by ship, and
when the ship doesn’t arrive there isn’t anything. Why
not make something ?
• • • *
The enthusiastic efforts of William Malone
to go on the land out Deniliquin way (N.S.W.),
are unhappily ended. In 1900 Malone applied for
an annual lease of a bit of land held by O’Keefe
under an occupation license. The Lands Appeal
Court in a friendly way booted O’Keefe off and
Malone entered on his land, renewing the lease
every succeeding year. The indignant O’Keefe
appealed to a Supreme Court Judge, who ordered
Malone off the land. Whereupon Malone put the
case to the Full Court, which ordered O’Keefe off
the land. O’Keefe trumped Malone’s trick by per-
suading the Privy Council to reverse the Full
Court’s ruling, and once more Malone was ordered
off the land. Giving O’Keefe best, the unflagging
Malone sued “the Crown” for a refund of the
money paid as rent for land he was not legally in
possession of, which claim ambled round the courts
until it came before the Full one. Unabashed
by the fact that its previous finding in the case had
been knocked endways, the Full Court announced
that the whole thing was as simple as possible. A
large and special rock on which the British Con-
stitution rests is that no one can recover on a mis-
take of law. Everyone—the Minister, the Lands
Department, the Lands Court, Malone, and others
(others being the august Full Court itself)—had
mistaken the law, and that ended it. Malone
must pay the costs. Malone is no longer on the
land, but O’ Keefe is, so the progress of land settle-
ment is not quite paralysed.

“A movement is on foot” (by the wayy
why don’t movements sometimes goby train, or
’bus, or motor-car, or something, for a change ?) to
have Balmain called a city. It used to be a plea-
sant old place, where sea-captains settled down
and spun yarns; but in its smoky dusty noisy
days it wants to put on dog, and “ the Mayor is
at the head of the movement.” Just what good it
will do Balmain, supposing the movement
arrives, isn’t mentioned. What is most likely
to happen is that the precious Mayor and
councillors will wear some extra frill, and will
want an extra coat of paint put upon the
unnecessary town hall, and another coat of
varnish provided for the cupboard that Balmain
could very well do without; and consequently
they will yap more angrily than ever at any pro-
posal to wipe out all the unnecessary town halls,
and cupboards, and the 43 or 44 little suburban
mayors of no consequence, and the same number
of council clerks. And as all this would postpone
the coming of a Greater Sydney, The Bulletin
hopes that the movement, and every other one like
it, will get corns and spring-halt and other infirmi-
ties, so that it will be on foot no longer. On
the other hand, if the mayor and his dusty
suburb of Balmain are not simply after
ham-frill, and want to do something of
real yalue, they might tackle Balmain’s debt.
The place owes £11,500, which is costing over 4
per cent, a year, and another £59,000, which is
costing over 5 per cent, a year, besides a good
amount of floating debt, and of the whole lot only
a poor little £3500 is represented by anything that
can ever earn any interest, apart from the Town
Hall. And though all this debt is represented by
dead works, Balmain hasn’t got anything in the
shape of a sinking fund—-it pays something off
when it feels that way, and when it doesn’t feel
that way it just borrows some more, and mean-
time is paying away about a fourth of its whole
revenue in interest. And all it can think of doing
in those circumstances is “ to set a movement on
foot ”to get itself called a city. The Bulletin’s
view is that every municipa'lty which is clean
and out of debt should be a city, and should have
a Lord Mayor if it wants one. If it gets into debt
again it should become only a town with a mayor,
and when the debt gets past a certain point it
should become officially a hovel, and its muni-
cipal head should be just called Bumble or Dog-
berry.
* * * *
Sydney Morning Herald, noting Chief
Justice Darley’s appointment to the English Privy
Council, said “it would be felt that his Honor
had established a claim to the call to the inner
councils of the Empire.” As a matter of fact, to
be a Privy Councillor to-day is merely an empty
honor. The Privy Council is never called upon
now to confer with and advise the Sovereign, and
the Sovereign doesn’t know most of its members
from a herd of cattle. The alleged position carries
with it the prefix of “Right Honorable” and
nothing more. It doesn’t even confer a free rail-
way pass.

Recently the Westralian Government im-
ported a man from Queensland as chief director of
Agriculture at £750 and a liberal travelling allow-
ance. His predecessor, who was not considered
good enough for the billet, was placed in charge of
the Rabbit Department, and draws nearly as
much salary as the new man, who was a jour-
nalist, or something of that sort, in Bananaland
His reports supply Perth papers with many
columns of free copy. Here’s a sample from one
of his latest lucubrations, a pretty fair sample of
the lot:
It is evident that the progress of agriculture in the
future must be along lines of intellectual achievement,
and the fanner, to be successful, must use his brains as
well as his hands. We have crossed the Rubicon which
separates the kingdoms of brawn and brain ; henceforth
and for evermore agriculture will be ruled and directed
from the kingdom of brain. The gospel of agriculture
must be preached, and then, under the new dispensation,
there shall be an awakening in the new agriculture in all
our rural districts.
Which is very large and beautiful and eloquent,
but, unhappily, you can’t grow ’taters on an
epigram or feed cows on a peroration. Westralia
still fails to supply itself with its own vegetables,
yet it is the only State where the Agricultural
Department beats Demosthenes and leaves Cicero
panting in the rear.

A SHOOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘Twas in the crowd upon the Block,
The tall man stood in front of me.
Six p.m. said the Town Hall clock
A week ago come Saturdee. *> -
He was a human monster that -
Stood six foot six, or pretty nigh,
He wore a glossy topper hat
And great glass window on his eye.
His ribs were wrapped in square-cut coat,
A brolly dangled from his arm,
His tartan tie and collared throat
Lent extra lustre to his charm.
Race glasses on his arm hung free.
It seemed he’d come straight from the course,
So I approached quite modestly
(I had a shilling on a horse).
I looked the long slab down and up,
Then humbly and politely spoke :
“Mister, what won the Valley Cup
(I’ve got a deener on the tote) ? ”
He handed down a withering stare.
It pierced me to the very marrow.
“ I nevah back demmed horses, sir,
I’ve been to Henley-on-the-Yarra.”

SYDNEY, AND 93 QUEEN ST., BRISBANE.


The capsizing of a punt on the Saltwater
River gave the Melbourne journals a fine chance
to improve the gloomy item. In the first place it
was announced as a wholesale Cup Day tragedy—
“ Several persons drowned—two bodies recovered.”
Next day’s scare-headings stated that the police
were still dragging the river for human corpses,
but had as yet only succeeded in recovering the
bodies of sundry cows, sheep and dogs. Then
slowly and reluctantly the press admitted that
the two persons first mentioned were the only
victims of the tragedy. The police had been
dragging the river for bodies that were merely
drowned in the waters of newspaper fiction.
* * * *
Before N.S. W. Arbitration Court, recently,
a coal-lumper confessed to having worked
hours at a stretch. “ Without a sleep ?” inquired
the Court, in astonishment. “ Without a sleep,
without a wash, and without taking my clothes
off,” supplemented witness. “Ever have any
other long shifts?” inquired barrister Hughes.
“ Yes ; I’ve worked so long in a week that there
were only seven hours left for sleep.” “You’re
an extraordinary man to be able to do it,” com-
mented Judge Heydon.
* * * *
Even the Law acquires a placid, gradual
habit in the sleepy town of Geelong. The other
Sunday afternoon a delinquent, suspected of
insulting behavior, moved somewhat rapidly
down the main street ahead of a policeman, peeled
off, and fled into the bay. The Sunday pro-
menaders were interested, and offered frequent
advice, but the Law took up a masterly attitude
on the discarded garments, fixed its eye on the
swimmer roving to and fro, and dwelt thus for
the space of two hours. The shivering youth was
on the point of collapse once or twice, but the
Law merely observed that it was much drier and
warmer on shore, and that everything was pre-
, pared for bis reception. Masterly inactivity
‘'•fZ'ipfy won Tiif day, and the damp, dilapidated
victim was escorted to the watchhouse and fined
£5.

* * * *
A notably heroic deed last week was that
of William Ferrier, who, the brigantine Bella
being wrecked at Warrnambool (Vic.), went out
in his little dingy, rowing with one oar through
seas that the lifeboat dared not face, and brought
two men off the ship. Ferrier is a fisherman—one
of the very few fishermen in Australia, which in
the main allows its sea-harvests to go untilled
whilst it imports tinned and salted fish from
abroad. Thereby it gets poorer food and more
ptomaines, and loses the chance of a big fisher
population, of all folk the most heroic in peace
and war.

At last Thursday’s sitting of the Melb.
Police Commission “Sergeant Rogerson com-
plained that the police seldom had one Sunday
free in a year. That made a man callous and
hardened, so that he did not care for God or Devil.
A man should think of his God” :
The namby-pamby Woman’s Page of S.M.
Herald has broken out afresh :
Sons of Japanese gentlemen are sent to Townsville in
numbers to learn the English language. To support
themselves they take positions as cooks, waiters, or
domestic servants. They are refined young men, and very
grateful to anyone who is kind to them ... A highly
educated Australian girl had a night-school until recently
in Townsville for the Japanese cooks, waiters, etc. The
“ boys ” always behaved well, and got on capitally with
their lessons . . . The moral teaching of the Bible is to
them very unimportant. . . When the teacher’s birthday
came round her pupils presented her with a pink silk
nightgown, a beautifully embroidered garment. They
were much concerned as to the fit of the sleeves.
Yes, they don’t worry much about the moral
teaching of the Bible, but they do take a wonder-
ful interest in the white woman’s bed-costume.
The police crusade against publicans in
Brisbane is giving the local police-court beak
quite a rush of business, and the J.P. is looking
more ponderously wise than usual. The other
day a man was fined for “drinking on a Sunday,”
but the publican, on the same evidence, was
acquitted. The curious part of the business was
that the evidence only showed the man had a
frothing tankard at his elbow when the police
entered. The publican said the-drink was for the
cook. As the Bench let the publican go, the in-
ference is that in. his case they believed the cook
part of the affair, but didn’t believe it as applied
to the other fellow. Also the fact that the pot
was full seemed to be regarded as proof that the
accused had drunk part of its contents. The ways
of the J.P. mind are like the track of a serpent
which leaves no footmark on the rock.

Sydney Telegraph lets loose a fiery, un-
tamed poet to mourn over the sadness of the results
of Federation. He records that at the inauguration
of the Commonwealth —
Let that poet cheer up. When the “mountains
circling danced” at the inauguration of the Com-
monwealth, it was because of the vast quantity of
champagne that Bill Lyne had poured out. Now
that the mountains have settled down to “stand in
silent dignity,” it’s really better for the country. A
mountain when standing can be used for fruit-grow-
ing, or pasture, or residence. A mountain cutting
giddy capers is no use to man or beast. Indeed,
one of the reasons why The Bulletin supports
Federation is that under Federation the moun-
tains are so quiet and dignified.

Christopher Robinson, the Governments were admirable
considered as orphanages and creches for tired mission-
aries, but farcical in any of their attempts to give Aus-
tralia interest for its money by easing the difficulties of
the white settler.
A blast against two sacred institutions in one sen-
tence—no wonder the Herald held its breath,
and didn’t recover it till the Saturday issue was
out, and it felt sure that the awful sacrilege
hadn’t crept in somehow while it wasn’t looking !
The Papuan law allows missionaries to take all
the natives they require without indentures or
payment so long as they take them for “educa-
tional purposes”; the ordinary person who isn’t
a missionary has, of course, to sign indentures,
pay his boys, and return them when done with.
Yet, according to Bedford—-
At one mission station a number of boys are finding
“ education ” in the missionary saw-mill, which takes
Government contracts for timber, and so competes with
white labor or the white employer who pays his brown
helpers; at the same station the price of boatbuilding
per foot has been so cut by the owners of the unpaid
Papuan for “ education purposes ” that the white boat-
builder is cut out of the trade.
Under that sort of law there ought to be pretty
good dividends in Papuan Industries, Limited—
always provided it mixes God and Mammon in the
right proportions.
Bedford’s yarn recalls the evidence given in a
case heard at Samarai (British Papua) a while ago,
and reported in the Endeavor Beacon, of Cook-
town. It was a case brought by A. S. Meek
against the Rev. C. A. Abel—and the Rev. F. W.
Walker, of Papuan Industries, Limited, appears,
to have been locum tcnens for the other brother in
the vineyard, which was a cowyard in this instance.
This Meek thought to start in business as a dairy-
man at Samarai, and, as Brother Abel was in
the business already, he tried to come to terms
with his only competitor. Meek’s story was that
they did come to terms; the missionary’s story
was that, when Meek bought a young bull and
four calves from the missionary for £5l, and other
cattle on liberal terms, he wasn’t buying the good-
will of the business, but only bull and calves ; and
when the missionary continued to send the milk-
can round Samarai, they went to law. The mis-
sionary won, but that is of no consequence ; the
interesting fact is that, according to the report in
the Cooktown paper, Missionary Abel swore—
I sold some cattle to Mr. Meek. I got a fair value.
After selling I left for England. Mr. Walker acted for me
during my absence. I left instructions, which were that
I had had a conversation with Mr. Meek, saying that I
would give him a fair start, and that as soon as Mr. Meek
was ready he was to inform Mr. Walker, and he would
allow him an open field. I made no promise to stop
sending milk. My milk supply to Samarai has never
ceased to my knowledge.
What Missionary Abel paid his “boys,” or
whether they were receiving an education in cow-
raising, and consequently didn’t call for such base
reward as wages, didn't transpire; possibly in
this case he did pay them—there is, anyhow, no
evidence to the contrary. All the same, the mis-
sionary is still Samarai’s milkman (or was till
recently); and the mere lay trader has retired,
beaten. He evidently didn’t know the right pro-
portions in which to mix God and Mammon.
* * * *
John Johnson, the North Shore (Sydney)
contractor who suicided a few days ago, leaving
five children to his sister’s care and a frantic letter
to a barmaid, had a romance in his life. As a
lonelv widower he sought the love of a girl who
dispensed liquids at a Chatswood pub., and after
a courtship which included handsome presents to
the lady and bushels of letters, a marriage was
arranged. The wedding guests assembled, and
the wedding garment was starched and ironed,
and the feast was ready, but the girl who used to
dispense fluid refreshment at the bar came not.
She was a minus quantity and a vanished memory,
She didn’t even leave her other hair hanging on
a nail. A week or two later she was arrested,
while jerking beer at a new bar, and charged with
getting goods under false pretences. The defence
consisted chiefly of scoffing remarks by P. Crick
about the bridegroom’s age. Johnson professed to
take it all philosophically, but he took drink with
it, and the end was sudden. The letter that he
left for the girl—a languid-looking girl in expen-
sive clothes and with great possibilities in the way
of fat—was pathetic but not reproachful. The
poor devil loved her.

Recalled by the death of its founder that
the Y.M.C.A. was originally started “for im-
proving the spiritual condition of young men
engaged in the drapery and other trades.” And
it has had the drapery fluff in its soul ever since.
TONGUE LOYALTY.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Postmaster-General (Mr. Austin Chapman) has
decided that the new Federal stamp shall bear the Kin„ s
head. —Cable Item.
When slaughtered is the western swan,
The emus to the bush are fled,
And Hobart knows not Wellington,
Rejoice that we shall lick instead
The head
Of Ned.
Then Royalty, by art refined,
Will be, as Mr. Chapman said,
Appropriately licked behind
On stamps that bear, in green and red,
The head
Of Ned.
The more the royal race we lick,
The more the craze to crawl is fed,
And all the tighter will they stick
Who laud with deference inbred
The head
Of Ned.
VIC.
A.B.
The Melbourne p’liceman’s daily beat
With sinful steps is trod ;
He giveth heed to cold and heat—
He meditates on drink and meat—
But O, the bobby on his beat
Gives little heed to God.
From month to month the same old round
The serg-eant stern must plod.
In summer time his face is browned,
In winter o’er the muddy ground
He goes his weary worldly round,
Nor ever praises God.
At some among the careless throng
He condescends to nod.
They whistle as they walk along,
They hum, perchance, a comic song :
But none among the careless throng
Remind him of his God.
The p’liceman needs his Sundays free
From sordid thoughts of “ quod.”
He would not go upon the spree
Or smoke beneath a shady tree.
If “ bobby ” had his Sundays free
He’d turn his mind to God.
With boots of regulation make
His sturdy feet are shod.
Now give thim rest, for mercy’s sake,
And let those fe t, that often ache
In boots of regulation make,
Be turned at last to God.
THE QUARREL.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A YOUNG man and a maiden
Upon the grass reclined;
With tears her eyes were laden —
He didn't seem to mind.
He watched the clouds sail over,
And smoked in peace profound;
She plucked the sweet white clover,
And gazed upon the ground.
To me she looked bewitching
(He didn't seem to care);
Her fingers they were itching
To pull—or stroke —his hair.
'Twas easy to discover
That each one suffered pain —
Ah me, to be a Lover,
And suffer so again!
N.S.W.
C.R.
Thine ocean laughed for joy, and all thy shores
Resounded gleefully ; the towering cliffs,
And deep-set, tree-filled vales had voice and sang ;
The rivers joined in tune ; while, hand in hand,
The mountains circling danced.
But now the difference :
Thine ocean smiles not, and its sea-washed sand
In sympathy is still ; silent are all
Thy cliffs and foliaged vales ; with mournful sound
The rivers flow ; in silent dignity
The mountains stand ; and all the pleasant fields
Share in the sorrow of the Austral sun,
As disappointment, nay, revulsion reigns.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Since 1860 N.S.W. has derived £50,046,052 from
the sale of public lands. Asa matter of very ordinary
honesty, the money, being- the proceeds of the sale of
assets, should have been put into other assets ; and if
this had been done, and non-productive works—such as
roads, culverts, and public buildings—had been paid for
out of revenue, as they should have been, N.S.W. would
have had all the public works it now possesses, and its
debt, instead of being- £53,(162,432 (0r£80,242,246 exclusive
of alleged value of works, &c., transferred to the Common-
wealth), would have amounted to only £15,440,040. In
i t ion'oo'o < S’, ’ n ,^ e P asfc 45 years N.S.W. has raised
£130,288,808 by tile sale of assets or by loans, and all it
has to show for it in the way of “ permanent and repro-
works” are undertakings that have cost
±05,486,692; and even those don’t earn full interest, not
to mention sinking fund.
The total amount of capital which N.S. Wales
has fooled away in 45 years has been £64,802,206. Most
of this is now represented by nothing whatever ; it has
been used to pay the policeman and to provide for public
drunks that are long- since forgotten. A little of it has
been put into public building-s, and roads, and culverts,
but that is a mere fleabite. The vast bulk of it
just upon £60,000,000 has been collared and
improperly used as revenue. The borrowing mad-
ness has been such a blastiferous thing that N. S.
Wales has never attempted to honestly face the
consequences ; it has dodged them, and still continues to
i dodge them, by selling its assets and paying Cohen with
the proceeds. The interest on the N.S.W. public debt
now amounts to £2,856,872 a year, and on temporary
loans to another £194,145. On the average of the past 10
years, £1,150,000 of this has been raised annually by selling
assets. Tlie debt is growing, the interest is growing, and the
assets are shrinking ; and the time is coming when, even
if borrowing- absolutely ceases now, another £1,150,000
will have to be raised in taxes to pay the interest ’bill
for want of assets to sell. If borrowing doesn’t cease
and the money is put into things which don’t
return interest and sinking- fund (and there is practically
no public undertakings which do), then something more
than £1,150,00G of extra taxes will have.to be raised when
the land assets are all sold. N.S. Wales has never yet
felt the full results of its foreign debt, because it has so
tar had assets to sell, with the proceeds of which it has
paid from one-half to one-third of the interest # but the
fact that it has now to buy back estates in order tb find
land for settlementproves that it is getting near the end
of its- tether, and that the assets it can sell are peterin°-
out. And when they do peter out for good, and N.S.
Wales has to collect another £4 or so per household
annually in taxation, and provide absolutely nothing in
return for it, then the State will probably begin to see
the real beauties of the borrowing system.
Though its public debt per bead is not the greatest
among the States, N.S.W. is in this other respect the worst.
offender, so a change to honesty (and it will have to be
honest some day when it has no more assets left to be
dishonest with) will give it,a worse bump than any other
State. This list shows for 1904 the interest charges
payable by each State, and the proportion of it dishonestly
paid out of the sale of assets
Proportion of
Land Interest Paid
■ Interest. Sales. Out of Assets.
N.S. Wales .. £2,841,368 £1,116,061 39-3 per cent
Victoria.. .. 1,885,953 278,554 14 -8
