So N.S.W. shows up as the boss loafer of the group ; as the
conspicuous example of shiftlessness, selfishness, mean-
ness, and financial incapacity ; as the State which, above
all others, is living- on its capital, and doing- all those
things which inevitably lead to bankruptcy, and generally
result in the bankrupt being refused his certificate. And
Carruthers, the Reform Treasurer, has not reformed this
practice worth a cent ; he goes on piling- up debt, and
selling land, and using the proceeds in the same dishonest
way, and generally hurrying on the time when the whole
business will have to stop with a smash, and a dazed
people will simply have to put their hands in their pockets
and pay up—or default.
TV * *
Employers’ liability laws have given accident
insurance business a long lift during the past few years ;
and there has been big money in it for the offices that
have been on the spot with their books open, and have
had the data to work on. But it appears to have been
easy enough to make mistakes. The big Ocean Cos.,
going into the guarantee business under the new English
Employers’ Liability Act (and this business is first cousin
to accident insurance), made a loss of £49,936 in 19D2.
Then it altered its rates, f mad out what business wasn’t
worth taking at any price, and soon got into profits again.
In Australasia the New Zealand Accident Cos. has been by
far the most successful. It began with a paid-up capital
of £2OOO ; but little by little profits have been capitalised
until capital now stands at £62,500 ; and the net earnings
last year ran to £33,816. This energetic co.’s recent
record of profit has been as follows :
1895 .. £6,118 1900 .. £9,082
1896 ... 7,286 . 1901 .. 15,465
1897 .. 8,080 1902 .. 15,385
1898 .. 12,210 1903 .. 27 674
1899 .. '11,898 1904 .. . 33,816
The co. says that it is all on account of its fine organisa-
tion ; and there is little doubt that there isn’t another
branch of insurance business wherein experience and
organisation and the constant accumulation of data count
for more. The accident insurance co. has its rates and
tariffs,-of course ; but they are subject to constant modi-
fications to meet special circumstances. Men working for
one stevedore, for instance, can get accident cover
at a rate which an office wouldn’t look at
if offered by the men working for another stevedore,
handling the same sort of cargo in the same port. And
only experience can separate one stevedore from another.
But, allowing for the “pull” that a few years spent in
the business gives, other insurance co.’s have lately been
hankering after this flesh-pot, and don’t see any reason
Why they shouldn’t make money that way, too. The two
big Maoriland co.’s are especially keen in the matter.
The Maoriland Government has an accident insurance
department, which last year showed a profit of £5418 ;
and as the M.L. Insurance Department has now gone into
the fire business, and thus trodden heavily on the co.s’
corns, the co. ’s are on the look-out for ways to return
the-tread. Therefore, at its last annual meeting, a few
weeks ago, the South British 00. altered its rules so as to
allow it to take any risks except life ; and now the bio-
New Zealand Insurance Cos. is absorbing the N.Z. Accident
Insurance Cos., or amalgamating- with it, or somethin<>-
of the sort,- with a view to pushing matters. And on the
Australian side one or two of the sprightly fire and
marine offices have been looking in the direction of the
accident and guarantee business, and will probably make
announcements on the subject before long. All this
activity is a good thing, anyhow. It is preposterous that
a man of some means should quite as a matter of course
insure his house and his ship and his horse and his
furniture and his plate-glass window, while the laborer
whose labor is the only thing standing between his familv
and destitution, should go through life with his whole
capital uninsured.
* * *
Last week’s cable, reporting that the New
York Insurance Department had determined the surplus
of the Equitable Life office at the reduced amount of
62,000,000 dollars (£13,250,000), does not say the last word
on the question. There is a more elaborate inquiry
proceeding—not conducted by politicians, but by experts
appointed by Congress. As, however, the Insurance
Department is practically on its trial as well as the
Equitable management (for there are admittedly things
Which the department should have found out and didn’t)
no doubt the present departmental investigation has been
carried through with special care. At December 31st last
the co. showed a surplus of £16,582,672. The depart-
ment cuts this down, as shown above, to £13,250,000. But
the lower figure has been arrived at by absolutely writing
off “ advances to agents,” amounting to nearly £1,250,000.
This seems to show that the overhaul has been pretty-
thorough, for, though it can’t be said for certain how
much of these advances are good, part of them no doubt
is a reasonably safe asset. Under the system adopted
by some of the American offices, districts are farmed out
to agents, who employ sub-agents, and these advances are
for the purpose of financing the principal agents, and are
made practically against future renewal premiums—
which, of course, may or may not come in, but most
likely will. That this whole item has been wiped
off the balance - sheet appears to show that
everything in the shape of doubtful assets has been
got rid of, so far as the department can see; and
there still remains £13,250,000 in excess of the amount
necessary to cover policy obligations. Thoug’b, in view
of the disclosures, American offices in Australia will have
such a hard row to hoe that they are likely to blunt their
tongues trying- to hoe it, the result of the investigations
will be balm to the troubled souls who already hold these
foreign policies and for the last few months have been in
doubt as to what they should do about them.
The policy of farming out certain business ex-
plains how it is that some favored officers of these
American insurance concerns have been able to make
such prodigious commissions. In one case—the worst so
far dug up, in connection with the Mutual Life of New
York the officer undertook to organise the foreig-n busi-
ness on a commission basis. Either he organised it so
well, or so much of it was waiting to be done without
anything special in the way of organisation, that thfe
business ran into millions, where only tens of thousands
were expected ; and the commission ran up to corres-
pond.

The Bulletin has received the following letter :
“ In your issue of the 11th May last commenting on the
Glissan v. Crowley case as the Full Court of this State
left it, you stated inter alia , ‘all the same the City
Mutual should have a disinfecting as rapidly as possible
and consult policy-holders as to future management.’
“ The obvious intention of the paragraph was, if I read
it rightly, to comment adversely on the principle of pay-
ing commision to directors on life assurance business
introduced by them (it is hardly necessary for me to say
that I do not agree with The'Bulletin’s views on this
matter), but it has been interpreted as a reflection by you
on the general administration of the affairs of this 'insti-
tution.
“ The affairs of a life assurance society are, and I think
wisely so, always open to the closest investigation and
scrutiny, but I cannot believe that The Bulletin deli-
berately intended to reflect on the administration of the
affairs of the City Mutual, and I would like your endorse-
ment of my belief.
“Yours faithfully,
r _ . “Geo. Crowley, Manager.”
[The Bulletin wished, on May 11, to make no reflec-
tions on the general administration of the affairs of the
City Mutual, the management of which has, at various
times, elicited its praise, for the good interest earnings,
the reduction of expense rate, and other items. The
adverse comment was confined to the point that commis-
sion should not be paid to directors on business intro-
duced by them. In that matter its opinion is unchanged,
and it hopes one day to find Mr. Crowley in agreement
with it.—Ed. Bulletin.]
* * * *
The biggest business-end of the English trip of
general-manager Dibbs, of the Commercial Bank of Syd-
ney, failed, and he had to return without fixing’ up any-
thing definite with the Bank of Australasia in reg’ard to
exchange business. The banks used to make a pot of
money out of this when they had an ag-reement as to
rates ; then Dalgety’s and others cut in, and the Bank of
Australasia broke away in order to knock the “out-
siders ” out, and foreign exchange business is now hardly
worth bothering about.

This bank continues its remarkably prosperous career ;
but there is always at the back of the glittering figures
the possible skeletons of worked-out mines, deserted
mining towns, and depreciated or valueless securities.
Probably no bank in Australia calls for more prudent
management, and the figures don’t help one much to
judge in that matter ; ;for one can’t tell whether bad and
doubtful debts are being written down hard as the bank
goes along. The increased dividend leaves about 40 per
cent: of the profits to go to reserves to provide for the
probable slump ; and, though that is rather better than
the Australian average, it certainly isn’t too much in the
case of a bank which has all its eggs in one basket, the
bottom of which is liable to fall out. Certainly the
profits for the past half-year are charged with £3350 bonus
to officers, and as that isn’t a recurring expense the situa-
tion is a trifle better than the figures indicate. The
return shown by'profits does not suggest on the surface
any over-valuation of securities at present. On the basis
of capital (now £125,000) and reserves, the earnings have
recently moved in this way :
March, 1902 £lO 9 1 p.c. March, 1904 £ll 0 5p c
Sept,., 1902 9 1 3,, Sept., 1904 11 69 ! ’
March, 1903 10 15 6 „ March, 1905 9 7 9
Sept., 1903 fl 14 0 „ Sept., 1905 94 9 ”
Among Australian banks only the Bank of Australasia
shows up better than this. But the Westralian conditions
are still abnormal, though they are becoming less so, as
these further comparisons show :

The very large proportion of deposits not bearing interest
has been a striking feature of the accounts; and the
bank didn’t allow all this money to lie idle—l 2 months
ago, in addition to the whole'of its capital, reserves,
tiTicl fixed deposits, the b&nk htid out in cidvances over
£250,000 of the public’s free money—a highly profitable
business. But during the year, whereas free deposits
have remained almost stationary, deposits on which in-
terest has now to be paid have" increased £326,766, and
advances have increased only £158,729, which means a
good deal less profitable business. As Westralian con-
ditions settle down, this movement may be expected to
proceed. The immediate result is a large increase in
specie and bullion (£1,114,764 as against £835,838), so that
the liquid assets now represent as much as 54 p.c. of total
liabilities to the public. But here again the figures can’t
be judged by ordinary standards ; for a bank which has
still so large "a proportion of current accounts couldn’t
maintain an ordinary 30 p.c. or so of liquid assets and be
safe.

The year’s distribution is represented as an 8 per cent,
div. on £IOO,OOO and a 4 per cent, bonus ; it is put that
way possibly to indicate that the directors are very doubt-
ful about the wisdom of it; and the doubt does them

doubt will disappear, and the directors will realise that
the co. has achieved its remarkable progress by the extra-
liberal additions it has made to reserves. There’s a vast
amount of Australian business yet to be won by Aus-
tralian co.’s, and almost the surest way to command it is
to give insurers the guarantee of a strapping reserve fund.
Even now, certainly, the year’s additions are handsome—
about 54 per cent, of profits—but the co.’s fine progress
during the last few years was partly the result of doing
much better than even that. As in the case with most
Australian offices, the unusually favorable experience of the
previous year in the matter of losses has not been repeated.

In 1900, though the expenses were but 337 p.c., only
14-8 per cent, of the premiums were profit ; in the past
year, with an expense rate up to 40’8 per cent., 14*2 per
cent, of premiums were profit; so that there is nothing-
much to complain of here. The assets include £70,000 in
public stocks (at cost), £55,000 on fixed deposit, and
£43,956 of real estate (book value); and the investments
have averaged £3 16s. 3d. per cent., which is reckoning in
as interest all income except from premiums, though it
certainly includes some fees. It still therefore looks as
though either not enough is charged to expenses for the
rent of such premises as are used for offices, or that some
of the assets could stand a little writing down.

This co. has the lumping habit badly ; and there are not
many concerns in which the habit is worse than it is in
connection with a trustee co. You can watch a bank or
a brewery, but tfie trustee only begins to work for you
when you are located where you can’t watch it—
not at any rate with the eye of the flesh. Therefore,
a trustee co. ought to take more pains than usual
to let the public know exactly what sort of a co. it is,
especially what the guarantees in the shape of assets are
really worth ; and that can’t be dohe until interest-
earnings are separated from commissibns. All that can
be said very definitely in this case is that the.paid capital
is £30,000; against that there is £IO,OOO in 3 per cent.
Government stock, £12,728 of mortgages, £3761 of
advances, and, in addition-to a few small items, £3355
bank deposit receipts “atcost, less amounts written off
and dividends received.” The last item should be written
down to its present value. Apparently most of the
writing-down done during the past few years, and shown,
has been in respect of advances against beneficiaries’ in-
terests, and they don’t now amount to much. The ex-
penses five years ago were 59 per cent, of gross income ;
they now amount to 56 per cent. In these five years the
profits have exceeded the divs. by £4623, which should
have added about £2OB a year to income ; and as the
income has increased by £915 (from £4147 to £5062), there
appears to have been a substantial increase in commis-
sions. On capital and reserves the profits show a return
of £7 4s. per cent. The £1 shares carry a liability of £4.

That is to say, after paying dividend tax (£3750) and
allowing for depreciation (£9021), besides spending
£12,589 in prospecting, the co.- has made a profit of
£189,214, on the capital of £1,200,000, or at the rate of
over 31 per cent, per annum. Of the reserves (and also
of the profits) £SOOO is, however, of a doubtful sort ;
it consists of the' nucleus of an insurance fund which the
co. has decided to establish in order to do its own insur-
ing. In ordinary circumstances, this (or some) amount
would have been paid to insurance co.’s and profits would
have been that much less ; as it is, there is a contingent
liability against it. The co., whose outlook was very
gloomy just after the amalgamation,, has made an extra-
ordinary recovery. The improved copper-market has, of
course, been a big factor. In the co.’s first half-year the
metal averaged £57 per ton ; in the past half-year, £67
9s. 3d. The copper output was 4253 tons ; so the rise has
been worth £44,600 to the co. in the half-year; and the
rise in silver has been worth nearly £7OOO. All expenses
have been paid by the silver (365,4400 z.), and the gold
(12,4250 z.), and a contribution of £23 per ton of copper ;
in other words, assuming the present output, costs, and
price of silver to continue, Mt. Lyell’could pay its way
with copper at £23 per ton. All over that (£44 9s. 9d.
per ton for the past half-year, to be exact) has been profit.
When Mt. Lyell Cos. was reformed in August,
1903, £140,000 of debentures were issued in place of North
Lyell’s previous London issue. Under the trust df-ed
these debentures have to be repurchased,; North Lyell
bad the same bond, but defaulted in its last half-year.
The amalgamated co. has, however, been mailing good
progress at clearing things out of the way. Like this :
Debentures Retired Special

The special reserve shown above, which is not included
in the reserv.es referred to in the table, is the result of re-
purchases at a discount. The current half-year should
see the debentures reduced to half their original size—in
two years and a half from the start. It isn’t many
mining co.’s that have the luck to do that. But, speak-
ing generally, the “ reserves ” of mining co.’s are a snare.
Mt. Lyell last half-year rooted out 213,809 tons of ore, and
since August, 1903, has treated 844,031 tons, and the mine
is the poorer by that much ; yet it still stands in the
accounts at £851,862, which was the original cost to this
present co. And it will no doubt stand at the same*
figure till the last payable ton of stuff is gouged out, and
the £851,862 is all moonshine. When that happens the
co. will require to nominally have £851,862 of reserves
before it really has any reserves at all.

From the £114,340 of profit there has been written off
plant, &c., £9659, leaving- £104,681 net. The accounts
don’t look like those of six months ago. A comparison
of liquid assets and liabilities at the two dates reads like
this:—

And meantime a £15,000 dividend has been paid. But
for most of it the sales of accumulated zinc “ tailings ”
and other by-products have been responsible. So far as
current work is concerned, comparatively little progress
has been made. Costs for ore-raising and milling have
been 15s. o"7d. per ton against 15s. the previous
half-year. But that is not a fair way of putting it. “ Ore
raising ” doesn’t include management, or development, or
“general mine work,” or “mine maintenance”; in fact,
apart from ore-raising and milling, the expenses, exclusive
of depreciation, run into 3s. 4d. per ton, with another sd.
per ton for outside expenses. This total of 18s. 4d. per
ton is a few pence worse than that of the South and other
cheaply worked mines ; but it is good. That it shows no
improvement on the previous half-year’s figures is due to
a larger expenditure on work outside mere ore-raising.
The mill-work shows an improvement on what the old
rattle-trap did in its last days ; but the expected improve-
ment upon the early work of the new mill isn’t discover-
able. While this new mill was running in the previous
half-year the recovery was 37 "29 per cent, of the silver
and 65T8 per cent, of the lead ; the recovery in the past
half-year was 36’96 per cent, of the silver and 65T2per
cent, of the lead—which are poor figures. Block 10,
which is getting its pre from deeper levels than any other
Broken Hill mine, has no doubt a tough proposition in
hand. But while this, in view of results, is some excuse
for the management, it isn’t anything in favor of the mine.
Block 10’s ordinary profits for the past half-year,
taking no account of depreciation, were at the rate of
£5 6s. 2d. per ton of the lead in the concentrates. Lead
averaged £l3 Bs. during the half-year; so if silver
remains steady, lead could fall to about £8 Is. lOd. before
the co. would be out of pocket, except for wear and tear
—on the basis, that is, of last half-year’s work, which
should be capable of a good deal of improvement.
Besides the residues which have been sold, the co. has put
520,000 tons of tailings under offer for £126,750 net. If
this deal comes off it will bring up the total surplus of
liquid assets over liabilities to £236,394, or £2 7s. per
share. But, even if the deal goes through, the last of the
money will not be available till 1911.

Broken Hill Block 14 figures for six months :
Profit from current operations £7,155
Bevenue from sales of accumulated zinc tailings 16,757
Total credit, working account .. .. £23,912
Less management expenses .. .. .. 1,491
Net profit £22,421
From this £619 is to be deducted for depreciation. A
very good result for a mine that had been worked to such
a standstill that six months ago the workings were full of
water. But as to what the mine can do in the future the
figures are positively no guide. To raise funds, it was
decided to root out the carbonates ; there is little of this
ore left in the mine, but by pursuing it with great energy
2860 tons were sent away ; and w'hile it only cost 14s. s|d.
per ton to root out, it realised more than 12,000
tons of the mine’s sulphides would have done. The
current half-year there may be a fair output of carbonate;
after that, only odd patches. With metals at present
prices co. would be foolish not to rush this ore out. The
lead (sulphide) mill was only in regular working order for
a week or two of the half-year ; but the zinc works were
going as regularly as an erratic supply of acid and a
plentiful supply of other troubles would allow 7 . And as
the carbonates can’t last, while the lead mill will do better
work, and the future of the zinc plant is doubtful, the
past half-year’s figures don’t give a line as to the future.
The underground developments in the mine have been
very satisfactory, however.
* •* * <
One of the difficulties that are liable to he
bumped against in connection with zinc-treatment has
been bumped against already by Block 14. During the
last three months the tailings were “ hardly suitable for
the acid method ” (the Zinc Corporation is going to use
the acid method), “being full of carbonate; and this
largely neutralised certain improvements effected in the
manner of working.”
* * * *
During this year market value of Broken Hill
mines has jumped from £3,750,000 to £5,810,000. The
improved lead and silver markets have been a factor ; the
investor, as distinguished from the gambler, will, how-
, ever, note that those markets are now 7 much above their
average. But the big deals in zinc material have made
most of the difference. Well, look at that matter with a
cold, unprejudiced eye. The Sulphide Corporation has
been making zinc concentrates for about three years. It
has paid ; but ordinary shareholders in the Sulphide Cor-
poration, who are very hungry for the divs. they haven’t
handled, know well enough that there hasn’t been a mint
of money in the business. Then the Australian Metal Cos.
has been a buyer of zinc tailings for about six years
past, and Block 10 has sold it tens of thousands of tons.
Yet some Block 10 shareholders have been acting as
though quite anew source of wealth had been opened up.
There wasn’t any boom w 7 hen both the Australian Metal
Cos. and the Sulphide Corp. proved that there was some
money in the “ tailings” ; it only arrived w'hen the Zinc
Corporation made some fairly big deals, and got options
for some much bigger ones. This Zinc Corporation
has, however, done absolutely nothing on an industrial
scale. The boom takes it for granted that it can’t fail.
But it can. Ten years ago the Sulphide Corporation
(Ashcroft’s Process), Limited., was going to do just as
great things. There wasn’t any trouble about getting off
a co. with a subscribed capital of £962,500. The thing was
so certain—the tests had been so conclusive—that £550,000
was paid for the patent rights. Then difficulties began
to crop up, and they went on cropping up, until the zinc-
production part of the business was absolutely dropped,
and £95,165 was written off the valueless zinc works. And
the Zinc Corporation doesn’t consist of shrewder men
than were behind the Sulphide Corporation. This assump-
tion that the Broken Hill zinc problem is settled, and
that there are millions in it (the market has jumped over
£2,000,000), is to a great extent premature. Certainly,
so far as some of the co.’s are concerned, they have made
their money—the Zinc Corporation has. taken the risk.
But the deals, actually completed, don’t by any means
run into millions. „ .

opened up 500 ft. long, 400 ft. deep ; average width,l4ft.
Estimate 300,000 tons in sight; also 100,000 tons probably
in addition. Average assays 22J p.c. lead, 12g p.c. zinc,
3Joz. silver. Ore can be concentrated easily ; extraction
of at least 70 p.c. of the lead, and 50 p.c. of the silver.
Cost of working, all included, 16s. per ton. Estimate
returning charges, £4 10s. Profit per ton with lead at
£l4 equal to 12s. ; with lead at £l2, 6s. Cos. can
also make a profit of ss. per ton by working
the zinc tailings, or can be sold at 3s. per
ton. Mine has great prospective value.” Shareholders in
the co.’s that have just sold their zinc tailings to Hoover’s
co. will be delighted to learn that they could have made
66 per cent, more by treating' the dumps themselves. No
opinion can be expressed about the estimate of ore
reserves ; Hertzig and Hoover had better means of esti-
mating than anybody else. But those of costs are
certainly low. The South, next door, with its vast ore
bodies, can’t work at less than 14s. 7|d. per ton for
mining and milling alone, and that isn’t counting in wear
and tear (very heavy in a concentrating mill), manage-
ment, and the cost of the new main shaft not charged to
working account. Block 10’s costs, “all included,” are
18s. lOd. plus depreciation. It is impossible to see how
the much-smaller South Blocks, which isn’t built for very
cheap working, can get down to 165., “ all included.” Re-
turning charges, estimated at £4 10s., have been
£1 higher than that, and are likely to be
higher again. On the other hand, the extraction
ought to g'o above 70 and 50 percent, respectively. Taking
8., M., and Co.’s averages, and that extraction, there is,
with lead at £l2 and silver at 2s. 6d., 425. worth of re-
coverable metal in the ore. Costs would be for mining
and milling 165., which is too little probably, and about
15s. returning charge (taking about six tons of ore to one
of concentrates); total 315.; leaving a margin of 11s.
The estimated profit of 6s. is therefore moderate—if the.
stuff will averag'e the 22J per cent, of lead stated. The
South ore only averages I'6£ per cent., though, and a
reduction of every unit in the South Blocks ore means
about Is. Bd. less profit per ton. If the estimate
crumples up, it will be mostly at these two points—costs
and lead value. But, taking Hertzig and Hoover’s figures
as they stand, there is £165,000 of profit in the mine with
lead at £l3; every £1 fall knocks off about £48,000 —
which is another risk in the gamble.

From a man whom The Bulletin has found
reliable :—“ C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), already marked down
as one of the biggest mineral developments of the year,
hasn’t so far had too many remarks made as to the
quality or quantity of its ore bodies. VVriter, who followed
the early developments in the Broken Hill mines, is
satisfied, after inspecting the C.S. A., that, in the sulphide
zone, there is going to be almost a similar deposit, pro-
bably both as large and as rich as the Broken Hill
sulphides. So far as .development has gone in the oxidised
ore, results are good in value right through an eastern
crosscut at the 450 ft. level. For a distance of 90ft., the
crosscut shows a practically continuous silver-lead gossan
lode, with carbonate of lead and crystals ; also the face of
the crosscut (that is, 90ft. east from the shaft) is still in
good grade ore, and with no indications of nearing the
hanging wall. In the western crosscut, which is 30ft.
in, values are low ; but as the country here and there
carries splashes of carbonates, chances are that, though
it would appear to be approaching the footwall, more lode
matter may intervene before that is reached.
“On the surface the O.S.A’s. outcrop is
imposing, and is traceable through the property for a
distance of 1000 ft. As the strike of the lode is 10 degrees
west of north —and assuming that it lives for any distance
—Gardner’s blocks should catch it first; further on south,
the Central and South C.S.A. take in all the country w’ith
reasonable chances. That is, reasonable enough for the
outside public to take a risk on.
“On the north end, in O.S.A’s. own property,
mneral leases 10 and 14 look like a fine pup, struggling
V.. Number 10 is right on the strike of
w. ; <;.nw*~;lst thy~, vition, confined inside 40 acres, for
good business. °°r
“Flotation of Trewenack’s blocks north of
C.S.A. intoaco. of 2000 shares at £lO went easily, and
just doubled previous float of Central Blocks, which went
in 1000 shares of £lO. Trewenack, however, didn’t put
all his eggs in one basket, as another block (No. 80) and
Doyle’s, which are within six chains on the west and 20
chains on the north of the C.S.A., are held in reserve.
It is now stated that these blocks go together into a big
float on the Adelaide and Victorian markets ; and as
they certainly occupy the only area on the north which
at the moment would appear to have a good gambling
chance, Trewenack’s career as a floater is not over yet.
“Of the 230 acres occupied by the C.S.A.
proper, only 60 acres can be reckoned on line of lode ;
and as the combs of the country at the deepest workings
are almost vertical, probably with an eastern dip of one
foot in eight, writer can’t see anything but the one pup
north worth rearing. Of the western outcrop, which is
charted some 15 chains west of the main lode, little or
nothing can be said until some development has been
done. There always is the possibility of parallel bodies ;
if there are any values there, O.S.A. mineral lease 11 may
also do something as well as M.L’s. 4 (where the shaft is
and 10.
“Entering into the question of development, a
10,000 yd. dam on C.S.A. property gets early considera-
tion from the management. A million and a half-gallon
storage capacity, together with condensed water from
mine shaft, should tide the co. over the initial stage of
proving the mine. Meanwhile a north drive in the lead
ore at the present level has been decided upon, and a con-
tract entered with the Goldfields Diamond Drilling Cos. for
1000 ft. to 1500 ft. of boring, in holes 300 ft. each into the
sulphides.”
* * * *
Perseverance Gardner’s shaft, which is roughly
15 chains south from the C.S.A. shaft (Cobar, N.S.W.), is
now down 207 ft. in promising gossan; and prospects of
catching the C.S.A. lode are bright. The upper part
of the lode is leached, and there is no telling what
differences there may be in the level at which this stops.
An eastern crosscut might shed some light on the matter.
Meanwhile work is slow. Gardner’s blocks are ear-marked
for a strong Bull co.
* * * *
Queen Bee, Cobar (N.S.W.), as a result of the
visit of John Fox (late manager of Cobar Gold Mines) to
the Cold Country, is likely to see changes. Understood
that Fox offers £50,000 cash, and 50,000 shares in a co.
not to exceed £250,000. Fox, of course, requires inspec-
tion of the mine, and, if satisfied, is prepared to pay
£2OOO cash for a three months’ working option.
* * * *
Two promising shows of silver-lead gossan ores,
one at Budgery, Hermidale, and the other on Restdown,
Boppy Mountain (N.S.W.), have been lately discovered.
Budgery is said to look particularly promising, carrying
gold, silver, and lead, and, being on the railway line
between Nyngan and Cobar, will just now command
attention.

It had almost been forgotten that H. B. Coles took
Mt. Drysuale (N.S.W.) to London to float, but a cable from
Coles says that Bull is sending an expert to look into the
Drysdale rich pockets, and if he’s satisfied they’re there,
or that the mine has still places where they probably will
be later on, London will become interested.

“A report from the manager of Coppabella
Block No. 1 (N.S.W.) states that a solid body has been
driven through 21ft. without the wall being found. Rich
galena ore has also been struck. At the Boulder at 115 ft.
in the tunnel, the lode has been struck; it is mineralised
and vertical.” All of which is so beautifully vague as to
be worth nothing. That solid 21ft. body—what is it
made of ? Has it been sampled ? What does it assay ?
Is it “ mineralised and vertical,” like the Boulder, with
the tunnel driven through buck quartz ? And how much
verticalness will it take to make a dividend?
* * * *
At Neild’s, Wyalong (N.S.W.), the second
biggest concern on the field, an old trouble has revived.
The other day miners employed were asked to work till
12 o clock on Saturday night instead of 11.30, as hitherto :
men again refused to yield up a time-honored custom,
and the owners knocked off one of the shifts. This
naturally hasn t improved matters, and relations are very
strained. The Golden Fleece Cos. is opening out on two
iair-si z ed reefs at the 800 ft., and has excellent prospects
ahead. A parcel of 40 tons yielded 91 oz. gold. True
Blue is making good progress with the erection of
machinery for condensers and rock drills.
* * • •
Associated, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), is in the midst
of many improvements. The surface equipment is being
renovated beyond recognition, and is expected to be com-
pleted by end of year. A clear run is anticipated in
January, or at latest in February, when 10,000 to 11,000
tons will be treated monthly. After that the mine will
rank as one of the biggest producers and div.-payers of
the Golden Mile. The incline winze on the Oroya shoot
at the 1000 ft. level is down 300 ft. and has still 100 ft. to go
to connect with the incline rise from the 1200 ft. This
work will be completed by end of November, and will
ventilate the workings at 1000 ft. Reason so little has
been heard of the Oroya shoot lately is that the
air is so bad that no prospecting is being done.
This winze will alter that. Some very rich telluride is
being broken from the Oroya East lode in the winze below
the 1000 ft. level. A truck-load from there contained
nothing but “ specimens.” The lode on the west boun-
dary, the best part of which was found to be in Persever-
ance ground, is being followed on its strike south-east,
and the continuation of the telluride shoot may yet be
found in Associated ground. At 1400 ft. the Australia
East lode isr being stoped out in a block 20ft. wide by 280 ft.
long, worth 18dwt. ; this is the best stope in the mine. An
enormous quantity of 8 to lodwt. ore is left in the top
levels, and can be broken out at a nominal cost. It is
safe to say the Associated is looking better than at any
previous time in its history, and will give a good account
of itself next year.
« * * *
Associated Northern, Kalgoorlie, supposed to
be absolutely done months ago, is now estimated to have
89,400 tons of 22'37dwt. ore in sight. This estimate is
made by manager G. M. Roberts, who takes no chances.
Cos., for months past, has been earning a profit of £II,OOO
monthly, and will continue doing so for at least two
years.
* * * *
Northern Blocks Syndicate (W.A.) is prac-
tically ready to start crushing on its new property, the
Great Leviathan, Southern Cross. Trouble is that the
late Government refused to supply water from the gold-
fields scheme unless co. paid £IBOO for the pipe line, guar-
anteed to consume a minimum quantity of water, arid
agreed to pay ss. per lOOOgal. Cos. offered to lay the line
itself at a cost not exceeding £I2OO, and to take as much
water as it required, some 30,000 to 40,000ga1. a month,
but objected to pay 50 per cent, more for the main than
it was worth, or to guarantee to pay for more water
than was used. But for this dispute some 100 men
would have been employed. The difficulty will, how-
ever, be settled now, and the mill will probably start
in December. The reef has been opened up at 60ft.
and 130 ft., and is stronger though poorer at the latter
level. A test crushing showed 16dwt., and the 10-head
mill is expected to treat 1500 tons of this grade monthly.
There is enough stone in sight to recoup the co. the
£12,000 to £15,000 expended on purchase, equipment,
&c. A main vertical shaft is being sunk, and will be con-
nected with the present 130 ft. level, which will be the
real first level of the mine. No more development will
be done till a connection is made, and the mill starts.
* * • #
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, is opening up remarkably.
Ore reserves are now estimated at 205,000 tons, worth
9dwt. This is above the 750 ft. level, on which very
little work has been done so far. At present rate of con-
sumption this means four years’ supply. Already mill
has treated 100,000 tons ; and this surely justifies an exr
tension of plant. It was mooted by directors some
time ago. Manager Moss vetoed the proposal for the
time being, but may change his mind now.
» * * •
Perseverance, Kalgoorlie, is crosscutting at
1100 ft. for the new lode recently found in its ground by
the Associated. This lode is at least 20ft. wide, and
worth 2oz. to the ton where penetrated. How much
wider it is is not known, as the Associated stopped work
when it found it was trespassing. The crosscut should
reach the lode easily this month, and may make a big
difference to the mine’s prospects.
• * * *
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), is very sick.
As long ago pointed out, all mines in the district, such
as De Baun’s Mulwarrie and Speakman’s Mt. Callion,
petered out at about 300 ft., and Pole is doing the same.
For the past three months there has been great difficulty
in keeping the mill going, and “cleaning of boilers,”
“ breakdowns of machinery ” and other excuses have
hinted at the truth. The fact is there is not enough
stone to keep the mill going. Nat Harper, the ex-
manager, has had a lot of hard luck. He has in succession
steered Frasers (Southern Cross), Robinson’s and White
Feather Main Reefs (Ilanowna), and Golden Pole (Davy-
hurst). All made a splash ; all came to grief. By the way,
Richard Hamilton, of Great Boulder, during the first week
in October inspected the mine, and, though he didn’t
boom it as much as some bulls tried to make out, yet he
certainly gave shares a fillip, and went into figures that
now won’t stand inspection.
• * * •
Queensland Menzies (W.A.), after many warn-
ings, has at last closed down. Every effort was made to
locate new pay shoots, but fruitlessly. Cos. has taken
over the Good enough, at one time under option to
Boulder Hamilton. Goodenough has produced 750 oz.
from 3,900 tons, but, like all Menzies mines, is erratic.
Still, as Manager Fergusson has thought it worth a trial,
there must be something in it, as he knows the district
well.
* * * *
Lancefield (W. A.) has discharged most of its
men, as management is in a quandary over the treatment
of its tailings. Ore reserves were valued at 345, but
September’s return only panned out at 17s. 4d., and
showed a loss of £I6OO. This mine was run by the origi-
nal prospectors under Strauss, and paid monthly divs. of
£360. At that time two-thirds of the gold came from the
residues, as it does still, and yet 8., M. and Cos. are under-
stood to say the residues are incapable of treatment.
The real fact seems to be that the mine was over-valued.
• • • *
Mack’s Creek, Kenington, and Ten Mile com-
prise a little nest of villages up Jamieson way (Vic.),
which blossomed out as a mining district over the Star of
the West boomlet. There was the original show of that
name, then the Extended, Champion Blue Ribbon, Rule
Britannia, and three or four other claims which fit as
nicely as can be into a descending scale of merit. While
it can be said of the Star of the West that it was
a lucky hit, as shareholders only had to put up
Is. 5Jd. to get on to the div. list, none of the “ pups ”
are dogs of the same breed. Thus while parent co. paid
7s. in divs. during the half-year, other claims were on or
about the call-list. Champion boasted of a big forma-
tion, with gold here, there, and everywhere. Yet, now
the battery is at work, shares don’t jig as they would if
the div. era was in sight. Still there may be a
surprise in store for the waiting crowd to galva-
nise both bulls and bears. Fate of the field hangs
largely on the Star of the West. There good stone
worked in No. 1 and 2 tunnels seems to have pinched out,
and operations lower down seem to be a very “blue”
spec. The Extended always was, and is, a gamble.
**< * ■
The Victorian Coal Mines Inquiry Commission,
investigating the conditions of labor arid the profits, etc.,
of the Gippsland mines, is getting what it was understood
to be appointed for—surprising information. So far as
directors and managers have been examined they seem to
be full of a vast mass of want of knowledge. “ You had
better ask the manager as to that,” or “ Perhaps the
directors could inform you,” or “That is a question for
the engineer to answer ” were the usual replies. Still the
Commission managed to drag out of Manager Nicholson,
of the Outtrim-Howitt Cos., the admission that before the
'O3 lock-out 700 men were employed as against 270 at pre-
sent, and that while, during the seven years prior to ’O3,
dividends were paid averaging 34 per cent, per annum, no
dividends have been paid since. Yet the public was
assured during the trouble that the miners were making •
such high wages that the companies could not carry on ;
also, when the difficulty was temporarily got over by the
aid of blacklegs, the same simple public was assured
that the companies would get along nicely without the
“ strikers,” and that “ plenty of good miners were offer-
ing.” On the other side, competent miners swore that
the conditions of getting out coal were so obsolete and
unsatisfactory that they could not make a living thereat;
the frequent handling of the coal reduced so much to
“ slack ” that they received payment for only half what
they won. One miner characterised the system at
Outtrim as the “ finest scheme of roguery he had seen
carried on.”

Hunt’s Extended, Gaffney’s Creek (Yic.), after
preparing to sink a new shaft in the basin of the dyke,
jibbed, and started to again fool money away driving
on spec. The usual result followed, and the mine is now
oh tribute.
* # * *
Great Northern Extended Consols, Rutherglen
(Vic.), after a long struggle with water and the trouble
caused by the burst which took place a few years ago, has
pretty well got from under the cloud, and should soon be
on the div. list. But Wilkinson, the manager, who has
brought the mine through its troubles, is not to get the
kudos of paying the first dividend ; D. H. Browne, of
Charlotte Plains and Spring Hill and Central Leads, and
his son, have succeeded Wilkinson as manager and assis-
tant manager respectively. In the face of a 3d. call,
shareholders are asking whether it’s more economical to
have two managers than one.
* * * *
Brisbane Stock Exchange, which, in a dull call,
doesn’t get beyond recording- the sale of 100 Lady Jane
Extenders at Is. ljd., grew angry last week because it
received no news concerning the cutting of the lode at
the O.K. While the Exchange was interrogating John
Newell, chairman of directors, and Newell was replying
that he had no knowledge on the subject, shares moved up
from 13s. 6d. to 17s. 6d. That 4s. rise broke Brisbane’s heart.
Later on another director, usually pretty handy to the
mine, wired still another director, who was in Sydney,
that the lode had been cut and assayed 18 per cent,
copper. The incident has made Brisbane Exchange people
more decided than ever that Brisbane is the proper place
for the co.’s office. In fact, much of the fuss is being
made with a single eye to that change.
* * * •
Willcocks’ Cloncurry syndicate has not yet quite
decided to erect smelters on the field, although the press
of N.Q. is sure upon the point. A “ competent man
Alan Gibb, no doubt—is first to report upon the pro-
posal. A wise step. Although it is popularly supposed,
in view ' of the Richmond railway extension (Clon-
curry-wards), that smelting on the field is bound
to be a success, such an issue is by no
means certain. After completion of the railway exten-
sion, many of the good copper deposits will still be over
100 miles from the railway line, and 100 miles cannot be
traversed by coke teams before breakfast. Cloncurry is a
big, scattered field. 0.K., with only half that distance to
traverse, is practically beaten by the transport difficulty,
and nothing but a railway will extricate it from the worst
of the mess.
* * * *
Ravenswood (N.Q.) blew the froth off a lot of
shandy at the end of last week when the Deeps struck an
18-inch vein going 3oz. gold to the ton. Considering the
importance of the strike to the many anxious investors,
not only in the Ravenswood Deep mines, but in other
properties, there was probably some justification for the
display of bunting and the drunk. To sink 1520 ft.
and get nothing, after expecting to tap the Sunset
reef at less than 1300 ft., did not deter Laurence Wilson ;
and as soon as John Bull had lent the show some money
he put out a crosscut over 300 ft., and now everything is
happiness and sore heads. It will be bad luck if the vein,
when opened on, proves a rotter, as was the case with
each of the lodes passed through in the main shaft.
Laurence Wilson, not to mention anyone else, deserves
better than that—he is such a plucky gambler. With a
drop of luck in the Deeps and his tube mills both working,
this “boss of Ravenswood” will make his field popular
again.
* # * •
It takes a battler to stick to a way-back district
like Cloncurry (N.Q.). The men who have been raising
copper-ore there lately have had poor hopes of getting the
stuff to market till perhaps March. There is now a 50-
mile dry stage on the road, and cartage is practically
stopped ; they are talking of camels. A load of ore, got
ready at Hampden in July, is still on the track ; and, as
it has the dry stage ahead of it, it will be some months
yet before it is delivered. On crude ore going 35 per cent,
copper, freight to market has been running away with
£l6 per ton of metal, and still more, of course, for lower-
grade ore. On top of that, some miners have had to pack
water as much as 15 miles to their camps.
* * * *
Bennion’s No. 2 shaft, on which Croydon (N.Q.)
is keeping its eye glued, is proving a disappointment. It
was put down with the idea of dodging the water that
flooded out No. 1 shaft; and though it has dodged the
heaviest flow of water, it seems also to have dodged the
reef, and either got into the intrusive bar or has the bar
between it and the No. 1 shaft. The plan originally
recommended, of fighting the water in No. 1, is now being
adopted.

“ M.C.” : Proposed to start pig-iron smelting at
Aldershot (Q.), where coal can be had at a low price. It
is said that ore averaging over 60 per cent, of iron, and
suited to the cheap acid process of furnace treatment, is
also available ; such ore would be immensely more econo-
mical to handle than comparatively poor ore, say 50 per
cent., containing phosphorus and sulphur, requiring the
more costly basic treatment. Iron smelting has been so
elaborated and specialised of late years that it should be-
come a special study for Australian students, and
encouragement and facilities to learn the nature of our
iron ore deposits should be given by State Governments.
Writer could name half-a-dozen deposits of iron ore near
railways in various States, but could not say which was
the richest ore and which could be the most cheaply
mined and smelted, simply because such knowledge
requires an elaborate study of the details of the business ;
and where are the means for the study ?
W * * 9
Distant peaks look golden. So with Lake
Jukes (Tas.), away beyond Mt. Owen, where the pros-
pector has been hunting for a second Mt. Lyell, and only
finding a copper-stained rock as green as the turf at its
base. Thither, in the wake of the prospector, went some
of the old North Lyell crowd, and thither seems to have
followed some of the old North Lyell luck. Bull cash has
helped the show on, and Bull optimism looks like having
its usual fate; A circular, stated to have been published
by the London Board, tells of “ a rich shoot,” of many
experiments made to find out the simple matter whether
ore is refractory or not, and of assays continuously giving
high values both in copper and gold. And it also stated
that, “ at the outset,” it was proposed to treat payable
gold-bearing ore only. How the shade of James Crotty
must have gasped when it read this 1 Why should
anything but p- yable ore be treated at any
time? Anyway, the battery return reported in
October was 130 tons for 90z., the official report
notifying that the ore was average-grade rock carrying
copper as bornite. Such a God-forsaken region as Mt.
Jukes demands something more tangible than under
2dwt. dirt to keep it alive as a mining region. If there is
payable dirt, why wasn’t it heaved under the stamps ?
Concentration to recover lost gold and copper is stated
not to have been commenced. No wonder ; and if the
copper contents are not of higher worth than the gold,
its best place is in the dump, to be forgotten.
« • * •
On the basis of market valuation of £1,800,000
Mount Lyell mine does not wash out so satisfactorily as
the balance-sheet. Give the co. credit for realisable
assets worth £600,000. That is 10s. a share. What is
there beyond this? To find out, the North Lyell ore
reserves have to be examined. The pressure for ore is
shown by the withdrawals from the 600 ft. and 700 ft.
levels. Nos. 1 and 2 levels are exhausted, No. 3 (which
was one of the main sources of supply during the
half-year) is almost exhausted, while above No. 4 the
main stopes are almost up to the No. 3 level. Therefore it
rests with the 500, 600, and the newly opened-up 700 ft.
levels to furnish the ore forthe future ; and it is admitted
that the 500 was not a “bonser." Sticht’s estimate,
made in March, 1904, was 185,000 tons to 500 ft. Look at
the work he has done since in the North Lyell mine
Ore broken.
Tons.
Sept., 1904.. 53,530
Mar., 1905 63,104
Sept., 1905.. .. .. .. 55,444
The rapidity with which stoping was begun at the 600 ft.
level in the March half-year, and at the 700 ft. during last
term, gives point to the story told by the above figures.
Therefore, while Sticht has certainly got ore well below
700 ft., the extent proved, so far, has only very partially
compensated for the inroads made on reserves during the
past three terms. So the splendid balance-sheet has to
be looked at very critically until it can be shown that
the shortage of the supplies in the North Lyell mine has
been overcome. No share movement ought to be allowed
to hide the fact.
* * * *
When a mine gets out of debt, when it is well
managed and dividends are ahead, it is usual to look for
a market rise. Briseis (Tas.) provides all these essentials,
but the shares don’t gee. Why is it ? The property lias
profits assured, tin is about £SO higher than it was ever
expected to go, and developments at the mine up the
lead are giving an increased area of drift. The first cause
of the refusal of stock to budge is that those who were
ever likely to buy into the co. did so long since, many
at the boom price. These don’t want to hold
but to get, for they cannot quite brush from their
memory Currie’s estimate, nor do they forget the
story told by the Cascade flood. Then there Is a big
bundle of speculators who hold piles of shares for the
market rise which is so slow in coming; and, then, the
public is very shy about nursing a baby in a 600,000 share
co. The addition of a prominent speculative broker to
the London directorate not only didn’t help to make the
stock popular, but it led to a good few realisations.
Slowness of the co. in getting up to the 100 tons-a-month
output is another cause, and there is still doubt about the
get-away for the tailings. And behind it all is the feeling
that the mine won’t pay more than £1 per share in divs.
on 600,000 shares. None the less, the portent is that the
payment of the first div. will be the occasion for a bull
move in the market: but whether the public will respond
is quite another matter.
A 12 inch Suction Dredging Plant is being built
for the Weldbrook Tin Mines (Jo. The plant will be
similar in design to those erected at the Pioneer and
Briseis Extended Tin Mines (Tasmania). The engines will
be of the vertical compound condensing type of about
300h.p. fitted with an Edwards type of air pump and
large surface condenser. The boilers will be two in
number, of the marine type, and will work at a pressure
of 1501 b. to the square inch. The nozzle pump will be
centrifugal and directly connected to a vertical compound
steam engine. The barge will be 45ft. square. The
Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, which
is constructing this dredge, has been particularly success-
ful in building these large type of suction dredges and
owing to their reputation have just concluded a contract
for the erection of a plant in the Federated Malay States
which promises to be the fore-runner of many more of the
like.*

It is of great importance to those interested in
mining to understar,d that the judgment in the recent
action of the Wilfley Co., does not deprive the industry
of the latest important development in concentrators.
The Card concentrates on an entirely opposite principle
to the Wilfley. In the latter the dressing takes place on
the smooth surface at the ends of the diagonal riffles
which is claimed by Wilfley as the essence of his invention.
Whilst in the Card the dressing takes place in the
continuous channels and not oh the surface of the table,
hence much greater capacity, higher concentrates and
poorer tailings. The injunction applies merely to two
experimental tops which were never sold by the licensees,
N. Guthridge Limited, who are continuing the manu-
facture and sale of the Card.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be serious]}' con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect, coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New .Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe: works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, holts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Aboriginalities.
WARNED OFF.
TBIMBATH: “ Hi, boss ! there's a bloke bin an’ fell down the shaft.”
MANAGER : “ Confound the fellow ! Couldn't he read ? ”
Janor : As so many aboriginaliars have
been snake-bitten, I would like to say (just to take
on that lonely feeling that one has who has not
been bitten) that I was once nearly bitten by half
a snake. Was splitting out mining laths in white
box country. They were big trees, mostly hollow
that is, the hearts had been destroyed by ter-
mites and replaced by earth. Consequently they
were bad sawing, and it was quite a usual occur-
rence tor the saw to jam when half way through a
m ysaw “ fitchered ” so one evening,
grabbed the axe, chopped saw out, and knocked
ott. .Next morning, saw the middle portion of a
torge black snake showing through the aperture.
He was lying between the wood and the core of
earth. A nick with the axe divided the reptile,
one half disappeared smartly. T’other half’
which I diagnosed as his latter end, remained
stationary, and as it was in the way of the cross-
cut I seized it and pulled. Then he came so sud-
denly that I almost overbalanced. Also I dis-
covered that I had hold of the better half, and as
struck for me I let go and spoilt his aim with a
lett hook. He missed my arm, and fastened on to
the rolled-up shirt sleeve. Grasping the half-
calamity behind the ears I slung him to Gehenna.
It was the quickest round of a life time.
“ Dave H.R.” : It is a prominent feature
of closer settlement that the breed of sheep is fast
improving. This year, whilst engaged in coaxing
our great natural resources marketwards, I have
had personal proof of the fact; and have also
heard it much mentioned by wool-classers and the
mdividual who washes the object pursued by
Jason. Said one of the latter: “The cocky is
beginning to realise that it pays him much better
to have 800 or 900 or 1000 well-bred sheep, with
heavy, strong fleeces, than 2000 straggly animals
that rarely cut 61b. apiece. Also with small pad-
docks, and consequently less roaming about on
the part of the animal through burrs and
herbage, the wool is freer from seed and other
foreign substances than station fleece.” A wool-
classer told me that with closer settlement on a
large scale, and the consequent small paddocks,
the breed of sheep must inevitably improve, and
he considers that ten years of closer settlement
would double the value of Australia’s annual
wool-clip. Also, as the railway throws out its
tentacles the fat stock business increases. Indeed,
not long since there was quite a glut in the mar-
ket-many cookies being this year within reach of
the fast and cheap circumstance who were last
year beyond its pale. Notably is this the case in
the matter of the extension of the Narrabri-Wal-
gett railway to Oryon. By this extension much
wool has helped to benefit the Railway Depart-
ment, which would otherwise have fallen to the
share of steamer-owners. There is nothing like a
railway to induce closer settlement, if the land
laws allow people to settle; and nothing like
closer settlement to send a country forward —with
a decent tariff to provide a manufacturing popula-
tion as a market.
“H.S.R.”: After all the talk of the
kookaburra, no one has mentioned this peculiarity.
He will die of starvation in the midst of plenty,
for unlike the magpie, crow, butcher-bird, and
others, he will not feed off carcases of sheep or
cattle. Still, if you cut pieces off and throw them
about he will swoop down and pick them up.
Perhaps in drought time he is merely saying
“Mutton, mutton everywhere, and not a bit to
eat” when he is supposed to be laughing.
Another thing I have not seen mentioned is that
the kangaroo rat destroys thousands of young
thistles, and that since he has been almost exter-
minated they have increased. The kangaroo rat
does little harm to the sheep-farmers that I
can see, but he does no good to the agricul-
turist, as he is addicted to bandieooting spuds
and other fruit, and is a good hand at driving
under fences, and probably in this way makes
roads for the festive dingo and rabbit.

“Da wine ” : Prohibi-
tion is in the air, and the
Cold-tea cat has her tail ele-
vated with all the fur abloom.
Now, I’ve had a fair experi-
ence on both sides of the
bar, 12 years with sleeper-
axe, crosscut, adze, bar, and
shovel, and nine years a
publican ; and to-day, look-
ing back across the broad
vista, with its variegated
tinting of beer and perspira-
tion, I fail to see why there
should be so much hysterical
solicitude concerning Aus-
tralia’s thirst. Australia’s
drinking (mostly shandygaff)
is largely a question of
environment. The bush-
worker spends his working-
time boundary-riding, sleep-
er-cutting, fencing, ringbark-
ing, timber-getting, grub-
bing, tank-sinking, etc., etc.,
leading a life that is almost
repulsive in its monotony.
When he takes a day off he,
not having any beautiful
harbors, Art galleries or
National Parks, slips on his
“tailor-made” (young Aus-
tralia, in the bush or out of
it, likes his bit of cloth cut
neat), and, to use his own
expression, “pokes over to
the pub.” Not because “his
hide is cracking for a spree ”
or that he is even thirsty,
but it’s the one oasis in a
desert of sombre monotony
and—well, what would you
do ? At the pub he meets
and makes his acquaint-

“Janor”: During the recent floods in
Cippsland I had an object lesson on the strong
aversion of the native bear to water. Came across
Mr. Koala one morning marooned on a dead white
gum which stood on a small flat submerged to the
depth of four inches by still back-water. There
was not a toothful of greenstuff on the tree, but
for eight successive mornings the tailless one
perched on his hungry throne and made plaintive
remarks to the waste of dampness around.
On the ninth morning the “ b’ar ” had vamoosed-
the flat was dry.

A CRUSHED PARENT.

A wail from Gippsland :
A few days to November, and bhroug'h the cracks in the
roof on to my rough table comes a steady “drip-drip.”
Up the road wander the sulky-looking cows, horning each
other as they go, the calves following dejectedly. In the
paddock opposite two mares and a colt turn hanging wet
tails to the icy blast as they root for tufts of grass, biting
and kicking on the smallest provocation. A couple of
swallows roost disconsolately on a rafter, and round my
door the hungry magpies gather. The creek is running
fast and chocolate-colored ; a little while and the muddy
snow-waters will pour from hill and gully, and it will be
a banker. The wind shrieks and every now and then I
hear a dull “ clunk ” as the heavy snow drops from the
roof. Round the blazing logs the men gather gloomy,
unsociable and unwashed. “ Won’t be a damned lamb
left alive,” they say. “ Shearin’ ?—there’ll be no damned
shearin’ if this goes on. Been on this place man an’ boy
this 30 year an’ never seen such a winter.” Along the
road comes a horseman galloping. We hope he has the
mail. Instead he tells us, “Poor Tom chucked a sixer
last night—‘pumonia’—an’ the police is out lookin’ for
the coach. Think it’s washed away. Lines down every-
where.” Through close on a foot of snow I make
my way to the nearest paddocks to have a look
at the sheep. Not a “woolly” in sight, but as I
go, I stumble across the new-born lambs. Around their
little bodies the snow has melted a bit, making dark
patches in the whiteness. Most of them are eyeless—the
crows, at any rate, have broken their fast. Up a hill-side
I climb that, two days since, was gay with creamy
clematis and purple sarsaparilla, and on across the sheeted
home paddock, where the dandelions and earl} 7 buttercups
and violets had said so plainly “ Spring has come.” A
few red-breasts dart to and fro, making bright patches of
color ; but where are the parrots, wood-pigeons, black
magpies and sparrow-hawks that, a week since, built so
busily? The glare has almost blinded me, and brought
on something extra-special in headaches, so I make my
way back to the hut to await the thaw, just in time to
hear the cheerful idiot remark, “Fine weather for ducks.”

Dr. T. F. Macdonald, of Geraldton (Q.)
Bureau of Tropical Diseases, concerning a White
Australia :
The geographical distribution of dark-skinned people
showed that the origin of color was not due to the tropics.
The blackest color was usually possessed by the most
primitive people ; and, by the action of time, color had,
in certain nations, slowly dissolved. Experience had
shown that white people could rapidly adapt themselves
to change of climate. He had seen white gangs working in
sugar fields, and they were healthier than colored gangs
engaged in similar conditions. The question arose : “ Can
the sugar industry profitably employ white men, at white
men’s wages?” His answer was in the affirmative. If
the Australian people were determined that the industry
should be carried on by Europeans, it was only a question
of what price sugar should be in the market. Would our
people be prepared to pay a little more for their sugar?
borne form of Protection to sugar would, however, be
necessary, as it would be absurd to expel the Kanaka
from Queensland and at the same time allow sugar to
come into Australia which had been grown under similar
circumstances to those objected to. Eastern people, if
they came here, would lower the standard of wages,
would interfere with national principles and the develop-
ment of Australian democracy, and therefore Australia
should remain white.

“ L. Dick ” : Some years ago was camped
on the Herbert River (West Queensland), and
came across a big nigger carrying a billy. I sang
out, “Hello, Buckley ! What you bin ketchum?”
He lifted the lid and disclosed a nice brown snake
mixed up with a fine goanna, a brace of rats, a
bird, a lizard, a frog, and beneath all these luxuries
a lot of big white grubs dug out of trees. Saw
same nigger, some hours later, fast asleep under a
tree, looking enlarged and very happy.

“David Richards”: Be the proposal of
the Cocky Carruthers Government to protect the
native cat or the domestic feline “which has be
come wild,” with the idea that it is on the road
to the solution of the N.S.W. rabbit problem—it
is a hass. I have known this method tried by
settlers. The feline will eat a few young rabbits,
but only a very few. After a short while they
fraternise and intermarry. There is far too much
food in the line of young birds, mice and rats, &c.,
in the bush, and will be for years «">« to ,
allow of any prospect of cats
bit. What is wanted is— popidatit>iC %.*-liy'hs *»-■'
that we hear very little of the ravages of bunny
amongst the small forty-acre men of Victoria ?
The country there is just as favorable to the culti-
vation of rabbit as any part of N.S W. And why do
we never hear complaints of bunny from England,
where there are no phosphorus carts, and no
wire-net* ing regulations ? The proposal of Car-
ruthers to make friends with the cat is ridiculous
in the extreme. There is only one way of coping
with the rabbit, and Australia wants to get up
and roar out till it is done—a graduated land-
tax that will burst up the big estates; good
legislation that will encourage close settlement (not
Peter Close settlement) ; and a decent tariff that
will cause a tide of desirable immigrants to make
a big bound for these shores, till the country has
so many people that they will eat the rabbit, or
put him m a can and sell him for good yellow
money to someone else who hasn’t the luck to
have any rabbit, and with the money build a gun
to shoot the person if ever he comes here to take
the rabbit without paying for him,

“Hexham” : “ A.D.” (B. 2/11/’O5) asks why
young fruit-trees in the bush are free from pests.
All pests travel —insects with wings, fungi through
spores carried about by the wind —and the bush
intercepts these before they can reach the fruit-
tree. The phylloxera vine disease spread through-
out the south of Erance in two or three years, the
vineyards being nearly contiguous and having no
great forests to protect them. In the county of
Cumberland, where there is so much hush, though
the disease was discovered at Liverpool a dozen
years ago, it took four or five years to get to Canley
Vale but three miles off, and only last year
appeared at Smithfield another three miles further
on. Re various pars on “old man,” or southern
wood, as a remedy for peach aphis. May as
well read the burial service over them. Peach
aphides can only be got rid of hy poison,
and it must be a poison that kills when it touches
them, such as nicotine, kerosene, etc. Have tried
horrihle-smelling mixtures suchastheChowsusefor
cabbage pests, and found them utterly worthless
for peach aphis, which, unlike cabbage aphis, is a
permanent resident on the tree, and can be found
there during the hardest frosts if searched for
carefully. Local orchardists generally spray with
infusion of tobacco-stalks, but this only kills the
live insect. The eggs hatch out the first fine day,
and the spraying has to be continually repeated.
The aphis-eating ladybird is a fraud. It does not
show up till the end of September, when the
aphis has been at work three months, and then it
would need ten times the average crop of lady,
birds to do any real good.

THE WOOLLY SHEEP.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My gentle friend, dear, golden-headed Tot,
Walks out one wet, warm morning in the Spring
To show me this, her last, her new delight;
And I—l am prepared for anything.
The big, bright eyes are proud. It is the pride
Of sweet possession ; all her face is full.
I see before me in his long, soft clothes
A woolly sheep, delightful in his wool.
He skates around sedately through the room,
And loud his praise the babbling cherub sings ;
My eyes go wandering out the open door ;
I think of woolly sheep and many things.
I see our thirsty, sunburnt continent ;
The wilderness where sluggish rivers creep,
They bear away thy burdens to the sea ;
The land is all for thee, O woolly sheep !
For thee we grapple with the undergrowth ;
For thee we spoil the glory of the trees.
All bloom must perish on thy heritage—
Begone, O singing birds and humming bees !
We store the trickling waters for thy sake ;
We tap the bubbling rivers swift and deep,
A thousand feet below all bright and cool— ■
’Tis all because of thee, O woolly sheep !
Thy paths are in the far back-stretching scrub
Where rain scarce falls and grasses never grow;
Strange shrubs and trees will give thee sustenance ;
Out, and still out, thy farthest heralds go.
We strip thee of thy treasures everywhere ;
By dead dry plains and by the river side.
The grim black ranges and the grave, grey heath—
These, too, are thine—our land is warm and wide.
But one sad thought is with us—we who toil,
And moil, and suffer, nor one prize can keep—
They make us clothes from rags across the sea ;
Thy wool is not for us, O woolly sheep I
Our flocks are out upon a thousand hills ;
Our statesmen screech and all our trumpets blow,
And greedily the big ships take from us
The best and brightest wool the world can grow.
For all the millions in the smoke and mist
The white flesh must be beautiful and cheap—
As soft as flowers in spring-time tenderness—
Gold are thy little lambs, O woolly sheep 1
VIC,
SHAW NEILSON.

“J.D.”: Recently, near Almaden, North
Queensland, I hired a darkie and his gin to find a
sugar-bag, and they proceeded as follows : The
nigger stripped to the waist-belt; the gin, by a
sudden and adroit movement —as A. Ward would
say—transposed her skirt into a pair of running
pants. Then they proceeded to a waterhole,
having first selected several pieces of down with
points from the dilly-bag. A great number of
bees were around the water, and one was easily
caught by the simple process of squirting a mouth-
ful of water over him. The pointed down was
quickly attached, and the surplus water blown
from the unfortunate insect. Then he was tossed
in the air and the chase began. The nigger ran
with his eyes fixed on the bee, whilst the gin
watched her boy’s footsteps. The reason for these
proceedings was apparent, for when the next
stride would have carried the buck into a small
tree-trunk, the gin gave a soft “Youp.” Then
the buck strode higher than usual and the obstacle
was negotiated. And so the race continued with
a constant succession of “ youps ” something after
this fashion : “ Youp, YOUP, youp, YOUP,
youp, youp,” and so on. The louder the gin yelled
the higher the nigger jumped. But Nemesis was
waiting on this colored brother. The flight of the
weighted insect led him directly towards a fallen
tree-head. As they approached, the gin was
measuring it with her eye. Finally, she
appeared to conclude that the “tchillan” I
had promised could not be earned without
some little risk, and she chanced it. A stride
from the obstacle she gave a “youp” that
curdled my blood. The nigger shot into the air
as though driven by a catapult, caught his foot in
one of the higher branches, and came a most awful
cropper. I rushed round the head of the tree and
found his lordship sitting up, with his waist-belt
in his hand, and remarking, to no one in particu-
lar, “ o—le —gose ! ’Possum-skin all broke.” So
was his own skin in a number of places, but this
did not seem to trouble him.

“D.H.R.”: One still remaining conse-
quence of the protracted drought is that fish life
in many of the N.S.W. out-back streams is at a
very low ebb. The Oastlereagh, which was at
one time quite noted for its abundance of fish, and
was the resort and living of individuals with vans
and lines and nets —the pernicious net —is now
almost destitute of anything with a fin ; and even
the shrimp has mostly gone to the Halls of Jasper
and other celestial spots. In the Barwon too, not
to mention the Namoi, Mooni, and numerous
other creeks and streamlets where fish was once
very abundant, it is now a novelty to catch one.
The scarcity has become even more pronounced
since the drought broke, and it is to be greatly
feared that unless a long close season is imposed
the fish will become altogether extinct.

“Ed.” : Stepping on to a snake is a mild
thrill compared with the horror of having a
goanna run up one’s body.
When disturbed, the loath-
some, clinging brutes, with
their sharp claws, will run
up whatever happens to be
nearest and looks like a tree
or a tree-stump. If the most
upright object in the neigh-
borhood chances to be
human, that human will get
a thrill which will last a
lifetime. The only way to
get quit of the brute is to
lie down and roll; it leaves
then for something upright
and stationary. Goannas
eat anything—beetles, car-
cases of animals in all stages,
eggs, moths, young and old
birds, frogs, snakes, lizards—
nothing comes amiss. Their
bite is one to be remem-
bered ; they close their jaws
with a snap, and hold until
the piece of flesh comes out.
Dogs bitten by them almost
invariably bleed to death ;
if it is a simple bite, with-
out tearing the flesh, mor-
tification, caused by the
poisonous, decaying food
particles from their teeth,
often sets in. The goanna
takes life easily. He feeds
well in the summer, puts on
plenty of fat until autumn,
and when he is well lined
and the weather gets
cold, he creeps into a
hollow tree and sleeps and
smells until early summer.

“ Cripper ” : Horrible is the appetite of
the lunatic of a peculiar senile type. Stones,
sticks, buttons, anything in fact that fit into the
mouth go down. A Mahometan in the Ballarat
(Yic.) Asylum, some few years ago, on being
opened up after death delivered up some of the
asylum’s strayed cutlery. Four forks, three
knives, a couple of large spoons, and a multitude
of buttons and unconsidered trifles, all of which
the doctor said had nothing to do with causing
death. A man I once had under observation
would chew up anything within reach. I noticed
blood about his mouth, and on sponging the same
away could not discover any wound or open sore.
I watched him closely but stealthily, and nearly
made a break for the clear outside world when I
found him jabbing his teeth into his forearm, to
which he hung with the tenacity of a starved wolf
when interrupted.

No other lo-

with the very re-

as if he really desired to learn some-
thing. He held a defunct milker’s tail while he put a few
questions about poisoned pollard ; then dropped the
source of his inspiration, kicked the dead cow’s stomach
professionally, noted the absence of lucerne distension
symptoms, and looked puzzled. Then he looked up his
pocket-book for inspiration, put another set of questions
to the lamenting cocky and a score of his neighbors
hanging round, anxious to know if an epidemic was pro-
mised. Queries proved resultless. At this point one of
the cockies said that he’d recently noticed a small weed
with a pucey-green, yallery-purple flower, such as he’d
never seen growing around before. Then all the cookies
remembered seeing this same flower, and had worn it in
their buttonholes to church, but it had only been around
since Socialism started. They fell over one another in
trying to find a specimen, but without result; which, of
course, proved the dead kine had eaten it out of exist-
ence, hence their demise. The expert thereupon looked
wiser than ever, and told the cocky to move his remain-
ing cows to another paddock (which had already been
done), and impressed upon him the efficacy of Epsom
salts when the beast’s tails waggled limp. Then he bade
adieu sadly. No more stock died in that quarter, and
the matter promised to be forgotten, when lo ! four
weeks later the daily papers came out with columns of
balderdash, telling how a mysterious disease had broken
out in the Oockadoodledoo district, that it was baffling
the experts, that it was unlike the measles, but some-
thing like the mumps, only not so much so, with a symp-
tom here and there of empty-decanteritis, and the rest of
it, the wise summing up of the whole matter being that
the disease was a new one, and appeared indigenous to
the country ; hence nothing could be done, except that
Epsom salts and telegraph-forms be kept handy in case of
another outbreak !

“ J.C.” : No Maori feast or Maori tangi
is complete without the quaint ceremony of the
tuku-kai, the “bringing of the food.” Up from
the cooking-ovens at the rear of the camp jog a
number of girls and youths in a long line, each
carrying a flax basket of pork, potatoes, fish, etc.
—roast rib of white man is “off” nowadays.
They advance towards the expectant guests with
a swaggering gait, a sort of “ Here-we-come-
gathering-nuts-and-may ” aspect, chanting a lively
song, the black-eyed damsels wriggling like
Nautch girls. The occasional intermixture of
English phrases with Maori chants is amusing.
For instance, here is a certain tuku-kai song I
heard a gang of young bloods at Rotorua bawl
out lustily as they danced into the meeting-square,
laden with hard ship-biscuits, their contribution
to the feast: —
Maori Lingo. Translation.
He alia te pihikete 1 What are the biscuits for ?
PihikeU } The biscuits ?
Hei whakapiri hi to taha o Why, to stick close along-
te kau. side the beef !
r, i. „ j, j, . (imitating the ante-mortem,
Ooh-ooh-ooh 1 bellow of the “ beef ”).
What-oh she pump !
And at the word “pump” each cheery coon
bumped his quota down beside the boiled beef
and the spuds with a thud energetic enough to
shatter the most adamant of “ hard tack.”
