Significant message from the Land of the
Great Drought by W. G. Stuart Russell, of Yel-
verton, to Queensland Weather Bureau :
Words in common use fail to describe the hideous pro-
cess of desiccation now being endured. Great tracts of
thousands of square miles are utterly waterless. The
Oloncurry-Camooweal mail passes to-day for the last
time, and residents here must beat a retreat for personal
safety to Camooweal, 50 miles west. The outlook for
domestic animals left behind is hopeless.
If only half the enterprise and energy wasted in
the Far West had been spent on the coast, what a
difference there would be in the position of affairs
to day !
“Vigilans”: The other day I struck a
buffalo shooter’s camp in the Northern Territory.
A splendid specimen of the white race, assisted
by two Mongolians, six niggers and their lubras,
did the shooting. The white man had spent most
of his 5G years in the N.T., with sundry trips to
Asia. He was in great nick and as hard as nails.
Lived on damper, buffalo and post-and-rail tea,
with an occasional tot of rum. Said he: “The
N.T. is all right for Europeans. Climate is
healthy, and fever only a bogey. The harder you
work the better you are; it’s only drones that kick
up a row about the climate. Hope to die in the
Territory.” The daily bag averaged 10 beasts.
Horses are perfectly trained ; will wear a beast
down and run right up alongside for you to shoot.
They seem to have the sport; the man has the
business.
# • • *
“Timbarra”: Passing through Wanga-
ratta (Vic.), the other week, I saw a Hindu
hawker anchoring his turnout in front of a store.
First he passed the reins through the front off
wheel and tied them round the lamp-bracket.
Then he chain-locked the wheels on both sides. I
thought this was all, but he next produced two
pairs of hobbles and coupled up the fore and hind
legs of his remarkably sleepy-looking gee-gee.
After this he backed off and critically surveyed
the fastenings, as if in doubt. I suggested that
he should get a length of rope, fix one end round
a large gum tree that stood in front of the store,
and tie the other end all round the cart. He said
that my idea was a brilliant one, but he had no
rope; then he remarked in Hindu that I was the
father of a burnt pig.

“ Hamer ” : The weird things one sees in
delirium are generally not absolute creations of
the imagination, but creative imaginations built
on a derangement of the optic faculties. Thus I
have seen a man imagining that a spider perched
in a distant corner was a grotesque monster
straddled over him binding him with ropes—his
upset focus brought the thing right on top of him.
Was out with a party, one of whom, in a state of
“ recovery,” suddenly focussed some ants wrongly
and yelled to us to save ourselves from “the
monsters,” while he cleared over the ground and
banged himself against things. Posts or hangings
on a bed or spots on the coverlet become converted
into snakes and spiders by a man with the horrors,
whilst in plain delirium, without any trimmings,
equally weird fancies will build themselves on
nothing in particular. I have imagined a pillow
left on the bed was George Reid sitting on top of
me.
* * * *
“Janor”: On the outskirts of a Vic.
mining town I came across two Australian kiddies
playing at mining the other day. They were
sinking a small shaft on a quartz leader in soft
sandstone, and were down about 14ft. Had a
whip stick rigged, and a large wether goat on the
job as whip-horse. As I neared the shaft the top-
man, or rather boy, sent down the hide bucket and
called out to his mate below to send up his blunts
and come up for crib. Just then he overbalanced
and flew down the shaft. The clatter alarmed the
goat, which bolted, ran the bucket up to the
pulley with a bang that broke the rope, and dis-
appeared in a cloud of dust. The bucket bumped
down the shaft on to the nippers. I took the
place of the recently-departed goat and rescued
the entombed miners. They were very much hurt
—at the behavior of their whip-horse, but
undamaged otherwise, and I left them despatching
a huge pasty and swearing vengeance on William.

THE SORROWS OF SATAN.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Sir, —
I have the honor, by command
Of his Satanic Majesty (D.G.),
To beg- that you will publish in your land
This protest ’gainst some things that should not be
His Lowness having culled and sorted well
Types of all damned—each family and genus
(Showing thereby, though you and I in Hell
Appear alike, there may be much between us)
Around the walls, in order, these exhibits
Arranged like writhing bugs on pins impaled,
A notice, in asbestos paint, prohibits
Sticks and umbrellas, and a space is railed.
Dear Sir, you will pronounce it, when you see ’em,
A grand collection. ’Twas the work of years.
His Lowness really thought that his museum
Was quite complete ; but now, Sir, he has fears.
The other day, the boatman brought a load in—
Assorted souls, some from your sunny land
(Each one had his own special trade-mark blowed in
The bottle, and each had his proper brand).
You sent a soul, Sir (kindly try and trace him),
A flat and florid perishable line.
With “ Boodles, U.5.A.,” we fain would place him,
But for his bill of lading and his whine.
Now, Sir, I’ll ask you kindly to imagine us
With all our wall-space full, our lists complete,
Encumbered with a damned soul oleaginous,
A buttered soul, Sir, in this dreadful heat.
And worse, by the last mail, we have received
Advices which would really make one wish
The mail contract was broken, for I’m grieved
To learn the next lot will be Stinking Fish.
Now, Sir, His Lowness orders me to mention,
Though novelty may have a certain charm,
And doubtless you are full of good intention,
The threatened cargo fills us with alarm.
The temperature down here is high at present
(I write this note in somewhat of a hurry),
And Fish, I fear, is bound to be unpleasant.
Yours truly, Blank Blank, Under-Secretary.
vio.

Justice Owen, of N.S.W., is the son of an
army man, who, though an Irishman, com-
manded a Highland regiment. He has a brother
whose surname is Kynaston, the name Owen
being abandoned by royal license to enable him to
assume heirship to the estate of Sir John Kynas-
ton, of Shropshire.

self of more “bulls’’than any other member of
the Victorian Parliament, though at a mixed
metaphor the late J. G. Francis could beat him
hands down. Had O’Loghlen stuck to his Crown
Prosecutorshi p he would have done better finan-
cially than by plunging into the precarious busi-
ness of polities.
J- W- Thomson, who has represented
Clutha m M.L. House of Reps, for 30 years is
retiring from political life at the age of 80
Ihomson, who was a Minister under Grey, is a
scholarly and very amiable old gentleman, who
never did any harm to anybody, but he once got
his dander up and spoke for 24 hours—the longest
speech ever delivered in the Parliament of Seddon
Land.
DILL! DILL! DILL!
Remarked in England that King Ned, when
doing his Continental jaunts, is not attended by a
oec. of State, and frequently has policy inter-
views with other monarehs without the presence
of a responsible Minister, which custom requires.
Apparently there is something in the tale that
King Ned aspires to be a little more than an
affable rubber stamp in the scheme of Govern-
ment.
B. D. Morehead, Lumley Hill, and Oscar
.Ue Satge constituted themselves a third party in
the Queensland Parliament long before the Labor
party was heard o f. The three pledged themselves
to light Griffith on one side and MTlwraith on
the other, and for a little time almost held the
balance of power. But Morehead joined M‘ll-
- and Hill and De Satge quarrelled, and the
third party disappeared.
“ Huffin-Guffin ” : Met Hart, the sacked
ex-president of the Vic. Locomotive Engineers’
Association, the other afternoon. He was navvy-
ing, and he can “keep his end up” at the work
too. From driving a locomotive at 14s. a day to
dittoing a wheelbarrow at 6s. is what may happen
to a man m Victoria for his sins of Unionism,
i et Hart was cheerful, and only spoke sorrow-
fully when referring to the fact that some of his
former mates thought that he and ex-secretary
Scorer were guilty of accepting br'bes in connec-
tion with the termination of the strike. It is a
itij el accusation, and Hart’s present occupation
should clear him from the charge for ever. Scorer
is running a small suburban wood-yard, and does
not get his old fellow workmen’s custom to the
extent he should.

English papers treat the eccentricities of
Tommy Bent exactly as we treat those of out-of-the-
way American celebrities. Reading of Tommy’s
escapades, as presented by the British journalist,
we find that he is regarded very much as a wild
man from Borneo _ or a woolly Texan, whereas
Tommy, after all, is only a bit of a cheerful ass,
and we have known greater asses who had dignity
enough to sit in a congress of bishops.
Benjamin Lee, for many years a softgoods
bush salesman, later a member of N.S.W. Par-
liament, and later still stipendiary magistrate,
was hale and cheery while celebrating his eightieth
birthday recently. He is not quite an Australian
?^r’^* ha ? bee ?. knocking about N.S.W. since
1829. His father died at 91. Pa Lee was a light
dragoon, and helped Wellington to win a number
of battles.
James Norton, father of N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council, is now in his eighty-second year
His father was also an M.L.C., and kept in harness
if anything in the Upper House can be called
harness— till his death at 81. The first of the
family known to Australia was John Norton of
Leatherhead, who dropped into Sydney about
1819 and took firm root. The present James
Norton was appointed to the Council in 1879 and
was once some kind of a Postmaster-General.
The bizarre mixture of Liberalism and
Toryism, which Lord Rosebery presents off and
on as Australian democratic sentiment, is attri-
buted in large part to his association with Dan’l
Cooper, the Australian absentee. Dan’l and
Rosebery went to Oxford together, and later
went on the turf together, and Dan’l is pleased to
be of service to his native land by telling people
what he thinks would be good for it, when he
can do so without telling them what he thinks
would be bad for Dan’l Cooper, absentee.
xr Mj llar d, M.L. A., the unfortunate
. . VV- solicitor and politician whose habit of
mixing whisky and business got him into a foolish
little trouble over a confused item of £5, claimed
at his trial to have won the Victoria Cross at some
back period of his history. But the published
lists of surviving V.C. men dpn’t include his name
so possibly this is a mere delusion. Anyhow the
Cross doesn t always go by merit. Many a man
has deserved it for the battle he put up against
fan tods and blue centipedes, with no weapon in
his hand save an empty bottle, and his bravery
has been quite overlooked.
The wedding at Neutral Bay (Sydney) of
Miss Moseley, daughter of Captain MoSey,
formerly of the 60th Rifles, and now a solicitor in
Sydney, recalls the expedition under Colonel
Barney in the forties to found a new colony in
the north. The vessel with the Colonel and his
staff and some legal officials, was wrecked on an
island- The party which included William
Wnaley Billyard, who had been appointed Crown
Solicitor of the new settlement, included but
°? e , lady* Mrs. Billyard, the grandmother
of the recent bride. She did the cooking,
&a, and was a mother to the wrecked party gene-
raliy On the morning of the rescue she had
jaked her last loaf, and had to enlarge it bv
adding to the last lot of flour the bran from her
pincushion.
Mr. Henry Cary Dangar, ML C a
plutocrat of N.S.W. Leg. Council, recently‘said
that, thirty years ago, Mr. W. C. Wentworth,
the father of the Constitution,” told him (Mr.
Dangar) that, if he had foreseen then what
Responsible Government was going to -bring this
colony to, he would have cut out his tongue before
he said a word m its favor. Wentworth has been
dead over 33 years, anyhow, and it is 43 years
since he was last m Australia. Wentworth was
for self always, and wanted everything cheap.
He and other plutocrats yearned for a resumption
of transportation so that they might have labor
for nothing, barring the rations and a suit of slops
once a year. The Dangars made their money
under similar conditions.

Australian Attorney-General Isaacs, who
is only some 25 years at the Bar, began early to
put his figures up, and receive them too, both
from the Vic. Government and private litigants.
The first time he was brought to Sydney his brief
was marked £5OO, which was so out of proportion
to the fees of the local men retained to act with
him that they refused to go on. The matter was
compromised, but it is understood that Mr. Isaacs
obtained every £ he bargained for.
An Australian Premier walked on a recent
Saturday night from his capital’s Town Hall to
the railway station supported by a Legislative
Councillor and a commercial magnate. Out of an
oyster-shop rushed an hilarious youth who, with a
demon yell, leapt on the beefy back that carries
the weight of Government, and bashed the hat of
the head of the State down on its ears. But it
was so obviously the natural sort of joke to play
on that Premier that nobody took any particular
notice of the incident.
“L.” : Alfred Deakin is a study, as he
wriggles and twists in his place in the House of
Reps. I started to take his record for two
minutes : Sat squarely in his seat and clasped
his fingers over chest. Twirled the thumbs
round and round. Pulled up right trouser-leg.
Tugged at watch-chain with left hand. Turned '
up corner of waistcoat, apparently looking for
a pin. Stuck two fingers between his collar
and his throat. Crossed left leg over right
and rubbed left ankle. Ditto, legs re-
versed. Planted his feet before him and
hugged his knees. Swung his head side-
ways to smile at Page. Bounced two
feet on the seat (apparently without
muscular action) to speak rapidly to John
Forrest. Stuck his thumbs in his arm-
pits, spread his feet—and sprang from
this attitude to step hurriedly from the
chamber, and even then there were 45
seconds of the two minutes to go. On
the opposite side of the room, behold
Knox. What a contrast ! Eyes closed,
hands clasped, knees well apart —no
movement except that of his breath
lifting his bulk gently up and down.
Always the same. No sooner had the
Northern Territory Chow who got on the new
Bynoe Harbor tin-rush made his few thousands,
than he pouched it and sailed off to China to spend
it. And he was naturalised, too. For all the good
it has been to Australia, that tinfield might almost
as well never have been discovered. Yet still they
cry for colored labor.
“ Bill ” : ’Twould be interesting to know
whence All About Australians gleaned its
information concerning the Willis family (B.
26/10/’O5). Nicholas must have been a very old-
fashioned youth when his father, John Willis,
“ went to ’Frisco and died there.” Long after
Nicholas had been returned to Parliament, John
Willis worked in a farrier’s shop in Armidale
(N.S.W.), situated at the corner of Beardy and
Rusden-streets, and entertained the rustics with
marvellous tales anent his son, “ the member.”
Also, Mrs. Willis, mother of the future land-
agent, whom “John left to rear the family,” had
gone over, and John had consoled his lonely life
by marrying the widow Smitheringale, relict of an
old New England pioneer. About eight years ago
John suddenly departed from Armidale, and no
more was heard of him. Government made pro-
vision for the twice-bereaved lady by appointing
her caretaker of the local public offices. Some-
body has been elongating the tibia belonging to
A. A. A.
“Jay” : Ex-Premier Boyd D. Morehead,
of Queensland, recently ' deceased, grew very
testy in his later days. I remember being at a
little suburban station one morning, accompanied
by my dog, when B.D.M. happened along. Dog,
being one of the friendly, lick-you-all-over sort,
capered up and rested a pair of persuasive paws on
Morehead’s waistcoat. The old man spluttered an
imprecation and kicked the dog hard in the i'ibs,
whereupon the dog, taking the kick as part of the
game, playfully seized his trousers. Then they
both got excited and the air became thick with
dog, and grey tweed, and scandalised station-
master, and ex-Premier with murder in his eye.
Disliking such degrading spectacles, I left hur-
riedly for home. A remnant of dog with a scared
expression turned up a little later.
Amid a confusion of reports from the
U.S. it is hard to sort out the story of Mr.
‘‘Friday ” Ellis, of Australia, and his row with
that American poker-player, who recently did a
good deal towards cleaning out the Sydney
National Sporting Club. It seems certain that
there was a quantity of blood scattered about a
house in San Francisco, and that the damage done
to “Friday” Ellis made him a fearsome-looking
pony-owner. One report is that the gentleman
who damaged and robbed “Friday” fled with his
wife and the plunder while his victim was away
showing his injuries to the police. Another is
that, finding he hadn’t killed “Friday,” he killed
his wife and then committed suicide. At all
events, certain Sydney poker and billiard people
don’t much mind who is dead so long as somebody
is dead. Some of them still feel so mad that they
would almost be glad to hear that everybody was
dead.
Seldom do Australians hear a Cheeke now
talked of, though the Judge of that name was
once an important person in Sydney society. The
name re-emerges casually in connection with the
Dailey divorce case, parties to the case and the
Cheekes in London being on visiting terms
Judge Cheeke was a legal adventurer who took
Sydney for his oyster. He got on to the District
Court Bench, and later pushed up to a seat oil
the Supreme Bench. A nephew (George) was an
apprentice in the navy, but finding deck work
hard, followed uncle to Sydney and climbed up
beside him as Associate. This George was more
remarkable than the uncle; he made a great
spfeh in society of 40 years ago, and married Long’s
third daughter, becoming thus brother-in-law to
James Martin and W. B. Dailey. George Cheeke
returned to England on the death of his uncle,
and was generally accepted there as the Judge
Cheeke of N.S.W. Supreme Court. The real
w! • 6 ee k. e was an inveterate sportsman,
j hile a District Court Judge he ran horses under
the name of Tait (John Tait was his partner), and
while Supreme Court Judge he ran under the
name of Jack Chafe.
“ J.N.” writes :
In conjunction with A. B. Buchanan, the late B. D.
Morehead, once Premier of Queensland, started the well-
known Queensland pastoral firm, which bears his name.
The former found the cash, and the latter was supposed
to supply the experience. In the eighties this firm had
practmally the stock and station business of Queensland
within its grasp. In his palmy days, Morehead would no<-
tolerate interference of any description. One iorp :
startled the Brisbane branch of the Onion Bah* oy
demanding payment in gold for a fifty thousand pound
cheque because the bank demurred to cashing the usual
drovers orders without at once getting the firm’s cheque
in exchange. His political life in the Assembly was
brought to a close by old Beattie, the hatter, who
defeated him by a two-to-one majority for Fortitude
Valley. Morehead was supposed to have the best of
chances for this electorate, but the figures went the
wrong way. Morehead lived to see the remains of his
once fine business, and the rest of his worldly possessions
pass into the hands of others. At the time of his death
he was receiving certain remuneration from the Legisla-
tive Council, and the Q.N. Bank made him an allowance
as chairman of Moreheads Ltd. The collapse of B. P.
Morehead will hold its own amongst the many sensations
provided by the MTlwraith party.

Chief Justice Martin’s law maxim, “ When-
ever it is a crime to take it is a crime to give,”
alluded to in connection with the Crick proseeu-
a (>n a PP eal in
the case ol one White, who had offered two mem-
bers of N.S.W. Parliament £25 apiece to vote for
the claim of a pastoral tenant. White was found
guilty and sentenced to three years ; the convic-
tion was confirmed on appeal, but in the end was
quashed on technical grounds. White’s defence
w as a particularly weak one, the chief point on
which he relied being that he was drunk when he
offered the money.

another point in the exact science of cadging. It
sends round strapping able-bodied men and lasses
to beg left-off clothes, etc., ostensibly to be given
to the poor; but, in place of giving these as freely
as they were received, it sells them to the hard-up
applicants “to save their self-respect.” Mani-
festly, the Army’s self-respect doesn’t need
saving—it hasn’t any.
“Lam ” writes :
It seems of little use to establish minimum wages in the
Commonwealth postal service while the contract post
office is allowed to grow plentifully. Instances were men-
tioned the other night in the Australian Parliament.
Webster (N.S.W.) quoted a post office “near Sydney,”
which used to be run by Government at an annual cost of
£5OO. Now a contractor undertakes to supply premises,
a postmaster, two letter-carriers (with horses) and one
telegraph messenger (with bicycle)—all for £290 a year.
Maloney (Vic.) followed with a mail contractor who ten-
dered at rates which involved a man and horse covering
14 miles over a bad road for ]|d. Apart from contracts
(according to Maloney) the minimum wage is often not
paid. Even the telegraph boy is stalled off with less than
his statutory 10s. a week. And the telegraph boy, under
Commonwealth regulations, may be a hulking youth of 17.
This opens up a difficult question. The unofficial
post-office is necessary. In places where the
business is very small it is absurd to keep a build-
ing solely for postal purposes, and a staff to do
two hours’ work per day, and loaf the rest of the
time. It is necessary, in the interests of economy,
to combine postal business with grocery or some
similar job in such cases. The only thing that can
be done is to see that the contractor’s employees
receive adequate pay, and make that the basis of
the contract.

“ Laz ” : I proudly share this motto with
the blue blood of England and Australia:—
“Labor is dishonorable; work is disgraceful.”
I went to church. The parson, in glossy black,
thundered out “ The Noblest Crown is the Ring
of Sweat around the brow of a Working Man ! ”
The fat man-in a front pew nodded approval,
with a pair of puce kid gloves folded over his
abdomen, a whity sepulchre for turkey and cham.
—not sham. We tip Labor a bit of honorable
bunkum —“A man’s a man for a’ that,” and so on
—to keep him sweet. “ Words, words, words,”
as Hamlet remarked in a foreign language.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
MrD«ABABI KI ._ SIDMI ' H 1905.
Chief Justice Darley, just made a P.C. as a
Jiing s birthday present, is a good walker for a
seventy-sixer. He may often be seen, when the
day is a pieasant one waiking into the city from
his Darlmghurst residence “ Quambi” and padding
back in like fashion. Walking is the up-to-date
exeFCISe.
At the Review on Saturday the C. J.’s consort
made a purple patch on the timber erection from
which some six Chocolate Creams viewed the
march past of the Waler section of the army. She
was ail in royal-hued silk, from sky-raking toque
to the tip of a long tail, and as she is some stones
lighter than a few years ago, the Chief-
tainess of Justice looked —well, as Pinero’s
smart people say, ripping. Bearing her com-
Mrs. Gordon (the commandant’s
wife), Mrs. Oswald Watt, in a hat like an
extinguisher, and her marvellous neck furred with
the costly skin of some dead animal. Marvellous
necks aren t usual except in early womanhood.
A review of even a very small army is a dra-
matic sight. The long bright line of helmets,
bayonets, scarlet and gold jackets, and tan and
white men, all coming out of the sunset and
swinging' along’ to the base where the Commandant
stood ; all pacing with a measured, machine-like
stride—it made one think of the war-verse of
Macaulay, and the Assyrians in their blue mantles,
and of Bland Holt’s squadrons. A review is a
better picture than the battle-pieces that spoil my
appetite in various dining-rooms where I feed in
the city. Why should one be faced with engrav-
ings of dead and dying gunners, and mangled
horses, and the smoke of burning villages, whilst
eating the peaceful lamb and harmless ver-
dant pea ? There is too much Butler in Sydney
hotel dining-halls. Away with Roll Calls,
Quatre Bras and Inkerman, and give us pictures of
Centennial Park, and our own army capering before
the eyes of the Commanding Goat. (This is writ
quite respectful. Remember the he-goat with the
notable horn between his eyes in Scripture ? He
was no common or garden goat, and he stamped
down rams and other stock that would war against
him.) lhen in hot weather let the pictures in the
dining-room be changed, and Jet us have a cool
green sea, and a cool green rock in the midst
thereof, and a cool green mermaid sitting on the
rock, and scraping her green jaw with her green
Dana, and nicking the sea with her cool green tail.
One ticklesome detail of the march-past the
saluting-point was the interested scrutiny by our
citizen soldiery of the women seated in the roped-
ln space. Officers kept up the stony, impassive
. e J es front ” expression, but the ranks mostly
just stared with frank curiosity.
Prince Ferdinand of Savoy, who arrives here in
the poetically-named Calabria, is a very long-
l®B’Bed, long-necked, thin, blonde young man in
the early twenties. A long-pointed nose, a short-
pointed beard, a moustache pointing angel-
wards, and a fascinating manner are some of his
points.” Also—Sydney society, and Sydney
declasse, will please remember—he is a total
abstainer and won’t take any gay trips to the
.National Park. This cruise is part of his edu-
cation a sort of glorified kindergarten effort.
women will find the commander of the
Calabria, Count Something Moriondo, the most
shining piece of ordnance on the ship. He is as
tall as a mast—some masts—and has “a strong
serious face.” Perhaps it is as well to add that
he has a wife. A group of the officers is strongly
musical. The T. H. Kellys will be glad to learn
that this group would pursue a diminishing
seventh on foot from the Quay to Rose Bay.
One of the Big Wool Winchcombes returned
last week from abroad in time for the season’s
fleece sales. Had a look at an auction at the
Jtxchange last week. A dog show is a deaf mute
jn comparison. To be a successful wool-
bidder and catch the auctioneer’s eye and ear you
must have rubber joints, and patent hinges ail
over your body, and a voice that yaps and whistles
and bellows. You must be something between a
fire m a lunatic asylum and an enraged zoological
garden with a little of a dancing dervish and
a howling monkey thrown in. This season’s
shearing is the best since the drought, and the
wool is of record cleanness. Amongst the wool-
ffrowers in the sales chamber—men in rusty
- 6S bashed Panama hats mostly—was
William Broun, the tenth baronet of his name
(family invented in Sixteen something). He con-
descends to live on a station near Manila (N.S. W.)
and raise the ram and ewe, and grow red-faced
and remain in ignorance of the Piccadilly Droop!
The Brouns have a family spook, but so far she
hasn t got out to Manila. She was blocked by
the Education Test though she was able to haunt
in four languages.
Stated by Justice Cohen, in N.S.W. Divorce
Court, that it is doubtful whether the marriage of
Jews by a Christian minister is valid, though
their marriage by a Jewish Rabbi or a registrar
would certainly be legal. To avoid all points of
thatsort, the law should insist on the civil marriage
ceremony before a State official in every case.
Parties could then indulge, as an extra, in what-
ever sort of religious ceremony they chose.
Announced that the widowed youngest daughter
of the Darleys, Lady Carmichael Anstruther, is to
marry, next month, Major Hanbury-Tracey
second son of Baron Sudeley. Bridegroom elect
is 34 years of age. Rhona, a sister of his, was
here a while ago on a visit to the Northcotes. The
Anstruther and Tracey families are already related
t>y marriage.
I didn’t attend the Anglican Synod, held re-
cently in Sydney—in fact I never spoke to a synod
m my life—but a correspondent sends me this
about it:—
At the recent Anglican Synod in Sydney, a full-blooded
rijian (in full Fijian dress, which consists largely of long
brown bare leg's) attended as a member, with as powerful
a vote as any of ’em. He was the first colored man to
sit in an Australian Federal Assembly of an3 r kind. Some-
one jogged the Primate’s elbow and suggested he should
say a few words on “ this occasion.” Bishop Mercer rose,
stammered something, laughed and said, “ I don’t know
what to say—Edward, stand up!” “Edward” raised
himself on his long brown legs, bowed, and said,
Tanks.”
A correspondent on the alleged baneful influence
exercised over woman by education :
R seems to me that this baneful influence is to be
credited rather to over-dressing. In America are made at
present the loudest outcries about the birth-rate and
about the evil of over-education, but in America, also, is
Pasliion gone mad; The great majority of its women have
not had the advantage of what is called higher education.
, Australia it is not over-education that is answer-
Jvy®. ,the raising of the age of marriage and for
the falling of the birth-rate. The State school course
is elementary, the greater number of girls attending these
schools do not attain even to this standard, while, of
those who do so attain, few go further. Private schools
generally show a more ambitious spirit, on paper, and
supply less education. Only a small proportion of girls
, a course °f secondary school instruction, and
a still smaller number complete a University course.
On leaving school—-State, private, or secondary—the
great majority of girls confine their reading to light
fiction, fashion and gossip snippets.
On the other hand, consider the dressing displayed in
the city streets and elsewhere. Whatever maybe said
against tus “frocking” from a hygienic and artistic
point of view, it leaves little to be desired from a fashion-
able standpoint. Some of the wearers are wealthy ladies,
but most are not rich, and many are poor, while all
appear fashionably dressed. Semi-starvation is cheer-
tuUy borne that the penny saved may be spent on dress,
and the resulting poor physique is debited to education,
light and uncomfortable clothing, high-heeled shoes, and
an insecure and ridiculous hat, are eagerly accepted as
contributing to a “ smart ” appearance, and the
nervous strain that results from these discomforts,
combined with the loss of the free play of the lungs, is
also debited to education. The mental effort required to
make one pound do the work of 10 or 20, and to persuade
a horde of unreasonable tradesmen to take 10s. a month
on accounts of £5O or so, use up the phosphates neces-
sary for the growth of a child, and that likewise is debited
to education. Those who are concerned about the birth-
rate, and interested in the welfare of the race might
instead of blaming education, .try to institute some
simple, healthful dress of fixed fashion.
Three sisters, members of Mr. Philip Lytton’s
amateur company, drew a £6OOO prize in a recent
sweep. There are live girls in the family, and
they now reckon themselves dowered.
Sydney society resolved itself into one wide
grin, last week, when it learned, through the Mel-
bourne representative of a local daily, that Miss
Burdekin had “come out” at the Government
-House Race Ball. The damsel has been going to
grown-up balls for three seasons, her friends say,
and she has been engaged to be married for a year
or so. At the Northcotes’ ball at our Government
House, early, last winter, the pretty little person
was present. She wasn’t in debutante raiment,
?, e 1 wear what English fashion papers
call an evening frock for a miss.”
Miss Constance Brandon Usher is the invisible
pianiste in the “Merely Mary Ann ” scene when
Gerrard is supposed to be playing his symphonic
poem. ± retty Constance has scores of fluffy girl
friends who seem to think those few bars played
behind a screen bring her greater glory than plat-
form appearances. Also, they’ll tell you that she
gets £8 a week for those nightly non-appear-
ances.
A swell George-street shop is making the
divided, cross-saddle skirts for riding. Demand
for them for up-to-date women learning to
ride is considerable. Medical men have for
long advised the cross-saddle, but Mother Grundy
with her prunes and prisms was hostile.
“ Adelaide ” writes
The eighth “ Federal ” picture show in Adelaide is ab
soiutely dominated by the local men. There are now four
of the latter standing out prominently—Will Ashton
Hayley Lever, Hans Heysen, and Power; all, except
Powei, are lately back from London and Paris. The £250
to be spent annually (out of the Thomas Elder bequest) in
buying Australian work goes this year to Ashton and
Lever. The bydney and Melbourne artists are compara-
bly weak this year. Tom Roberts, Lister-Lister, Long-
staff, Bunny Julian Ashton, and others have all
received good cheques in the past, and perhaps think
there is no chance of being bought again so soon.
Anyway, absent they are. Blamire Young asks 350
guineas for his brilliant pre-Raphaelite portrait-group of
Tennyson reading to his friends. Percy Spence has two
good things, apparently done under American influ-
ence. The Adelaide people are much as usual, the
Hambndge sisters having a group of good miniature and
other portraits, and Ueorge' Webb a really fine study of
the three judg’es who sat as the S.A. Bench for 20 years—
Way, Boucaut, and Bundey. Place of honor on the end
wains givery to Julius Olsson, young Ashton’s teacher in
England. He has sent out two large sea-pieces, and
Adelaide has jumped-at the chance of buying both.
Strong man Sandow has been telling how he
taught his system to Indian ladies of rank. Most
of these, even if beautiful, run to fat, and they
are just as eager as European women to “ lean ”
down a few stone. He did not see his pupils, but
stood in a strongly-lighted room, the women being
in another darkened one, separated from him by a
beaded screen. They proved eager pupils,
and later asked Sandow to put his arm
through the screen so that they might
look at its muscles. This reminds me that
Mr. Erskine Raymond, who used to wear
captain s clothes in the British army, is back in
Sydney, and has re-started his School of Physical
Gulture for hims and hers at 397 George-street.
Health, strength, vitality and will power are some
of the things that come to you with cultivation of
all your joints and muscles. The day will come
when men and vvomen will be forbidden by law to
get very fat, or if they will persist in “lining their
kidneys, as the men out-back say of those grow-
ing adipose, they’ll be forbidden to go about the
streets. Women falling into middle age are the
EMBARRASSING.
worst offenders in the fat line. They won’t be
bothered to keep and “ cultivate ” their figures.
Not much of the “best butter” is back from
Melbourne yet. You remember the March Hare’s
best butter. Among the b.b. at Rosehill races
yesterday were Mrs. Jack Toohey, in a short blue
linen frock ; Mrs. Monty Severn, all indigo blue ;
a squatter girl, Miss Florence Cooke, from Moree,
and Mrs. Irving-Keyes, one of the Deloitte
family, who has come to live in Sydney. The day
was a gusty one, and several wise women had
adopted Madame Louise’s clever arrangement of
veils, so they didn’t look as if they’d been raked
and harrowed.

The Thomas Dibbses —Mr., Mrs. and two
daughters—are amongst the Returned. These
days, if people come back from a tour and are
unable to give their friends a lot of news, the
latter nickname them “the returned empties.”
However, that’s got nothing to do with the Dibbs
family. They are a philanthropic family, and the
banker head of it mentions that in London “he
found Australia and Australians not in fashion just
now.”
. Well-known architect Spain, who has also been
m similar regions, mentions, en causerie, that
after dining with artist Tom Roberts, the latter
produced a brass bowl and a bundle of gum leaves.
With deliberation and reverence, and as if in per-
formance of a religious rite, the leaves were burnt
m the bowl to conjure up “ the Australian atmos-
phere. That sort of thing answers well enough
for Australia, which is wide and free and breezy.
To get the Asiatic atmosphere you would have to
burn some dead bodies. A purely English atmos-
phere could be got by making a bonfire of a top-
hat m a fog.
A cable says that “ the Queen is greatly in-
terested in the movement.” That is in the appeal
now being made in England on behalf of the
(Sydney) Royal Alexandra Hospital for children.
But there is no mention that Her Majesty’s in-
terest took the form of a cheque.
Elocution of actors Gerrard and Gran in
“ Merely Mary Ann,” is awful. I thought I was
getting deaf, and wasn’t going to blame the actors
for what seemed to me their chortlings and
mumblings and clutterings. But the majority
of persons who have seen “Merely M.A.”, say
that Gerrard’s and (especially) Gran’s dialogue is
lost to them. Last-named mime might be talking
with a railway rug tied over his face for all that
his listeners at the back of the circle and stalls can
hear of what he says.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEH[?].
When one man has shot at another, and
the police have run him in, their first idea seems
to be to ask him his religion. At such a time of
excitement very few men could remember what
their religion was, even supposing they had one.
.rrobably Dill Macky, if he were rushing along
the street with a smoking revolver in his hand”
would hardly remember for the moment if he were
a holy Roman or not. Grant Hervey, after the'
trouble with Walter Baker, said he had no reli-
gion, and no one seems to have asked Baker what
sect he belonged to.

MELBOURNE CHATTER,
Melbourne, November 13, 1905.
My Dear Yarrie,—
There was nothing over-dazzlesome about the
1905 Cup. A temperature that goes with beads
on the brow of the fat man, and a succession of
small and nasty dust-storms were not favorable to
fine apparel. Consequently flutterers wilted in
second-rate clothes, and only a small incon-
sequential section strutted in its very best
fresh-air yoke and league of chiffon tail.
But Lady Northcote was in a wonderful get-up of
rose and orchid shot-satin, with layers of tinted
lace. The head-gear ran to floral excesses, and a
parachute of endless circles of lace. It was a joy-
ous and highly festive Cup rig. The crowd felt
that the Governor-Generaless was that day doing
her duty. The Tasmanian Lady once more wore
sky-blue tints of muslin. An inky hat trailed inky
plumes on its brim. A better example of the
Island style was furnished by Mrs. Charlie Youl,
whose deep orchid chiffons were contrasted with a
top-mast running to red and pink floral garniture.
The Kelso King sisters bounded along in scutty
linens and small hats. The plump blonde of the
family bounces no end.
The three features of the 1905 Cup were less
dress exaggeration, less drinkytite on the part of
the fair sex, and more betting among them. Some
ball-room gowns were flaunted by a few of the
Goths, and some fighting colors were blended by
the brave. But such humors of Cup clothes
occurred at rare intervals. In the refreshment
rooms the full tide of spirits and soda did not flow
over the premises as in former years. Instead,
there was a run on tea and temperance draughts.
The one excess was the amount of betting. I saw
many gorgeously caparisoned women arguing the
point with the booking fraternity and coquetting
them into ‘‘a fair price” on the favorites. A
minor feature of the show was the display of petti-
coats on the part of the fashionables. Mrs.
Everard Browne, Mrs. Harry Payne and Mrs.
Alston Wallace were vivid examples of the poeti-
cally frilled. Dainty colors, fantastic designs, and
a French effusion of ribbons and laces were in
evidence.
The Northcote dance was carried through with
any amount of pomp and dog. Long after the
guests had gathered, the house contingent trooped
in, a double file of caddies leading the way, most
of them aristocratically gone at the knees. The
feminine part of the procession left most of the
rustle trail and gleam to the hostess. That bright
dame had enough for the lot. Her white and
black chiffons had lines of silver moonlight aslant
the skirt, and black Chantilly lace clouding the
corsage and spreading lengthily to the back of the
skirt. Diamonds and emeralds lent a blaze to
head, neck and shoulders. The Tasmanian
Government House dame was in white satin
with purple patches over the material. Her
honeyed thatch was arranged in touzled puffs,
at which Toorak—brilliantined and upholstered
to the highest degree—gazed in amaze-
ment. An outspread pair of dark midnight
wings on the head of Miss Stuart-Wortley was
the one departure from diamonds in the, i •
quarter. The line of demarcation i> v
and the hosts was severely drawn. v;o-
greeting of the Cream. Once arrived, the North-
cotes permitted the programme' to proceed—the
dame overlooking the crowd with the help of a
refrigerating lorgnette. It was impossible to
catch the Yice-Regal eye. It wasn’t to be caught.
At 11 o’clock a bugle-call announced supper, and
the Northcote troop, with Mrs. Deakin and a
dozen other official personages, proceshed to the
special buffet. Their Exes, then went to bed, but
they allowed the gas to keep burning for the
dancers who wanted to use up the band music.
A Sydney daine wore the most uncommon dress
at the Northcote ball. This was Mrs. Burdekin.
She made the splash of the night (as far as color
went) in vivid cornflower blue —a gossamer blue,
gleaming with moonlight-on-the-Danube sparkles,
over a snowy satin ground. The smart dame
comported herself starchily in the singular garb.
Miss Ivy Clarke was a debutante. The prettiest
of the white flock was a sweet young daughter of
Senator Robert Best. The Federalist was absent,
saying his country in the Senate, and his fair
chick had to depend on a pretty step-mommer for
agood time. But nodebutante hasa brilliant or jolly
time at a Government House ball. The frigidity
of such an affair, and the scarcity of young dancing
fry, prevents anything of the kind. The Madden
dame and her two plump offspring made a comely
brood, the dame looking like a gentle elder sister
whose pretty manners had outlived her pretty
youthfulness. Mrs. H. Payne was one of the
stateliest of fair craft in aesthetic tones of mauve.
An orchid was set upon her topmost coil. Mrs.
Urquhart flourished a diamond fender. Likewise
Mrs. George Chirnside and Mrs. Everard Browne.
Ihe Cli veden dowager and Mrs. Edward Miller
helped in the illuminating of the scene. A tall
diiughter of Prime Minister Deakin, deep in
science studies, cast a learned glance over the
whirling multitude. A daughter of pastor Bevan
had a softer eye for the polka flying. She’s a
picture girl of the ‘“Gardener’s Daughter” type.
There was no weather-complaint on Oaks Day.
The dresses, too, had space to trail. Dames with
notions and a passion for clothes, pinned their faith
to mauve tones. The Cliveden dowager was one,
Mrs. Burdekin was another. The latter rather
foolishly wore a crude violet hat with a frail lilac
frock. The chiffons consequently looked faded.
The one striking black rig of the Oaks was
flourished by Mrs. George Chirnside. Mrs.
Everard Browne, Mrs. Fred Payne, and a host of
buckrammed seniors flourished Directoire coats of
flowered brocade. Miss Shylie Blue wore a
muslin that seemed to have been fashioned for her
former plump size.

shows last week. The ex-Aide, with the deter-
mined jaw, didn’t lose his girleen after all. It was
Miss Leila Armytage who shipped herself and
bandboxes to Colombo t’other day. Miss Connie

As soon as Toorak learnt that the grey dame of
Stonington had Captain Fitzpatrick out for a
chat and a stroll the Sabbath before the Cup, the
clan changed its mind about the chappie and fell
upon his neck with surprising enthusiasm. He
and his fluffy fiancee have been buzzing round at
all the Carnival sprees. This week the ex-official
makes tracks for Bananaland again to resume
wool interests.
Early in 1906 Gov. Reggie of this State takes a
jaunt by sea, probably to the Cold Country, iri
which case he will not hie back again. The Tal-
bottles can’t knuckle down to a Governor-General,
especially when the high, grey, blue-blooded
dame has to bend the knee to the little Northcote.
Gov. Talbot suffers from sciatica, stiff neck and
lofty notions. It hurts him to take second place.
Mr. Victor Hood comes from the jovial Le
Hunte staff, and is to take the place on the Vic.
staff of Cap. Buxton, the young man who wears
expressions and forgets to lock up his coun-
tenance, or leave his feelings at home when out
on the heel of the Governor. It seems a terrible
thing in a V.R. caddy to see an impromptu grin
breaking up his visage and surprise, or any other
feeling, on his duty face. The ideal Aide is a mere
machine —the machine of a rubber stamp.
The brightest private spree of the Carnival
was the hop given by Mrs. Fred Payne.
The gardens were dotted over with colored lights.
The house and guests beamed with illuminations.
One Sydney dame, Mrs. Noel Paton, glimmered
in green raiment and many emeralds. The hostess
herself was habited in pallid chiffons suggestive of
dawn mist, with scintillating points of golden
light. Gems of great lustre added radiance to the
toilette. More gold was added to the scene by
Mrs. Harry Payne, whose opulent chiffons,
however, were minus much gem glory—the stolen
diamonds being not yet replaced. Mrs. Burdekin
was mildly garbed in cerulean blue with a sky-
like spread save where cloudy laces broke through.
All the aides from both Government Houses gave
an official tone to the proceedings.
The one Toorak lawn party of the carnival was
given by Mrs. C. Armytage, when “Como” flew the
festive flag. A Sydney flock were to the fore, Mrs.
Philip Morton most prominent.
The Deakin lawn spree was almost limited to
Parliamentarians, and they're the worst in the
world at flaunting bravely in the butterfly fashion.
With the G.-G. troop came the Earl of the ruddy
side-whiskers, and the Governor from the Apple
Isle nearly burst his vest buttons in an effort to
address everyone he remembered. Mrs. Deakin
was garbed in arsenic green taffeta, with
the same hue repeated in her toque. The
one languorous trailer was Mrs. Isaacs, who
has recently been licking her studious spouse into
social shape. He’s coming round to the ways of
frill and folly nicely. She wore a cream lace frock
with girly sash-ends of satin, and blush pink head-
gear.
There was a rush of the best dresses for the
Steeplechase. The G.-G. dame was once more
Joyously garbed—this time in faint champagne
pink and peach tones, all glimmering softly over
her neat form. The rest of the party looked dull
and frumpish. Mrs. Everard Browne, who has
been wearing the most expensive clothes of all the
she-sports of this Carnival, was in a regular ball-
room rig of white silk clouded with net, the net
being broken up by strips of sky blue satin. A
pink hat of the gayest Carnival order went
with this. In general, hats and clothes ran
greatly to cinnamon and burnt onion tones.
Miss Ethel Guthrie, fresh from a trip abroad,
pinned her millinery hopes to a Romney
shape, flying a wonderful feather of those
hues. Miss Hose Musgrove did likewise. Miss
Maud Amber, of Travesty Stars recollection, was
a joy to behold in a whole outfit of cinnamon and
burnt onion chiffons, cut to give every chance of
display to her curves.
A point about the Cup junkets was the failure
of all the Tooraketty to shine. Not one of the
Society charmers got within coo-ee of the first
nxnk of competitors. Lauded girleens like the
Misses Madden, Fair bairn, Oliver, and Anderson
were nowhere. Frisky dames left them in the
ruck of the clothes-horse race.
The thousands travelling out to “Stonington”
the other Monday for a strawberry crush cursed
the distance they had to cover to get there. A
start was made by many before breakfast, and
they landed, tired, hot, and nerve-shaken, just in
time to grasp the paw of the Vice-Regals before
they left off hand-shaking. The motor gang mostly
arrived in hot house machines, from which they
issued foith minus dust and road debris, but plus
many degrees of warmth. The summer motorist
recognises that she has either to swallow several
pounds of circulating earth, or steam within the
glass covers, bhe mostly prefers the steaming.
However, the crush was a chance for airing fine
dresses. The vice-regal dame herself was garbed
m bereavement silk, the dull kind out of which
the tears have washed all lustre. In the high
funereal hat a funereal feather stretched its solemn
length like the long arm of coincidence. When
the greater Exes, and their following landed on
the reception mat, the hosts gave up shaking the
hand of the multitude and trotted off to the tent of
plenty. For once Lady Northcote left off gor-
geous trimmings and contented herself with a
simple peach-pink taffeta, with tinted lace in
frescoes about the top tier and flounces making a
minor fuss about the skirt. Lady Edeline Strick-
land was the one indifferent dresser of the show
She was a mere blue dab. Her blonde attractions
went to waste that day.
The _ most considerable Sydney dasher at
Stonington ’ was Mrs. Spencer Brunton. And
she’s a heavy-weight dasher. Her billowing
chiffons were strewn with gauzy La France roses.
Mrs. Burdekin and Mrs. Philip Morton were a
pastel pair, the pallor of the second being accen-
tuated by the faint aesthetic tones of her toilet.
But for pallor and chiffon elegance Mrs. George
(Jhirnside took the biscuit. The only objection to
her lovely lines was a porcupine ruffle of angry
tulle. This spikey neck ornamentation spoilt the
fur dame’s garb. Mrs. Harry Payne displayed her
marvellous curves and rigid back-line in one of
the lace creations of the day. It was a heavy
Irish guipure over a glace silk. The wearer ap-
peared to have been moulded for the garb, not the
garb for her., Among the grey dames, the figure
and cream raiment of Mrs. Herbert Power were
subject for most note. Those senior dames boast
the dress coquetries and the curves of sweet
seventeen. The men were disturbed as to the
matter of clothes. The beaver-lidded, tail-coated
variety were in the majority, the sac-suited straw-
thatched specimens in the minority. Archbishop
Garr wore his full skirts. As he moved heavily
along, his flock shyly saluted him, some bowing
their crested tops over the kissing signet, others
giving a kind of mixed bob and salaam. The
Gravan Duffys are generally quoted for doing the
correct sort of thing in the way of a Holy
Homan salute.
The tobacconists who, in the first flush of
Federal matters, packed up midget snap-shots of
members of Parliament in their cheap cigarette
packets have found the scheme a failure. The
people who patronise cheap cigarettes expect to
get a bust view of Tottie Fewclothes, and would
find a photo of Julius Cassar, M.P., or Senator
Napoleon, a bore. The street-urchin pitches the
politician snap-shot at his enemy, the Dago of the
banana barrow, and snorts out his disgust.
The jaunty canes which the V.R. caddies air
with their blue-and-red regimentals seem an in-
congruity. A Tommy Dodd stick in conjunction
with a bloodthirsty uniform is as ludicrous as a
fragile sprig of forget-me-not in the coat of a
porky politician.
At the most swagger lawn-spree of the season
a Toorak dame of great wealth and hostly pro-
minence, sat dazed from the effects of over-much
omelette or turtle-soup at lunch. Anyhow, some-
thing at tiffin time had played up with her equi-
librium. It often does. The hands extended to
her by unwary pals dropped back unshaken, and
nought but an inane smile met the how-do glances
of greeters.
Toorak is so tired of seeing one giddy couple in
the shackles of an engagement that it prays for a
speedy marriage, when there’ll be once more a
chance of finding the pair apart. Marriage has a
great power for lengthening the tether of love.
Some matrons’ notion of selling tickets for a
charity hop and then behaving as if they were
private hostesses, has annoyed the dancing con-
tingent who turned up at a recent frolic. There
were two supper rooms—one for th© swagger ele-
ment and the other for the grpat. unleavened.
In the latter the champagne ran 1 out. Then the
indignant ran home.
The Federal Turners are on their way back
from Monkey-land. They touch Brisbane this
week. Advices to hand state that 'the ex-
Treasurer is feeling fit and hearty, and she is
more strenuous than ever.
Mrs. W. Williams—daughter of the late Mr.
Pat. M‘Caughan—has been shining of late as one
of the frilliest of young dames. She dresses like
a Parisienne who knows a thing or two.
Miss Agnes Goodsir is back from extensive
brush-work in Continental centres, and has
some works on show just now. The inter-
esting artiste is a daughter of a prominent
Customs official. Some welcome homes have been
keeping the painter in festive mood.
One of the showiest promenaders at the Flem-
ing^oll Grand Strut has been Mrs. S. Josephson,
of Sydney. Her black and rose chiffons at the
Cup were eye-splitting in their gorgeousness.
Surgeon O’Hara and his pleasant dame wind up
the whirligig with an afternoon spree on Wednes-
day (15th). The O’Hara hospitalities are usually
jolly affairs, with a streak of Bohemia in the pro-
ceedings.
Miss Irene Marks landed at the Grand on Cup
day looking, so report says, as cool as a lemon
blanc-mange. The Madden girls called in to see
the traveller, and bore her off in the evening to
“ Paul Jones.” Next day a shoal of flatterers saw
her off by express to the Holy City.

TRYPHENA.
“Yarrum”: It was Trafalgar Day. I
hiked at a break-neck pace between Charlestown
and the lake to catch a boat to Toronto. Managed
to scramble on board just as she was putting off.
Was hot and irritable, and the noise of many
beery revellers didn’t tend to allay my troubles.
I wanted to think about Nelson and Trafalgar,
but couldn’t. An ancient rum-cask squatted itself
beside me, and asked me to drink schnapps—
never could stand schnapps—from a teacup with
much, froth about it. He said it was a day for
rejoicing. My loyalty wouldn’t stand the strain,
and I refused to imbibe. Then the cask started
to regard me suspiciously, and a bandy-legged
Geordie said I must be a pro-Boer. Later on,
the old prevaricator informed me that his father
had fought side by side with Nelson, who wouldn’t
remove his medals, although the old man had
begged of him to do so. Also, the old man had
heard Horatio bequeath Lady Hamilton to his
country, and had helped Hardy to carry him
below. The old humbug was regarded as some-
body uncommon by the yokels about him ; and no
doubt that fact accounted for his advanced stage
of intoxication. At Toronto that night I took
much whisky from a clean vessel. I felt it was
up to me. Last I can remember was a Dago
fisherman murdering “The Death of Nelson”
through his nose.
Vic. Premier Bent’s closer settlement policy
proceeds unsatisfactorily. The first estate cut up
and offered—SpringVale—consisted of 20 farms, of
which six are still unapplied for ; of the 47 blocks
in Memsie, 10 are so far ownerless ; while of the
53 in the stony Overnewton Estate only 31 went
off, leaving 5400 out of the whole 9200 acres in the
Board’s hands. It has now dawned on the said
Board that something is wrong with the works—it
apparently hasn’t the least idea what—so it isn’t
going to offer Wyuna or Strathkellar “until
harvesting operations are over.” What “harvest-
ing operations” have to do with the man who
wants land, but hasn’t any to reap a harvest from,
Heaven only knows. But the Board has to earn
its wages in the way provided by Bent, and Bent’s
Act didn’t provide for compulsory purchase on
ftiir terms of first-class land near a market; nor
has Bent any apparent intention of imposing a
land-tax to force the big land monopolists to closer
settle their own estates.
It is generally thought that there is no
cure for stoutness, because the usual treatments
only confine themselves to combating single
symptoms instead of attacking the root of the
disease. Pyrolene Pellets remove not only the
superfluous fat, but also the internal deposits of
fat which hinder the action of the heart and lungs.
We advise a trial. Pyrolene Pellets improve the
breathing, give the heart freedom, and enable the
lungs to expand naturally. Sent free by post to
any part of the States on receipt of Postal Note
for 3s 91 or 135., according to size required.

communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

“P.X.” : I live in Mullumbimby, and the
postmaster tells me that he hasn’t got any print-
ing plant on the premises, and that for years past
all the postage-stamps I’ve been buying from him
have been printed in Sydney. You’ll see the
great injustice this is to Mullumbimby. I’ve
therefore called a meeting of our progress com-
mittee, and given notice that I shall move—
“ That the attention of Senior-Constable Smith
be drawn to the serious and unconstitutional act
of the Government in having the postage-stamps
for Mullumbimby printed in Sydney.” Ned
Smithers has also given notice that he will move
that- the Senior - Constable’s attention be
drawn to the serious and unconstitutional action
of Carruthers in having all our dog-tax forms
printed anywhere but in Mullumbimby. (P.S.—
I am informed that the same infamy is practised
with regard to Gobarralong, Pampoolah, Puddle-
dock, Knockrow, and Milparinka. This sort of
thing must stop, or we shall separate.)
T’other day, Sydney daily papers (fired by
Dill Macky and Woolls-Rutledge) were be-
spattered with indignation at the audacity of the
Federal Parliament when it passed a resolution re
Home Rule for Ireland, and suggestions were
heaved about that the British Parliament ought
to tell the Australian ditto to keep its own door-
step bright and shiny without dabbling in the
government of other parts of the Empire. The
question looked so simple that even the N.S.W.
Legislative Council woke up sufficiently to
mumble something about it, but when a
Councillor asked what the talk was about
no one seemed to know, and dreariness o’erspread
the Chamber of Toddlers once more. The
gist of the rumpus was that Australia should
mind its own affairs, and allow other people to
mind their personal troubles. But the excellent
suggestion was still hot when Premier Carruthers
decided to join with the other State Parliaments
in asking the Australian Government to poke its
nose into Russian affairs, by “ forwarding a strong
remonstrance to the Imperial Government against
Russia’s Jewish atrocities
Caution.— There is no genuine Aromatic
Schnapps but “Wolfe’s.” Call it by its full
name. Wolfe’s Schnapps counteracts the effect
of bad liquors, bad beer, bad water. It cures the
troubles they bring on.
The Objective.
E n eXact i S1 j e T of tlie riffc the ranks of
' Lal ? or Party is a thing that is
moult to estimate, but, whether it prove large or
small, one thing seems certain—that purposeless
and unornamental article called the “ objective ”
is partly responsible. Considering the uselessness
of the objective it is a pity that this incentive to
n not a ' to ? et T he r dropped. The programme
of the Queensland Labor party sets forth the first
halt ot its ultimate aim, or target, or zenith, or
apex, or culmination, to be
nroHnopr^w 6^ 111 re ?, ulfcs of their industry to the wealth
ES by .the collective ownership of the means of
thrn, /h distribution, and exchange, to be attained
fnnptinna if' X at n ? 10n ? f tlle industrial and economic

The party then lays down a list of measures—in
e main most valuable and essential measures—
which are expected to lead up to this result. They
may lead there or they may not; many things
don t lead where people imagine they will;
anyhow they won t lead there this session or
next or the one after that—probably not this
generation—and at present the essential is to pass
the measures. They are for the present; the
objective is, for the future. But meajiwhile
the objective ” looks very vague and alarming
to many mild, ordinary people, and by publishing
it, and declaring that simple business-like pro-
positions such as the abolition of the useless
otate Governor, the progressive land-tax and
income-tax, the establishment of a Public Trust
State Liquor Monopoly, and the Public
i)ebt Sinking Fund—are expected and intended to
lead up to it, the party really warns these people not
to vote for the aforesaid business-like propositions.
It may be honest to tell the whole story in
advance, and to play the game with the cards on
the table, but it is a useless and unprofitable kind
u honesty. If the Labor party said nothing
about its “ objective it might think about
it as much as it liked—and simply plodded
on, securing _ one item after another of its
programme, it would gain support as it
progressed. After it had secured a dozen or more
of its reforms, and shown that, instead of the
country being ruined by them, it was all the better
and all the richer, many of the timid souls who
are now scared to death by the “objective” would
probably hear of it without turning aiiair. Again,
if the party were not. so visibly chained up to its
objective, ’ and didn’t make its acceptance so
bdter a dogma, it might secure the support of many
politicians who are ready to subscribe to all, or
much of, the platform, and only draw the line at
the nature of the alleged millennium which is to
follow. There is no reason why it shouldn’t use
these politicians as it goes along. They will pro-
bably be dead in any case before the “objective ”
is arrived at. The Democratic party which
has achieved most success—that of Maoriland—
started without any declared “objective.” It
i dn u * n vance where it was going, and
thereby alarm timid souls about the length
of the road and the startling character of the
destination. It just went, and now it is nearer
to arriving than most parties. The “objective”
is not only the most useless item of the Labor
party s luggage; it is also the chief weapon of the
party’s enemies. On it they hang their loudest
and most plausible shrieks about “confiscation,”
‘Socialism,” and “revolution.” And when the
Labor party is revising its programme, tlie worst
difficulty and the nearest approach to internal
breakage is generally over the “objective.” In
fact, the Queensland State Labor party and the
Federal Labor party have already divided on the
point. The struggle to define this Mecca or El
Dorado or New Jerusalem at which the party
hopes to arrive in the dim, remote future, is a
very great struggle at times, and the New Jeru-
salem, when it is defined, does no real good. Also
it helps to divert the party’s own attention
from matters of immediate concern. It is
just a millennium—as vague and of as little
immediate purpose. If the party must have
an “ objective ” it should appoint a trusted
committee of one to draft it and write it
out, and then let him put it in an iron box, and
bury it deep in his back yard, and say nothing
about it. And meanwhile the party should de-
vote itself to deciding on the measures it wants,
and when they have been all secured it should dig
up the “objective ” and look at it. Solomon, who
was occasionally threatened with brains, said
something about an individual who told all his
mind. And his son Rehoboam, who was never in
danger of brains, told all his mind in one act, and
lost five-sixths of a kingdom right there, and had
his rate*collector killed into the bargain.
+
At one time The Bulletin was, in its advo-
cacy of a White Australia, pretty well *‘A voice cry-
ing in the wilderness scarcely any other import-
ant weekly or daily paper supported the principle
m its entirety. Nowadays, however, the tide has
turned, and even the London papers are being
converted. London Times gives the policy its
tardy, but hearty endorsement, declaring that
It is certain that an absolute laissez fairs policy as
regards colored immigrants would make Australia the
home of a preponderating yellow-brown population.
The Outlook, one of the sanest and most thought-
ful of the English weeklies, printed this lately :
All Australians are agreed in accepting the root
principles of the white Australia policy, which, with its
eccentricities expunged, and logically developed under the
present Prime Minister, will make for the safeguarding of
Imperial interests in the Pacific. There can be no doubt
whatever that_Australia, and also British Columbia, are
wise in excluding Asiatic labor, even of the best type.
As regards the present position of the
Federal capital question :
The people of N.S.W. accepted the Federal Constitu-
tion by a solid majority when there was in it no stipula-
tion at all about where the capital should be. But their
vote was annulled in advance by the State Parliament
N.S.W. had invited the other States to come into the
movement, and its Parliament agreed that the Federal
Constitution should be accepted if a majority of the
people voted for it, and if that majority mustered at
least 50,000. votes. The other States adhered to their share
of the agreement; the N.S.W. Parliament broke its
pledge by raising the minimum to 80,000 votes. It was
the one Parliament which couldn’t keep faith—a Parlia-
ment of mean traitors, of promise-breakers, of sneaks and
shufflers, of repudiationists, dodgers and backers-out.
When it had annulled the popular vote in acceptance of
the Bill it was agreed, without reference to the
people, that there should be a fresh deal on
the understanding that the capital should be in
N.S.W., not less than 100 miles from Sydney, and
should consist of an area of i\t less than 100
square miles. Now a section at least of the N.S.W.
Parliament seeks to again break the agreement, and is
the same old faithless, lying, shuffling disgrace. It again
wants to alter the bargain, and to insert variations. One
variation it demands is that the capital shall be close to
the 100-mile limit from Sydney, which is not in the
agreement. Another is that it shall not be much over
the minimum of 100 square miles, which also is not in the
agreement. A third is that N.S.W. shall have a voice
apart from the voice of its Federal members, in fixing the
site of the capital, which, again, is not in the agreement
As before, the N.S.W. Parliament is the one Legislature
m Australia which can’t keep faith—the one Legislature
whose solemn undertaking is of no more value than
Wilkins Micawber’s P.N. or Caesar Borgia’s oath. Under
the circumstances, if N.S.W. isn’t ashamed of its miser-
able State Parliament it can only be because it and its
Parliament are a pair, and a very bad pair.


POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S. Wales put up another record in the
past year (to June 30); for the first time its State
interest bill got past £3,000,000.
They are now beginning to talk in Victoria
about the “Brick Works Scandal,” and there is a
heaving of the surface as if some buried accu-
sation was trying to get out of its grave and have
a word with somebody.
S.A. Premier Price, finding that the Rail-
way Department was called upon to pay too high
a price for its ice, has resolved to start a State
ice factory. It will be a handy place for the in-
furiated Tory, red hot with anger at Labor
legislation, to drop into for a cool down.
The Tasmanian Assembly has just thrown
out, as violent and revolutionary, a Bill providing
for a proper audit of the accounts of friendly
societies, so as to make sure that the members
shall get the benefits they pay for. It is hard to
guess what wouldn’t be regarded as revolutionary
in Tasmania.
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, has a
scheme on hand whereby an old age pension shall
be payable to every aged inhabitant if he likes to
claim it, regardless of his wealth or poverty.
Denmark, where the old age pension commenced,
works on that basis, so there is a good sound
precedent to go upon if one should be required.
Prime Minister Deakin again decides that
there won’t be time to pass the High Commis-
sioner Bill and appoint a High Commissioner this
session. And unless Deakin gets a set of stand-
ing orders that will quell obstruction, and a lower
jaw more like the cow-catcher of a locomotive,
he never will have time for anything of impor-
tance.
That Australian slanderer, Tozer, has been
at it again. The other day he had the audacity
to wire to Premier Morgan, suggesting that the
new State Governor, Lord Chelmsford, was in a
difficulty about getting his retinue past the
Customs when they reached Australia. The reply
was one which even a rhinoceros like Tozer will
recollect for a day or two.
Arthur Griffith made a sensible suggestion
in N.S.W. State Parliament last week that, instead
of spending a huge sum of borrowed money in
constructing anew library building, the Go vernmen t
should utilise Government House for the purpose.
Government House is now used, on an average, a
month or two per annum, and just now N.S.W.
has three Government Houses empty —at Sydney,
Rose Bay and Moss Vale.
Apropos of the “Belata” belting case
evidence, it is a curious fact that, whilst the
Freetrader is always howling about the “lob-
bying ” of Protectionist manufacturers, such
lobbying has never, to The Bulletin’s know-
ledge, been sworn to in a court of law; but the
Freetrader is constantly being shown up in the
witness-box as a person who employs agents to
“ work ” members of Parliament to secure reduc-
tion or abolition of duties with a view to the
destruction of Australian industries.
In the face of keen competition, old John
Warren, M.L.C., achieved the record for absolute
puerility in the debate on the Shearers’ Accommo-
dation Bill recently before the S.A. House of
Fossils. As a last despairing effort, Warren said
that most of the shearers lived in very small
dwellings, and the increased breathing space
provided under the Bill would cause them to
catch colds and snuffle. And you must have a
special property qualification before you can vote
for great men like John Warren in S.A. !
“ Caucus ” from Westralia :
I was not surprised at ex-Prenrier Daglish ratting from
the Labor ranks. He never was a Labor man at heart.
In South Melbourne, some years ago (Daglish and Bent
lost their deposits at the same election), he was opposed
to the Labor cause. When he moved to Westralia he was
still an opponent and tried to prevent a labor league being
formed in Subiaco suburb, which, later on, he represented
as a pledged Labor member. His secrecy, his want of
confidence in his colleagues, his inability to grasp facts,
and his general unfitness as leader were the main causes
of the wreck of the Labor Ministry.
The fact that 11 contractors are on trial at
New York for importing English workmen under
contract will perhaps at last make Bruce Smith
leave off deceiving audiences of credulous females
with his oft-told tale about how the United States
opens its doors to all comers.' There is ri. line
beyond which even a politician shouldn’t go
m the way of misrepresentation, and misrepre-
sentation which consists of the bald statement
that the thing is what isn’t is a crude affair. Any
pushite or domain loafer is equal to that kind of
diplomacy.
The stand made by Prime Minister Deakin
in “refusing to adjourn the National Parliament
for a horse-race, ” and thus forcing the House to
sit on the afternoon of Cup Day, would have had
some, value if the obstructives of the Opposition
hadn’t taken rip nearly the whole of the afternoon
before Cup Day in discussing whether they ri'ould
Sit on Cup afternoon. What is wanted is riot
overtime or extended hours so much as a method
of getting some strenuous, useful work done
within ordinary working hours. And Deakin is
too affable.
Answering Williemotor Kelly in the House
pf Reps, last week, Minister Eyving stated that
the Government was favorable to locally-owried
warships, and was considering the question of
buying some of Britain's obsolete stock at the next
auction 1 You can almost hear the dull thud of
falling Japanese stocks. Simultaneous cables
|tate_ that Canada is also starting a navy, the
Dominion Minister for Marine having arrived in
England to arrange for the construction of three
new cruisers. Note the difference!
Apropos of the bitter, bitter wail ,of N.S.W.
treipier Carruthers because the Australian Govern-
ment proposes to print its stamps at Adelaide (it
must print them somewhere, and it finds that Ade-
laide does the cheapest stamp-printing in Austra-
lia)—it only seems like yesterday when Carruthers
was fighting tooth-and-nail to get the building of
60 N.S.W. locomotives done in the U.S. for the
sake of cheapness. According to Carruthers,
therefore, it is right to take a very large job from
Sydney and give it to America, but it is a cause
for rage and cursing and secession to give a small
job to South Australia. As already mentioned,
the stamp-printing business must be concentrated
somewhere for the sake of economy. Queensland,
Victoria, Tasmania and W.A." will lose their little
job just as N.S.W. will, but it is only N.S.W.—
the spoilt infant of the Commonwealth—that is
blubbering.
A MATTER OF NO IMPORTANCE.
T HE BOSS (to applicant for position as office boy): “ Well, and what's your name!"
CHEERFUL YOUTH (airily): “ Don't let that worry yer, boss. Call me Bosker.”
(N.B. —He lasted one week.)
Thus Prime Minister Deakin :
If we pay more for our loans, if we get fewer people
who emigrate from the Mother country than we ought to
get, if our progress is less than it should be, it is largely
due to the malignant falsehoods of those who call them-
selves Australians in Australian newspapers.
Which is excellent English, and quite true so far
as it goes. But if Deakin would give up politely
bowing and scraping over the landscape and Do
Things, he could smother the Stinking Fish party.
When a Government is only going to do some-
thing, that something will serve for years as a
Stinking Fish argument about the ruin that is
bound to follow on Socialistic legislation. When
it does the thing—does it well and proves it bene-
ficial instead of injurious—then panting Fish
stinks after it in vain.
Two paragraphs from an article headed
“Employees That Pay,” in S.M. Hf.rat.d :
To get good work and give good wages shows level-
headedness. To get good work and give little or no
wages shows talent. To get good work and handsome
profits in exchange shows genius.
Mr. Suttor, the Commercial Agent for the East, now
suggests that Japanese students (for whose names and
position he will vouch) be sent to Australia to learn wool-
classing and kindred subjects. They would sort out wool
and do other “dirty work,” and pay well. It is a splen-
did idea.
With Kanaka cane-cuttera and mill workers,
Afghan and Syrian carriers, Chinese cooks, gar-
deners and boundary-riders, Red Indian stock-
men, Jap wool classers and shearers, nigger
vignerons and fishermen, Booth’s scum for agri-
culturists, coolie miners, and the riffraff of the
earth for general rouseabouts, this misgoverned
country would begin to approach somewhat to the
ideal of the capitalistic minority. What their
own white countrymen—the men who blazed the
track —would do doesn’t matter. Yet, if the cap-
able Jap persons offered to edit and sub-edit and
report the S.M. Herald for nothing, and the
editor, who thinks it a “splendid idea ” when his
poorer fellow countryman loses his job, lost his
own job, he would probably cry out in quite a dif-
ferent key.

Sons of Japanese gentlemen are sent to Townsville (Q.)

as cooks, &e. —and incidentally to do white cooks
out of their billets.

Preniier Bent’s attention has just been drawn
to the fact that four members of the Yic. House
of Rich Landlords, directors or shareholders of the
Melbourne Tramway Co. rind thus personally and
pecuniarily interested, \ cited for the airiendmeni
