Another Australian is beirig
ruined, according to the daily papers ; the Immi-
gration Restriction Act is said to have driven
away 125 pearling boats from Thursday Island to
the Straits in the last two years. An Australian
industry ! It will make those smile who know.
Practically, it didn’t employ one Australian or
European. The discolored scum whom it did
employ spent practically nothing with Australians
or Europeans. All they' did for Australia
was to skin its best pearling grounds. The
S.M. Herald, which is shocked at this new
atrocity of Labor legislation (which, by the way,
isn’t Labor legislation at all), should be equally
shocked if a policeman stopped a party of burglars
from breaking into Mr. James’s house and having
a riot in the back-yard with the proceeds.
Again the N.S.W. farmer and pastoralist
are to be greased with a subsidy. Under Carru-
thers’ Shires Bill a farmer or squatter who pays
local rates on his land will not also be liable to pay
land-tax to the State, nor uill he have to pay
income-tax on the rents received from the land nor
on income derived from the use of the land. Yet
the manufacturer pays land-tax to the State on
the value of the ground his factory stands on,
municipal rates on the value of the ground and
building, and income-tax on the profits of his
industry. The pampered “ backbone of the coun-
try ” mustn’t be taxed twice ; it doesn’t matter in
the least if the manufacturer is taxed three times.
The ponderous British War Office has just
gut up a record for motionlessness. When the
ohen-China war commenced, some six years ago,
and the first Cohentingenter went across from
Australia to Boer-land, an application was made
that Australian officers should be allowed to
officially retain their local rank in the field. The
War Office has just published, in the London
Gazette, its consent to this request; and it prints
a score or more columns of the names of those
officers entitled to the “concession.” Against
many of the names is the remark “sincedeceased.”
Possibly the War Office isn’t yet aware, officially,
that the war is over.
The Sunshine Harvester Works have just
been forced to discharge 130 men. The American
Harvester people are selling machines so cheaply,
under the present miserably inefficient tariff, that
it is becoming impossible to compete with them.
Of course, they may lose by it for a time, but, when
the Australian industry is killed quite dead, and
all its men are thrown out of work, they will raise
the price sufficiently to make good the present
loss three times over. There will be no Australian
competition then. The U.S. has a tariff which
doesn’t allow of foreign competition, and the
Harvester people can make enough profits there
to more than cover any temporary loss incurred
while (with the assistance of our Foreign-trade
push) they are killing an Australian industry.
British politicians, &c., as described by
Melbourne Age’s London correspondent:—
, They are content to remain densely ignorant of the
actual state of affairs in Australia, Maoriland, and the
Pacific, and few of them have ever been as far as the; Cape
or Canada. Some of these are the men who still accept
the Freetrader, Sir Charles Dilke, as a colonial authority,
on the strength of a book that he wrote some sixteen
years ago. Sir Gilbert Parker is another of their instruc-
tors. A third oracle who influences many of them
through the leading columns of a Freetrade morning
paper—often to the disadvantage of Australia—has never
been outside England in his life, except for a few summer
holidays on the other side of the Channel. On colonial
questions he is one of the specialists of Fleet-street. A
couple of years ago he wrote an elaborate work on South
Africa, and did not find it necessary to go further than
Bloomsbury for his facts.
“ X.Y.Z.” : What folly is all this naval
celebration business ! When England defeated
the Sp'anish Aririada, ahd Drake, Frobisher,
Raleigh arid Co. raged over all the world’s seas;
England had only the same population that Aus-
tralia has ridw. Therefore it seems clear to me,
after studying George Reid’s speeches on the
impossibility of Australian naval defence, that
England, in those days, had no right to have rifly
riavy at ali, that it couldn’t support one, arid that
it should instead have paid a subsidy to someone
to deferid it. Why do not our school histories of
England point out that, according to Australian
policy, it was darned cheek for Drake, Frobisher
and Co. to exist at all ?
Premier Bent’s brutal outspokenness
brought him into collision with the Walhalla
(Yic.) shire councillors lately. They called him
“arrogant” and “discourteous”; also, he whs

Treasurer Kidston’sßill, providing that the
proceeds of Queensland land auctions should go into
a Public Works Fund, met with bitter opposition
from the Socialism-in-our-Time section of the
Labor party, which complained that the idea
would encourage the jobbing away of the public
estate. But it is hard to see how there will be
more temptation to scatter land revenue that way
than the other way, and at least Kidston’s scheme
means that the State will have new assets to show
for its land-sale money. Under the old system
the land went just the same, and there was nothing
to show for it except the joy of possessing Robert
Philp. Certainly the land should not be sold, but
Queensland is between the devil and the deep sea,
and the sea is at least cooler than the devil.
There was a terrible row in Sydney last
week because a German steward, awash with
beer, strolled on board an anchored ship and
hauled down the British flag. The press was
furious, questions were asked in the Legislative
Council, the Government began to snort, and the
German Consul-General sent a hurried apology,
intimating that the miscreant was drunk at the
time and had been severely dealt with. Had he
brought in a shipload of goods and closed up a
couple of Australian factories, throwing a hundred
or two of Austral-Britons out of work, the papers
that howled for his execution would have written
about the expansion of our blessed commerce.
There’s a lot too much regard for the flag, and too
little for what the flag represents.
Said that the Jap authorities made in-
quiries of the Australian Government —during
Reid’s term of office—as to the terms on which
Jap doctors would be admitted to practice in the
Commonwealth. The Medical Boards of the
various States were communicated with on the
subject, but the Reid Ministry collapsed before
any action was taken. It would be interesting to
know whether the present Government has come
across the papers dealing with the matter. The
Bulletin has its doubts about doctors, but it
thinks that Japanese barristers and daily paper
editors should be encouraged to come here. The
Argus with a cheap Jap editor and a briefless
Bruce Smith manfully facing Jap competition
would be too beautiful for words.
S.A. Legislative Council has contemp-
tuously disregarded the verdict of the great
majority of the people at the last general election,
and thrown out the Constitutional Reform Bills.
Premier Price, anxious for rest and recess, does
not want, it is said, to fight, but Labor Leader
Archibald is urging him to take up a definite
stand. Still, with an immovable Council, what is
a definite stand, and how do you take it up?
Unless the Assembly is prepared to stop supplies,
and put the whole Government machine on strike
till the Council climbs down (and that is what it
should do) a stand is of little value. It may be
something to put a hot teapot on, or it may mean
a street corner where you sell bootlaces, but all
these things are vanity.
A whole lot of people lately awoke to a
sense of the sinfulness of a State establishing a
fund to do its own fire-insuring ; and Eat fumed
that it was a rotten idea from a business point of
view and a gross interference with Private Enter-
prise. Carruthers had hardly crumpled up as
usual, abolished the insurance fund, and collared
the accumulated premiums, when the Mt. Lyell
Co., one of the representative Eat institutions of
the Commonwealth, announced that it had decided
to do the very thing that the State had aban-
doned :
In view of the high premiums which the Fire Under-
writers’ Association has fixed for the co.’s Yarraville
Chemical Works, and considering the manner in which
the risks are spread over a large area, your directors have
resolved to do the whole of the co.’s fire underwriting.
It has therefore been decided to form a Fire Insurance
Fund.
So Bill Knox, M.H.R., Bowes Kelly, and others
of the Fattest of the Fat do the very thing that
poor, half-baked Carruthers was scared avvay from,
because somebody (with his own fire-insurance
scrip up his sleeve) told him that no business man
would think of doing it.

SPORTING NOTIONS
Melbourne last week was strewn with the frag-
ments of shattered Oup dreams. The dreams were more
circumstantial than usual, and in two or three cases they
involved the death of the dreamer prior to the race, with
the result that the sorrowing relatives of the deceased
felt very confident about “ landing the double.” A
butcher, living down at Brighton (Melb.), had a dream
which admitted of profitable interpretation. He saw a
number of different animals running for the Cup—a sort
of go-as-you-please menagerie race. The animal that
came in first was a bullock —as might be expected in a
butcher’s dream —so he put £5 on Blue Spec because
F. Bullock was riding it.
* * * *
Fair to assume that Tom Payten’s pair of Oup
candidates —Scot Free and Elvo—might have finished first
and third but for their 71b. penalties. Scot Free didn’t
get a 71b. beating from Blue Spec, nor was Elvo far be-
hind Tartan. Turning into the straight the mare was
running about fourth to Scot Free and Fabric after a big
effort to get clear of the ruck. Under the circumstances
she must have been much troubled by the extra 71b.
* ■» * *
The backers of Blue Spec couldn’t show much
cause for their triumphant “ What did I tell you ! ” after
the race. Blue Spec got away better than most of them,
and being a naturally wary or “ handy ” horse in a big
field he was never in a bad place. He has the necessary
stamina, and he outstayed Scot Free. But it wasn’t
a brilliant win from a strong opposition. As for his pre-
vious victory in the Moonee Valley Cup, there was the
fact that Aurate, who finished third, had also run im-
pressively, and when Aurate, a week later, was seen to be
so short of condition that he couldn’t win the Hothani
Handicap with the minimum weight, the “form” of
Blue Spec looked very moderate. It was his staying
power in a very fast run two miles that enabled him to
carry Bst in front of an exceptionally poor field. Not a
high-class three-year-old in the crowd. Among the older
horses, two of the fastest—Marvel Loch and Glad-
some —have failed to keep the steam going over a
longer distance than 12 furlongs. Tartan (9st) was
a Sydney Cup winner, to be sure, but he carried only
7.12 when he won the Cup from the moderate Tatter-
demalion. Warroo might be equal to the distance if he
were fit, but the bulky fellow was not fit on Cup day.
And Maniopoto—the one brilliant performer in the field—
is not yet proved to be a better stayer than Marvel Loch,
though probably he could have gone two miles on the
day he won the Metropolitan. Since then the Maori-
lander has been affected by something more than a bad
cold. The boy who looks after him in the “ birdcage ”
carries a stick—a hunting crop—as though the horse
were another Emir. But if Maniopoto is not another
Emir, and his legs serve him through another couple of
seasons on the turf, he may be-confidently expected to
make very small potatoes of the horses that beat him in
the Melbourne Cup.
* • * *
“E.F.”: One gets rather tired of the languid
non-racing cynic who doesn’t believe in backing favorites
for the Melbourne Cup and always speaks of the'news-
paper tipsters as misleading oracles. Melbourne Cup for
years past has been a satisfactory race for punters who
backed not less than four or five of the shortest priced
horses at the start. These in each case were fancied,
more or less, by the llth-hour prophets. The five
favorites this year were Tartan, Scot Free, Maniopoto,
Blue Spec and Warroo. The three foremost horses came
from that little lot, and the actual winner’s price was 10
to 1. Also Fabric, the sixth favorite, finished fourth.
Last year, the winner and runner-up were fourth and fifth
favorites, and the winner, Acrasia, started at 14 to 1. In
1903 the winner, Lord Cardigan, started third favorite at
5 to 1, whilst Wakeful, the runner-up, was fourth favorite
at 12 to 1. In 1902 the two horses that beat the first
favorite were very little fancied at the start, but in 1901
the first and second favorites finished in accordance with
the betting. Punters couldn’t afford to back five horses
that year, for “ dead bird ’’ Revenue started at the short
odds of 7 to 4, and San Fran, the runner-up, was only a
4 to 1 chance. But, then, the tipsters confined their
tips to those two horses. If -the public were
to let themselves be warned against the least
likely horses, and were to form their fancies from
a combination- of haif-a-dozen different tipsters’ “lots”
on the morning of the race, they would not come to much
harm by the Melbourne Oup, taking one year with
another. It is the early backing of imaginary “good
things ” at comparatively short prices, and the late back-
ing of hopeless things at long prices which account for
ninety per cent, of the profit on Ikey Mo.’s book. After
looking through the records one is struck by the fact that
the Melbourne Cup rarely provides a remarkable “ turn-
up.” The Victory was a2sto 1 chance, but the sturdy
battler had run a good second to Wakeful for the
Melbourne Stakes three days previously'. Clean Sweep
started at 20’s, but he, like Blue Spec, had captured the
Moonee Valley Cup. For a genuine surprise we have to
go back to Tarcoola —a matter of somewhat ancient
history.
* * * *
“F.C.” : A few Cup statistics: —Of the 45 races
the first favorite has won 11, though during the last 15
years only Newhaven, Merriwee and Revenue have been
first in the betting and also first past the post. Victoria
has annexed 25 Cups, N.S.W. 17, S. Australia two, and
Westralia one—the latest. Twelve have been taken by
3-year-olds, twelve by 5-year-olds, nine by 4-year-olds,
seven by 6-year-olds, and five by old and venerable horses.
Bay horses have won 23 times, browns 14, chestnuts four,
blacks three; only once has a grey caught the judge’s
eye—Toryboy, 40 years back.
* * * *
If Lady Wallace is the “ great ” filly that some
horse reporters would make her out to be, how much
greater are the Maoriland 3-year-olds that ran away from
her at Randwick ? Her best performance thusfar was the
V.R.O. Derby win from Grama, who finished about sixth
for the Melbourne Cup with only 6.9 on his back.
* ft * *
Proceedor, winner of the Veteran Stakes at
Flemington, is said to have pulled a buggy for a while
after he had seeming-ly lost his racing pace. The buggy
treatment brought the gelding back to form again.
♦ * * *
The betting on the Flying Stakes last Thursday
was less remarkable than the fact that it escaped caustic
comment. Gladsome, although she had run less bril-
liantly than usual in the Melbourne Stakes, was neverthe-
less a good thing on paper for this w.f.a. race over seven
furlongs. Punters at the outset accepted a shade of odds,
and then, to their amazement, the mare went back in the
market until as much as 7to 1 was laid. Meanwhile, the
two-year-old Oreillet shortened to 6to 4. The race was
practically confined to Oriellet, Scotland (the outsider),
and Gladsome. Scotland chased the favorite, with Glad-
some in close attendance. The moment the outsider had .
collared the youngster Gladsome came past the pair of
them and won by a head. Whereat the public, who had
taken unaccountably liberal odds about Gladsome, were
full of joy, whilst those who had stood off the mare on
account of the extraordinary betting, could' only shake
their heads and wonder what it all meant. Gladsome
actually started at 5 to 1, Charles Stuart and Maniopoto
being backed at the same price. There was a shadow of
reason for the hope that Maniopoto would improve on
his recent performances, but the idea of Charles Stuart
(Bst.) beating even a deteriorated Gladsoihe (8.11) seemed
clearly preposterous except on the supposition that the
mare was not a goer. Last year odds of 7to 4 were laid
on Gladsome for the same race, and she won easily from
Sylvanite, the penalised Derby winner.
Aurate’s performance in the V.R.O. Handicap
was nothing to blow about —he made slow time with a
light weight—but it was enough, and it bore out Tiib
Bulletin's prediction that the son of Auraria would be
sufficiently forward in condition to win a handicap at the
end of the meeting. In excuse for Blue Spec and Boabdil,
the first and second favorites, they were said to have got
shut in against the rails coming down the straight. The
success of either gee would have been mortifying to the
other one’s jockey, for Bullock, who landed Blue Spec a
winner in the Melbourne Cup, was now riding Boabdil,
whilst Norn,an Godby got the show on Blue Spec.
* * * *
The luckiest winner at the Cup meeting was
Grama. The colt’s owner captured the Carnival Handi-
cap on a protest against Jargoon, Whose swerving across
the course had probably prevented F.J.A. from beating
the pair of them. But seeing that Grama finished second
to the swerver, and had done nothing to deserve dis-
qualification, a protest from F.J.A., the real sufferer, was
out of the question. As often as not the runner-up that
fairly gets a race on a protest looked likely to have been
beaten by the third horse if there had been no inter-
ference. In these cases there is no sporting reason why
the stewards shouldn’t order the race to be run off by the
two that were interfered with.
* * * *
At the initial meeting of the Kauri Stump Race
Club the local Maori chief acted as judge. He arrived
resplendent in a silk hat, frock coat, and red tie. The
course, marked .out with sticks, was a very small one,
and the only intimation of the finish that the jockeys had
was by the ringing of a bell as they entered the straight.
The following- conversation was overheard between judge
and steward as the horses entered the straight after
several times round: “Who in de flout now?”
“ Pakeha ” (white man). “ Lound agen.” Then as they
entered again after completing another round, “ Who in
deflontnow?” “Maori.” “Ling de bell.”
•* * * *
Saturday last was a day of some importance in
N.S.W. On the Hunter River, Arthur Towns and George
Ford were rowing for £2OO a-side ; at Sydney Cricket
Ground, the Australasian Athletic Championships meet-
ing was in full blast; and in the evening the same locality
saw some desperate cycle racing in the final of the Orient
Plate Scratch Mile, and a fine sprint at the close of the
Five Miles. . There were other things, but these were the
most important- and frilly.
* * * *
The Ford-Towns race was a well-patronised
affair, but so large a proportion of the spectators were
along the banks that the scullers must have been moved
to tears. The attendance on the official steamers was
meagre. Towns, wearing a light blue cap and dark blue
jersey, won the toss from Ford, who was in red and white
stripes, and took the windward side. The race was over
what is known as the Raymond Terrace course,
a straight stretch of water, 3J miles long. The
breeze blew dead behind the scullers. It took some
time to effect a start, and then they got away well
together at 37 to the minute. Roars, yells and
exact instructions how to win the race were shouted by
the crowd on the banks as Ford, after 200 yd., went to
the front. For three-quarters of a mile Ford led the way,
pulling the faster stroke, and then it was seen that
Towns was the stronger. With a slower stroke lie over-
took and passed Ford, and at the end of the mile was a
length and a half in front. Ford spurted desperately, but
Towns drew away every time, and in the end won easily
by six lengths. Had he put his ears back he could have
won by twice that distance. It was a most decisive
defeat. Now the old rowing hands are saying that
Arthur Towns is the coming champion.
* * * - *
South Tasmania comfortably defeated the
Northern team, in the Tasmanian eight-oar struggle,
recently on the Derwent River, and demonstrated the
fact that it isn’t always brawn which succeeds at sport.
The Souths’ average weight was list sJlb, while the
Norths averaged list 121 b.
* * * *
Stanbury’s efforts to clinch a championship race
with Durnan have failed; the Canadian stays at Torontoand
issues challenges which he should know nobody will accept.
Towns’ deposit is still bearing no interest while waiting
for a return tussle with the champion ; but no doubt
Stanbury will agree to race the much disappointed Towns
very'- shortly. The trouble seems to be that Stanbury and
Towns are so very much ahead of the other scullers that
struggles for the title will be but periodical matches
between the two—until the old-age pension damps their
ardor.
■*■*■*■*•
But for the fact that there was rheumatism in
the air this scribe would have got drunk on Saturday
night. For the first time he saw Australian bike riders
at Sydney Cricket Ground conceding haughty Americans
a start, and the spectacle of Floyd MacFarland, the
Handicap King, receiving 15 .yards in half-a-miie from
Australians broke him up altogether. Also, it broke up
MacFarland, and he scratched rather than submit to the
indignity.
* ir
The two semi-finals and the final of the Orient
Plate were wildly exciting. The Orient Plate is a paced
mile scratch event. In the first heat Pye made a
phenomenal bound, which left G. H. Horder and
MacFarland nearly a length behind, fighting a desperate
whirling match for the second place. The second heat of
the semi-final found the riders disinclined to take the
pace, and the men were recalled and started again with
cautions. But they' loafed again till Meg-son, seeing- bis
chance, dashed away, and got a 30yd. break. Lemon,
the pacer, lay right down to it, and kicked out so
vigorously that he brought the field up with the erst-
while champion. At the scoring board Goulburn Brook
rushed to the front ; Olarke and Walker came at
him, but he broke away again, and finished
with a length to spare. Lemon’s great dash after
Megson had cut down the time, and the mile
was covered in lmin. 59sec., the last 220 yd. being done in
12see., the fastest sprint of the season. The final was
slower, but, nevertheless, more exciting. After the bell,
Brook flashed past MacFarland, Pye, and Walker, and
went for a great three-quarter-lap sprint on his lonesome.
At the members’ stand he was a good couple of lengths
ahead. MacFarland came with a magnificent effort, and,
trying to pass on the inside, touched Brook’s machine ;
but tbe N.S. Waler Hew on and crossed the line first amid
tremendous applause. The time was 2min. 4 4-ssec., and
the last 220 yd. occupied 12isec.' The Lawson Five Miles
scratch was a series of attempts by small bunches of riders
to get a break, ancl just as desperate racing on the part of
the field to prevent them. With four laps to go, Mutton,
J. Arnst, and Franks sailed away, and were leading by 40yd.
At the bell their start had been reduced to about half
that distance. The field looked as though it was going to
be left through a unanimous refusal to pace, but going
past the scoring board Megson sprinted and took the field
up to the flying three. Then there was a rush, and at the
members’ stand MacFarland came sailing through the
: ruck, past them all, one after the other, and crossed the
line first.
* * * *
Victorian cyclist A. E. Pye’s luck evaporated
with considerable suddenness. On Saturday night he
rode a great race in the semi-final of the Orient Plate, but
could get no closer than fourth to the boodle, and his
desperate rush for the Orient Plate £3O so exhausted him
that he scratched for the Five Miles. On Monday night,
in the final of the Wheel Race, worth £5O, he was handi-
capped quite out of it. With a man like 0. H. Brook
70yd. in front of him, and a gap of 40yd. between him and
George Horder, and with no assistance to cover the gap,
it was a foregone conclusion that the plucky little Vic-
torian had no hope. Still he put up a game struggle,
and at the bell he was up with, the tail of the
bunch, but was so exhausted by his effort that
he had to retire. A hundred yards past the
bell Brook sailed away, and the field had never a hope.
Brook “ walked ” in somewhere about half-a-dozen
lengths to the good. Twenty minutes later found Pye
engaged in a wild-cat struggle with Farley, who had 10yd.
start of him in the final of the Port Jackson Half-mile,
which was utterly ridiculous. Of course the champion
won—he could have done so without any handicap. The
third heat of the Port Jackson Handicap again found
MacFarland on the 15yd. mark, and again the
haughty American declined to race. However, he
came out in the Walker Five Miles scratch, and put up a
beautiful run, but Australian champion George Farley
came like a bullet at half way home. The American made
another effort at the members’ stand and drew level, but
Farley, with apparent ease, jumped clear and finished
first by nearly a lengthy It was a night of splendid
racing, although the wind interfered with the times. The
attendance was wretched, and has been right through the
meeting. Yet the racing was, on the average, far above
that of any previous meeting, and some of the finishes
were absolutely thrilling.
• * • *
George Farley is the bulkiest rider on the
Australian cycling tracks this season. Last year’s cham-
pion scales 14st., and his forward riding at the carnival
the other night gave the promise that trouble may be ex-
pected before the season is very far advanced. Farley has
almost improved out of sight as the outcome of his Con-
tinental trip.
* » * *
The Dunlop Motor Reliability Test commenced
on Tuesday last, and to-day (Thursday) the competitors
are supposed to be struggling over the 121 miles between
Albury and Gundagai (N.S.W.). Last time the Test was
held from Sydney to Melbourne H. L. Stevenson buzzed
along in front from start to finish in a huge Darracq car,
and got over the 572 miles in 23b. 42min. Later on, in a
non-stop run-off, H. Tarrant, in one of the elegant Argyll
cars, won the first prize. The present race will finish at
the Motor “ Gymkhana,” whatever that is, at the Agri-
cultural Ground, Sydney.
**'■**
Re that overloaded punt which upset on the
Saltwater River (Melbourne) on Cup day, and drowned
some people. It is alleged, as a grievous essay on the
horrors of Drink, that the publican across the river gives
an unusually large beer for 3d., and that that was the
reason why so many people wanted to get across, and
wanted to do it in such haste that about 30 of them
heaped themselves into a craft qualified for a much
smaller number. Alleged, also, that the man who ran
the punt wasn’t paid ; he was a Cup day volunteer, who
relied on the tips given him by people who were sprinting
for that unusually large threepenny beer.
-**•*-*
A dying nor’-easter made yachting on Port
Jackson on Saturday afternoon a thing to be endured by
the aid of plenty of liquid refreshment. The modern
mind yearns for pace, and yachting is only fascinating
when the winged craft go plunging through it, and
tossing the spray high and far. The Yacht Squadron was
out after the Challenge Cup, the gift of Dr. M Cormick,
which has to be won three times to be permanently
acquired. Isea has won it twice, but was not competing
on this occasion, and six others have won it once. The
only contest was between Magic and Fleetwing, from Dob-
ro.vd to Manly, and Magic got in »front and stayed
there to the finish. But, as said before, it was a tedious
affair, and required much whisky to keep competitors
afloat. Sydney Flying Squadron’s show was somewhat
more interesting, and F. S. managed to work off two heats
and a final. The light wind brought out a flock of kites,
and although the racing was slow the spectacle was first-
rate. The heat-winhers turned up in Australian and
Arawa, and the final was appropriated by Australian,
with Alert and Federal second and third.
* * * »
Balmain’s 56th annual regatta was held last
Monday, when the usual mammoth programme was faith-
fully gone through, and a dinner w&3 eaten, at which' the
usual aldermanic nonentity arose to heave dirt at
the Federal Parliament, and was not crushed by
Wilks, who replied. The afternoon brought along
a steady easterly wind, which was excellent for
the sailing events. There were yacht races, cutter races,
open boat races, all boats races, skiff and dingy contests.
Also motor-boats flashed about, decorated from mast to
water-line with palms, pot-plants and streamers. Besides
the two motor-boat races there were ten rowing events.
Altogether it was one of the brightest and best exhibi-
tions ever seen on Parramatta River—only disfigured by
Wilks and the dreadful alderman.
* * * *
Notwithstanding that the representatives of
five States were competing for the Australasian Amateur
Athletic championships in perfect weather at Sydney
Cricket Ground, on Saturday, the attendance was very
poor. The chief feature of the meeting was the upheaval
of the poor old Mother State. Even the sour, grab-all
Carruthers must have been satisfied that there was, on
this occasion, no vile Victorian conspiracy. The 100 yd.
and the Mile Walking championships passed from Bent’s
State to N.S.W., Nigel Barker winning the running and
Pomeroy the walking match. Maoriland lost the 120 yd.
Hurdles, which went to C. P. Stewart, of N.S.W. The
440 yd. and putting the weight were retained for the
Cornstalk State by Nigel Barker and W. O’Reilly. Vic-
toria managed only to retain, through G. A. Wheatley,
the one mile championship, which Harvey Sutton won
for it previously. On Monday the tide' of Cornstalk
success continued at th ■ flood. ” Pomeroy carried off the
three-mile walking championship, which had previously
been held by Barrett, of Victoria. Maoriland did
not attempt to defend its ownership of the pole-
vault championship, which fell to R. A. Adams, of
N.S.W. The 161 b. hammer premiership also went to N.S.W.
per W. O’Reilly. Victoria lost the running- broad jump
to Nigel Barker, who also retained his hold on the 220 yd.
event. Wheatley, of Victoria, won the 880 yd. event,
which had previously been held for his own State by
Harvey Sutton, and W. P. Steele succeeded in retaining
the three-miles running championship for Victoria
against Waddy of N.S.W. Victoria’s solitary gain was
the 440 hurdles, which had previously been held by
G. W. Smith, of Maoriland, and over which F. P. Brown
beat Stewart and Nigel Barker. In points N.S.W. scored
10J, Vic. 4, Queensland J, and M.L. 0.
* * * *
If appearances are anything to go by, at time of
writing Queensland is in for a most unholy drubbing at
cricket from N.S.W. The Cornstalks heaped up 691, a
record score for inter-State cricket. Mackay made 203
and Charlie Gregory 102, aed Redgrave got within six of
a century. Altogether it was an amazing slogging exhi-
bition. In reply to this the Bananalanders could only
show a miserable 132, and, of course, followed on.
As Ecclesiastes said in regard to Rugby,
“ there is no fun in this sort of thing.” It isn’t
sport. At Adelaide the match between S. Australia and
Victoria showed an inclination to follow the same lines.
S. Australia hadn’t a century about it, and left only 244
behind it, and Victoria, at the end of the second day, had
put up 292 runs for the loss of only three wickets,
M'Alister carrying his bat for 135. Armstrong, the other
not-out man, was going strong at 65 when stumps were
drawn. When S. Australia was at the wickets it was con-
sidered that the side was making a very excellent stand,
and the batsmen were reported as “defying everything.”
Now, unless Victoria has a very attenuated tail, things
look bad for the Crow-Eaters.
*’ . * ♦ *
Frank Laver predicts that both Jackson and
Fry will come'along next year with an English eleven if
the control of cricket in Australia has been “properly
settled.” Fry might join the team, but Jackson, who
has many times declined the trip, is a very unlikely
visitor. He shirked many of his county engagements last
season, pleading inability to spare the time.
■* * * »
There is more than a suspicion in N.S.W.
cricket circles that there will be a big dropping of Aus-
tralian XI. players in the inter-State fixtures for the
coming season. Personal associations have played a big
part in the N.S.W. team for many years ; but the ousting
of Noble from the selection committee is believed to be
the preliminary step to the inclusion of young players in
the team. There are hosts of young colts of great pro-
mise stamping their feet outside the State team’s door-step.
* -Jr * *
“B.B.”: Just-ended Australian XI. tour will be
the last for J. J. Kelly—the hardy stumper having yielded
to medical advice, and decided not to be a target for bowlers
henceforth in any branch of cricket. Kelli' has borne the
brunt of the wicket-keeping for Australia and N.S.W. for
many years, and appeared to be invulnerable; but the
inability of reserve-stumper Newland to withstand assault
and battery during the recent tour, left Kelly no option
but to get smashed up generally. While Kelly was in the
Australian team, all Australia was satisfied ; henceforth
there will be much inter-State wrangling in the matter.
The decayed, out-of-date game of ping-pong has
only just struck Rotorua, and tlie Maoris are in ecstasies
over it. They spend most of their ample leisure playing
“ te pinga-pong," and te pilliard saloon-keeper and his
chalky ■ employee are looking round for work. All day
long and far into the night the tappy, flippy little ball
flicks on the table amid the excited yells of brawny,
brown, bounding, bootless persons who seem to have gone
mad.
* * * •
The cast-iron r.e; * e of the unpaid magistrate.
When the persons arrested in the recent raid on a
betting-shop came before Adelaide Police court, honor-
aries to the unheard-of number of j ,i> crowded on to the
Bench to “ support ” the P.M. The Crown Solicitor
looked them over, then consulted the betting-lists seized
on the premises, and announced that two of theJ.’sP.
assembled to try the case were, though not seized in the
raid, evidently among the club’s patrons on the same
day ! The P.M. spoke firmly to the two, and they with-
drew.
* ■* * *
Gus Blanc, who enjoys the title of world’s
champion axeman, chopped a mighty lump off the pre-
vious record of lmin. 34sec. for cutting an 18in. log, at
Yarran (Vic.) t’other day, the new figures being lmin
13 2-ssec.
* tt * *
Last week Victorian law had a further set-to with
the “ gambling evil,” finishing by leaving matters in as big
a mix-up as ever. A while back Justice Hodges foolishly
held that Chow lottery tickets showed no evidence of
gambling, as they might be religious leaflets, or lessons in
moral culture, or anything at all, and the fact that cer-
tain persons planted certain blots on certain of the
characters, at a cost of 3d. or 6d., didn’t prove any
gamble. Later brother A’Beckett cordially and wisely
scoffed at this judgment, and remarked that nobody but
a lunatic would pay to be allowed to put ink-blobs on a
piece of yellow paper for the fun of the thing, and that
anyone in his right mind could see the evidence of a
lottery. Last week these two g-entlemen sat with brother
Hood as a Pull Court, and as brother Hood sided with
brother Hodges, the decision went their way, and the
convictions—with their £2O fines —were quashed and the
cases ordered to be re-heard. Thereupon the collective
Ah Sins, knowing that the Crown might produce more
detailed evidence, and mindful also of the robustness and
virility of the gentleman named Costs, casually in-
quired “How muchee?” and agreed to have their £2O
fines reduced to £5 each, the convictions being allowed to
stand. As matters dwell now, some 20 Chinkie banks
have been fined a fiver each for selling their precious
“tickets”; they still continue the game, and may be
summoned again at any moment; but as the Law has
already decided that their conviction was illegal, it may
upset any future conviction—or again it may not. _ The
Law, in the matter of gambles, seems to be getting fright-
fully uncertain as it goes along.
* * * *
The effort to revive professional pedestrianism
at the Golden Gate track in George-street, Sydney, last
week showed that the labors of the promoters are
appreciated by the public, if not by a section of the
athletes. There was a general air of cronkness sur-
rounding many of the heats, and the committee dealt
vigorously with a few of the assassins of the game. One
heat was so “strong” that it was declared “ no race,”
and three months’ disqualification (“life” would have
been more fitting) was dealt out to the runners.
As an excitement - maker there were points
about the Sturgeon-Errol scrap at Sydney National
Sporting Club last week. Errol hails from South Africa,
and Sturgeon is the man who, in the days when he was
familiarly known as “ Snowy,” was for a long time unable
to get a fight, bruisers reckoning him a good man to kstp.
clear of. The rst round of the mill was very brilliant,
Sturgeon hitting like lightning. He tried to stand off and
thump, but the man from the Chow-flogging continent
continued attempting to clinch, only to be hit on
the jaw by both hands before he could manage it.
He appealed to the referee, but the appeal
was dismissed. Then he waded in and thumped like
a machine, and Sturgeon, in his turn, was compelled
to hang on for respite. Recovering, he caug-ht the
African against the ropes and hooked him unmercifully
about the head. It was a most sensational round. Errol
pulled himself together and gave Sturgeon a very rough
time till the bell went. The third round saw the African
rush in. Snowy backed off, but was too slow. A left
hook caught him on the jaw, and a right swing hit him
■on the back of the head, and a wild-cat strugg'le ensued,
at the end of which Errol tried to clinch. But Sturg-eon
worked his arms free, and rained a shower of blows that
sent Errol to the floor, where he was counted out.
* * * *
At Sydney Gaiety Club on Saturday night Mr.
Green, of Balmain, shook hands with Mr. Ladbury, of
London, Bow Bells, England. For the first seven rounds
it looked as though the fight, like the brook, would go on
for ever. As a boxer Ladbury was distinctly superior,
and he continued to land with grace and deftness on the
Balmain dial. His left fell regularly and softly as the dew
from heaven, Green retaliating with hooks and body
swings. Ladbury’s smacks lacked power, but they kept
Green off. Each succeeding round was warmer than the
previous one ; both youths worked harder, but neither
seemed to be able to inflict any damage ; and it looked as
though it was going to be a test of boilers and
nothing more. In the eighth round, however, Lad-
bury stood in close and brought a heavy, resounding
right on to Green’s jaw. The man from Balmain heeled
over like a ferry steamer at a boat race. Then the
Londoner threw his caution to the winds and rushed in to
settle the Australian, and was astounded to meet a right
and left swing fair on the jaw. He sagged at the knees,
shock and astonishment seemed to paralyse him, and
then another right struck the same spot, and he took
five seconds’ respite on the floor. Then he was up and
down again for another five seconds; and after that he
was up and down again for six seconds. His increasing
down ness was noticeable. Next Mr. Green tapped
him heavily on the stomach and the jaw, and Mr. Ladbury
went down altogether, leaving' Green still bantam
champion.

Ex-Senator John Downer seems to be
gradually assuming the leadership of the Conserva-
tive party in the S.A. Council, Squatter Duncan
dropping into the background. The parties in
the Chamber are divided thus: Chief-Secretary
Kirkpatrick and blacksmith Bice are on the side
of the Government, while arrayed against them
are (counting the President) 16 National Ass
votes. The prospects of progress are therefore
not bright.

The defence in the Pinkenba (Q.) case, in
which Samuel Hannah and Sarah Howden were
charged, at the Brisbane Supreme Court, with the
murder of the latter’s husband, had been just
entered upon. Hannah was in the witness-box—
his last chance of defence—when the strains of
slow music were heard in the street outside the
court-house. It was the Dead March that was
being played in front of a Salvation Army officer’s
cortege. The court was shrouded in the dark
n ess of an approaching storm, the rumble of
which could be heard in the distance. Justice
Heal sat up straighter than ever—silent, listening,
meditating. Examining counsel sought to break
the spell by proceeding with a question just as tha
accused vvitness broke down in the box and the
woman in the dock swooned. The Judge stretched
out his hand ; then, as the sound or the dirge
round a street corner, he said:
(gentlemen, I did not anticipate this. Adjourn
the court till to-morrow morning.”
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has promised,
in a casual sort of way, to drop a large part
of his Old Age Pensions Bill. It is an iniquitous
Bill, and Carruthers isn’t strong even in his in-
iquity. Also when the Upper House demanded
that the farmer and pastoralist, who paid local
rates under the new Local Government scheme,
should be exempted from land and income taxes
(though the city manufacturer has to pay all
three), the weak Carruthers caved in such haste
that he passed the Exemption Bill through the
Assembly without waiting to be sure that the
Upper House wouldn’t reject his Local Govern-
ment Bill after all. Then he started to denounce
the Australian Government with unusual viru-
ifnce for proposing to get its stamps printed in
Adelaide in the interests of economy. Every time
the weak Carruthers doubles up like a wet rag on
his State policy, he tries to retrieve his character
as a strong man by denouncing the Australian
Government.
At the recent sitting of the High Court,
at Sydney, barrister Gordon rose and, in an
injured voice, complained to the Bench that there
was actually no gratis foolscap on the table for
the Bar to make notes upon. All the other
learned gentlemen present looked deeply hurt,
and one member of the Bench was impressed with
the profundity of the grievance, and remarked
that the Department was conducted with “ex-
treme economy;” though why on earth the
Federal taxpayer should be expected to buy
foolscap so that highly-paid barristers can scrawl
caricatures of their Honors upon it, while waiting
fora “learned friend” to conclude his address*
only the acutely-trained legal intellect can under-
stand. Of course, if Gordon, K.C., really cannot
afford a penny notebook, the Government might
charitably give him a bit of paper occasionally to
draw pot-hooks upon—provided that Gordon,
K.C., looked becomingly grateful for the kindness!
But when a rich barrister is begging for paper he
should be respectful, like any other person who
wants something without payment.

THE SUGAR BONUS.
The daily newspapers of Sydney have been so
busy of late discussing the appalling injustice
that has been inflicted upon the poor old Mother
State by the decision of the Australian Govern-,
ment to get its stamps printed in Adelaide at 2£d.
per 1000 instead of in Sydney at 7£d. per 1000, that
they haven’t had time to draw public attention to
trifling little matters like the progress of white
labor in the sugar industry. A report, recently
presented to the House of Representatives by Dr.
Maxwell, contains figures which appear to dis-
prove conclusively the old but oft-repeated yarn
that sugar can’t be grown by white labor in
Queensland. But while the stamp-printing busi-
ness is on deck, and the rights of the poor old Mother
State have to be preserved, even at the risk of
secession, it can hardly be expected that the
newspapers should find space for the dis-
cussion of points which involve nothing but
a mere matter of national policy, on which
the future of one of the largest Australian
industries depends. If only someone could dis-
cover in Dr. Maxwell’s report something which
threatened a new “hardship” on the Mother
State, this thick blanket of indifference would
lift. The Daily Telegraph would print leading
articles darkly hinting that secession was not so
impossible as some people thought; old gentle-
men in the Legislative Council would call upon
the heroic J. Carruthers to rise up in defence of
State rights, and put an end to this intolerable
burden of injustice and oppression; and .T. Car-
ruthers himself would tell stupid members of
Parliament, in answer to asinine questions, that
the Government viewed the suggestions of Dr.
Maxwell with grave apprehension, and every
care would be taken by the Government to con-
serve the rights of the poor old Mother State.
But though Dr. Maxwell’s report can’t be
twisted anyhow into a threat of injustice to the
poor old Mother State, it is an important and
interesting document. It proves that the people
who said that the sugar industry could not be
maintained without Kanaka labor were absolutely
wrong. And, although the daily newspapers
don’t seem to think so, that is a matter of immense
importance to Australians; and even if all this
country’s stamps were printed on Wild Horse
Plains in S.A. it would matter little by com-
parison.
r • * * *
The policy of charging a reduced excise on
sugar grown by white labor was adopted in 1902,
with the avowed object of encouraging sugar
growers to get rid of the Kanaka. Dr. Maxwell’s
report proves—so far as three years’ experience
can prove—that that policy was sound and states-
manlike. In 1902 there were 1421 white growers,
cultivating an area of 36,538 acres. In 1905 there
were 2681 white growers, with 72,606 acres. The
total number of sugar growers in Queensland is
3422, and 78 per cent, of these employ only white
labor. Of course, the great majority of white
growers, and the greater part of the area culti-
vated by whites, are naturally in the southern
and central districts. But the most striking
feature in the report is —that by far the greatest
relative increase in the employment of white labor
has taken place in the northern district, the very
fortress of black labor. In 1902 there were only
36 white growers in this district, cultivating an
area of 1882 acres. To-day th ?re are 124 white
growers, with 5024 acres. This rate of increase is
nearly, if not quite, double that which has occurred
in the southern and central districts. Sugar can

Unfortunately, this is not the wnoie story, in
spite of the fact that 78 per cent, of the growers
employ only white labor, Dr. Maxwell points
out that the other 22 per cent.. who employ black
labor and who represent nearly all large growers,

sugarproduced in Queensland. 1 nese large growers,
of courseware hanging on to their beloved Kanaka
till the last minute, in the hope that something
v turn UP They are doing this, as Dr.
may vu F- Rnnrlabere- district
Maxwell shows, even in “laberg a at ot,
where it has been proved beyond all question
that white men can and will do the work, unaer
these circumstances they will hardly expect much
svmnathv from Parliament if, when the time
comes for them to part with their Kanakas, they
have a difficulty in finding, on the instant, white
men ready to take their places. Perhaps it is
hardly right to say that they won’t expect
sympathy; it is safer to say that they won’t get
it. This, however, is by the way. If nothing
“ turns up ” to save the Kanaka before the end of
2906 _ an d at present there seems to be mighty
little chance of anything of that kind “ turn-
ing up ” —the growers who are continuing
to°use black labor will have no one but themselves
to thank if they find themselves in sudden diffi-
culty. Probably the very useful Bills introduced
by the Government to increase the excise and the
rebate, and thus to decrease the Protection
afforded to black-grown sugar, will afford these
gentlemen a useful reminder.
Westralian Land Settlement.
Westralia is making great efforts to attract im-
migrants from England, but it is difficult to
reconcile the alluring pictures presented by lec-
turers with eloquence and magic lantern slides,
and the blank depressing reality as set forth by
Ministers in some of their more candid moments.
For instance, this was almost the last jeremiad of
the last ex-Minister for Lands :
During September 40 homestead blocks have been for-
feited. This means practically the loss of £240 in survey
fees, to say nothing of the fact that these blocks have
been locked up from settlement for the past three years.
. . . Numbers of conditional purchase blocks have also
been taken up and forfeited.
Which means that you can put almost any kind
of people on to almost any kind of land, but it
takes more than lectures and lantern slides to
make them stay there. The lot of Westralian
selectors does not appear to be particularly happy,
unless their holdings chance to be on one of the
few favored spots along the Great Southern line.
There is some really good land in that region, but
it is nearly all locked up by land agents, Crown
land inspectors and their friends, who have got in
first. One official drawing £4OO per annum owns
over 4000 acres within a mile or two of a big rail-
way centre, and as each acre is worth £4 to £5
the State might reasonably give him permanent
leave to look after his estate. In the Bankruptcy
Court at Perth recently, a man who had been in-
duced to go on the land said he only had £l2 to
start with, but he got money from the State Agri-
cultural Bank on the security of his unpaid-for
conditional purchase. Then he thought had
invented a new air-ship, and went off to England
to make a big pot of money out of it. He made a
breakage instead, and sorrowfully confessed his
inability to pay anything in the pound. He has
had the Agricultural Bank’s money and the bank
has the land, but as the land belonged to the State
in the first instance taxpayers may well ask where
they come in. There is one way to promote a
little real settlement in Groperland—to cut up
such good land as there is among real settlers
instead of letting John Forrest’s friends keep it
in big areas mostly unused, but that prospect, to
those gentlemen, is too horrible for words.
A Coal Tragedy.
“ Ku-Klux,” from a Victorian coal-mining town :
My first view of Outtrim called to mind most forcibly
the old Bible verse—“ A city that is set upon a hill and
cannot be hid.” The township is built on hillsides
surrounding a basin in such a way that no privacy (in the
day time) is possible. Mrs. Smith, washing in her back-
yard on one hillside, can hear Mrs. Brown, half-a-mile
away on the opposite hillside, making audible comments
on the quality of her window-curtains. There is little, I
am told, to identify Outtrim of the past. In the old days,
money flowed freely, all men were friends, and the town
waxed fat. That was in the days when the curse of
Unionism was rampant in the land. Now all things are
changed. Men come and go (mostly go), and the town
is dead. There are, I am told, 175 empty houses in
Outtrim. 1 counted 105 myself, and then got tired of
climbing hills, so I take the figures as given.
Money is scarce—so scarce that the local boardinghouse-
keeper waits at the mine on pay-days for the new class
of coal-miner, who has a painful habit of departing with-
out saying good-bye. There are three classes of men
here First, the remnants of the men who were locked out
—only a mere handful now, but all men who had homes
which they were unable to sell, and so had to stay
ou under the new conditions. Second come the original
blacklegs. Their numbers are also fast diminishing. They
seem to have been a cross between a Flying Angel and a
Bourke-street Rat. At any rate, after their work was
done the mine management soon found an excuse to sack
o£ th - The t fc ird )ot are men who came here after
fche lock . out was over—respectable, hard-working men,
mos t of them, to whom the deceptive offer of 9s. a day
se emed a fortune. They came to find that if they worked
hard they might earn, after tools, oil, and explosives were
paid for, about 4s. 6d. to 6s. a day. The average is more
DRUNK AGAIN.
DEADBRORE (after he, has partaken) “ Better chalk these down, Miss!"
HEBE (with some heat): “Chalk drinks down to you Not much. Don’t do things
like that, my man.”
D RATIBRORE: “ Just as you please. But you might forget them.”
like 4s. 6d. These tools, oil, &c., are all supplied by the
companies at high prices. A new-comer is put on to
work, and supplied with pick and shovel, lamp, oil, &c.
He works, say, four days, and then gets the sack—he
probably never saw a coal mine before in his life. He has
to dispose of his tools, so he sells them to the company ; of
course, after four days’ work they are nearly worn out,
and he only gets just what the company thinks fit to give
—which is not much. And he probably departs in debt
to some unfortunate boardinghouse-keeper. And the
company sells his tools over again to the next fool. I met
two men yesterday who had worked five shifts and had
drawn lid. from the.company. Not exceptional cases,
either.
The men who came since the lock-out all regard
the blackleg with contempt. Indeed, amongst the
blacklegs themselves there are a few who see to what
a rotten state they have brought the Victorian coal-
miner’s lot. The local business man talks of
the awful tyranny of the Unions, and then in the
next breath proceeds to deplore the present state of the
town and compare it with Outtrim of the old days, when
almost every man owned his own house (there are not
many such in Outtrim at present), and all had money to
sptnd and spent it freely.
And now the houses stand empty, and the fences are
broken down, and the trail of the blackleg is over it all.
But men look hopefully in the direction of Melbourne,
whence vague rumors drift up of a Royal Commission
appointed by somebody to inquire into something.
♦
George Graham, M.L.A. (Victoria), has been
associated with agriculture all his life, and repre-
sents a farming electorate. He is one of the most
solid Conservatives of the Vic. Assembly, and
nobody would accuse him for a moment of being
influenced by the Trades Hall or any other radical
institution. Yet listen to what George has to say
about State ownership of mail steamers, based on
his acquaintance with the export trade and his ex-
perience as a member of the Butter Commission.
On Thursday, he was examined before the Select
Committee on State-owned steamers, and here is
a piece of his evidence
Prom the inception of the butter industry the mail
companies have looked after No. 1, and extracted from
the producer as much as they could get. . . . They have
been a great bar to the dairying industry. Exorbitant
freights were demanded.
The Chairman : Did you personally ask the manager of
the P. and O. Companv'to carry butter at id. per lb. ?
Yes, I did ; but he replied that the mail companies were
carrying at bedrock prices, and that even then, when they
were charging |d. per lb., there was a loss. At that very
time the companies were secretly arranging with a com-
mittee appointed in Victoria for another contract for
three years at fd. per lb.
By whom was the committee appointed ?—lt was sup-
posed to have been appointed at the request of the
Director of Agriculture, but, reading between the lines,
we, as a commission, saw that it was working in the
interests of the mail companies, with a view of getting
another contract before the report of the Commission
came out.
What freight do you think the Commonwealth Govern-
ment could guarantee per annum ?—I think £300,000.
Can you suggest a remedy for the present position ? —I
think the surest remedy would be for the Government to
carry our produce by water as well as by land. At pre-
sent it is brought by rail to the seaboard, and then it is
at the mercy of the shipping combine and the middlemen.
No doubt some will say that it would be outrageous
for the Government to get ships of its own, but when
we consider that upwards of £130,000,000 is invested in
the railways of the Commonwealth, it is surely not too
much to ask that £5,000,000, or even £10,000,000, should
be spent in carrying our produce and taking our mails
over the ocean.
Do you think it would be a financial success ?—I feel
certain it would. Moreover, the ships would be a training
ground for the navy, and in time of war they could be
used as auxiliary cruisers.
♦
Melbourne City Corporation has added to itself
the municipalities of North Melbourne and
Flemington and Kensington. This is a step in
the direction of a Greater Melbourne scheme,
which is to take under one central administration
the whole of the metropolitan area, at one time
divided into 22 municipalities. Melbourne City
is notoriously wealthy, and, though it calls it by
another name, notoriously socialistic. It owns
baths, electric lighting, markets, abattoirs, its
own insurance fund, and a host of other things,
all of which return a profit, and form splendid
arguments for the socialist. In the Melbourne
City Council there is not, however, one Labor
member, and anti-Socialism smiles contentedly at
the socialistic schemes of members of its own
party, without knowing that they are socialistic.
The hated Labor element is going to come into
the City Council with a rush now, however.
Prendergast, leader of the State Opposition, is
Mayor of North Melbourne, and certain to be
returned to the council. Stephen Barker, the
secretary of the Trades Hall Council, is another
certainty. Dobson, the Mayor of Flemington
and Kensington, was last year president of the
Trades Hall Council. Altogether, the City Coun-
cil will receive as strong an injection of real red-
hot national Socialism as it is possible to give it,
and it may be expected to go far in the socialising
of industry and business.
In The Outlook (London) for July 29 and
August 5 Richard Jebb, known to Australis-
readers for his able and comprehensive “Studies
in Colonial Nationalism,” puts forward a good
case for an Imperial Telegraph system, owned by
the various Governments within the Empire. He
argues:
A cable monopoly, like the railway monopolies in the
United States, wiil continue to wield an irresistible
weapon in differential rates, secret or open, against
which democracy and its legislators may rage in vain.
In this age of combination, the perpetuation of the cable
monopoly in private hands, together with the encourage-
ment of the Press by means of a lower scale of rates,
would lead ultimately to the control of newspapers by a
cable ring, and the throttling of the genuine expression
of public opinion throughout the Empire. It is alleged
even now that the big newspapers in Australia have com-
bined in an arrangement with the cable companies which
effectively closes the field to new journals. The rumor
gains color from the unnatural hostility which has been
manifested by a section of the Australian press towards
the Pacific cable.
That view will be promptly accepted in Australia,
which suffers now seriously from two evils : igno-
rance as to the affairs of the great world outside—
the news supplied by a press monopoly acting
with a cable monopoly being meagre and some-
times distorted ; and ignorance as to the Common-
wealth in Great Britain, which is mostly supplied
with Australian news from Stinking Fish sources.
Premier Bent, of Victoria, has periodical attacks
of Socialism. It is true that the Tory press and
the Tory members of Parliament generally
succeed in curing him before anything socialistic
really happens ; but still some of his schemes may
materialise, and then Torydom will stand aghast
at the work of its servant. Bent s latest
proposal—a nebulous proposal, so far —is
a tax on unimproved land values. He
lately announced in the House that.he intended to
place before the Cabinet a revision of the whole
system of taxation. He had seen 40 miles of
country near Melbourne, only 500 acres of which
were cultivated. The owners of this land were
going to do a great deal when they got a rail-
way. They got it, but still did nothing. One
estate the Government had purchased —Over-
newton —paid only 2s. 6d. per £lOO in land
tax. He wanted to alter that sort of thing
and lighten the burden in other directions.
These are brave words, and if Bent can only
screw himself up to sticking point he may do
something notable yet in addition to his one great
performance of reducing the public debt. Swin-
burne is the only man of character in the Cabinet,
and he is much more advanced in his ideas than
Bent. The others will follow Thomas to the
gates of Hades, so long as by so doing they can
keep their portfolios. Where the new idea will
meet with opposition will be from the proputty
class in both Houses. Manifold and Co., in the
House of Landlords, will, of course, raise a shriek
of indignant protest; but that sort of treatment
doesn’t affect Bent. It's when the enemy gets
him by the ear far away from the public eye that
he crumples up. Bent in public is heroic, and if
he could always be kept in public, and be made to
even go to bed in public, he might do things.
But in private conference his stomach turns to
woter, and all his great plans melt away into
nothingness.
♦
“Puck” on the subject of Australian defence: —
How General Hutton must have laughed to himself at
his Australian masters ! His scheme of organisation was
drawn up, not for the defence of Australia, but to provide
an expeditionary force to supplement the British army ;
and he made no secret of his views in his report (19021.
Big.toiin Forrest, protesting to the Reps, hy All his gods
this should not be, left Australia to its fate (and Hutton)'
while he hastened to the Coronation grovel.
The expeditionary scheme, which was then adopted as it
stood, provides the defence organisation of to-day, while
Parliament remains blissfully unconscious of the fact.
When Parliament insisted on retrenchment Hutton
reduced the Garrison Artillery, a purely defensive force,,
till hardly enough men were left to clean the guns]; but
the expeditionary forces have to-day a stronger war-
footing than was ever contemplated by the original
scheme. Hutton knew what he wanted, and took care-
he got it. Australia, which paid his salary, knew what,
it didn’t want, but it got what it didn’t want all the same..

HALF-YEARLY REPORT of the DIRECTORS of THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA,
With the Accounts for the Half- Year to 10th APRIL, 1905. Presented to the Proprietors at the Half-Yearly General
Meeting* held on THURSDAY, sth OCTOBER, 1905.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance Sheet as at 10th April last, with the Profit
Account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and
Colonial rates and taxes, and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half year amounted
to £136,152135. lid. , , , , • o
To this sum must be added £16,951 ss. brought forward from the previous half-year, making a
total of £153,103 18s. lid., out of which the Directors have declared a dividend for the half-year at the
rate of 12 per cent, per annum, or £2 Bs. per share, free-of income tax
The Dividend will absorb £96,000, and the Directors have further appropriated £30,000 to the
Reserve Fund, and £lO,OOO in reduction of the cost of Bank Premises. There will then remain
£17,103 18s. lid. to be carried forward to the next account. ...
The dividend will be payable, in London and in the Colonies, on the 6th October next.
The Directors have, with much regret, to record the unexpected retirement through ill-health of
Mr. John Sawers, the Superintendent since 1887 of the Colonial Establishments of the Bank. As the
Proprietors are aware, the Directors have always placed the highest value on the services of JVLr.
Sawers, and the termination of his connection with the'Bank is sincerely regretted.
Since the date of the last Report the Bank has opened new Branches at Crow’s Nest, m Queens-
land, and at Eltham and Temuka, in New Zealand, and has closed its Branches at Ravenswood, in
Queensland, and Lennonville, in Western Australia. n . rir ,„.,,t •
4 Thread needle-street, London, 25th September, 1905. L- SANDERSON, Chairman.

“Robert Hap,” on the innards of the
community:—
It is not good to be “on the lodge.” If a person really
wants a start along- the uneven path to the Necropolis,
let him buy a medical work and keep it handy. Then,
when the pork disagrees with him, he can look up the
book and study the symptoms of every disease, and finally
decide that he hasn’t long- to live. After that it is quite
easy to make his relatives and friends wish he had an even
shorter time to live. A healthy man joins a lodge because
he fancies that some day an illness will overtake him.
The rest is easy. Continuous good health and the mono-
tony of paying the regular weekly subscription for four
or five years bring on an attack of what is known as
“lodge patient’s hypochondria.”
Women who live in mean streets are very fond of
lodges. They “pay in” in the hope of one day having
to call in the doctor for some trivial disorder. They
imagine that a doctor’s motor-cycle at the front door
lends tone, and that his visit brings the family under
sympathetic discussion (although some neighbors are
nasty 7 enough to withhold sympathy 7 till after the funeral).
People who don’t belong to lodges wonder why a
doctor bothers with a lodge patient when he knows he
isn’t really ill. These people should know that the
doctor hasn’t a say 7 in the matter. The member says
he is ill, and there’s an end of it. And if the doctor
doesn’t write something' in Latin in the patients hook,
his discourtesy is made the subject of debate at the
next lodge meeting, and at the end of the year he may
find himself minus his lodge practice—and by a majority
of one vote. In this way, healthy beings are being
transformed into human drug repositories, if not victims
of the drug habit. I)r. Holmes said, “If the materia
medica was sunk in the sea, it would be all the better for
man and all the worse for the sea.” But when people can
obtainas much medicine as they wish for the sum of Is.,
paid weekly, there is little hope of the whole materia
medica finding its harmless, necessary grave.
‘‘Nulla”: In about eight months time
200,000 acres on the Dorrigo and Camboyne
(N.S.W.), mostly covered with fine timber, will
be thrown open. Then, before the selector’s axe
and firestick, timber with a present and prospec-
tive value of hundreds of thousands of pounds
will make way for the straggling ears of corn and
the animal Bent raises his hat to. The bush
must retreat before settlement, but with a sen-
sible land-tax there would be more than enough
arable land for years to come without the neces-
ity of destroying valuable forests.

“Mirumbeek”: The legend lingers even in
the city that the average jeweller takes the good jewels
of a watch movement and puts inferior ones in their
stead. After 30 years at the trade, me say I never
found a jewel set in a watch yet that would repay me
to remove just in order to replace it with another. Nor
are gems often stolen from rings and brooches, as some
believe. The gems in 99 out of 100 articles of jewellery
worn nowadays are not worth giving a dishonest thought
to.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“ Two Seas” : As I pay-1 s. 4d. per lb. for Paris
green, with postage added, I would be glad to hear from
“ F.C.” (B. 5/10/’O5) the address of the country storekeeper
who sells it at 6d. It strikes me that storekeepers are
not allowed to sell poisons now, as they used to—not even
bluestone and arsenic, of which country stores supplied
large quantities until the chemists found a way of sup-
pressing the trade in their own interests. No doubt
-Barabbas added drug-selling to his flourishing land-agency
business; but, all the same, I have never noticed the
uniformity of chemists’ charges that “F.C.” mentions.
My latest experience of the cost of one prescription was :
Made up in Sydney Is. (id., suburbs Is. 9d., country town
(on railway line) 2s. 6d. To the latter I ejaculated,
“ Whaffor'i” and he knocked off 6d. without a murmur.
A business that will allow its owner to knock 20 per cent,
-off his price with a smile might appeal to Barabbas. Yet
even a chemist doesn’t keep as many motor-cars as his
brother in the drapery business.

“JE.W. A.” : Re the automatic stoker (B. 8/6/’O5).
It may interest your readers to know that at the Ultimo
(Sydney) tram power-house 36 large boilers are stoked by
iron “men.” Also all the work of coal-trimming (about
250 tons per diem), and emptying the ash-pits is done by
the same ‘ persons.” [But it was a marine stoker that
was called for ; practically all up-to-date land works use
the land-boiler variety.]
Adelaide is a go-as-you-please hamlet. When
there is a rush on one of the suburban tram lines a super-
annuated extra vehicle is put on, and there isn’t suf-
ficient staff to man the extra car. So the ancient trap
does without a conductor, and when the driver goes round
collecting fares, the passengers take turns at driving.
* * * *
“F.” writes: “ Melbourne Age published, the
other day, a letter from ‘ A Disgusted Australian ’ re
the Stinking Fish tactics of a shopman at a local
softgoods store to whom he had applied for a piece
of Victorian tweed and a hat which wasn’t imported.
The writer stated that he was warned against the
tweed on the allegation that it ‘ loses color directly.’
Also the hat—-of a popular Vic. brand—was derided as a
thing which ‘ turns green in the box,’ whereupon the man
in favor of national industries went elsewhere for his;
goods. Assuming that the miserable counter-jumper
in this case was an imported person who cried
‘ Stinking Fish ’ without authority from his bosses,
the fact remains that every retail trader whose interests
are chiefly ‘wrapped up’ in packages of foreign goods
is a natural enemy to the local producers. Moreover,
he is a more dangerous enemy than the wholesale im-
porter, for he has the ear of the purchasing public
and he breathes the doctrines best calculated to
meet his bills. Customers are not likely to hear
any good of Australian manufactures at any shop
which has no considerable connection with local
factories, and which has no business purpose to
serve in creating new demands for Australian products.”
[The Bulletin has heard the same tale in almost the same
words in Sydnev. All the same the editor is pleased to
say that his present suit of Marrickville tweed is holding
out much better than an English-made article that he
recently had to buy in a foreign land, and at £4 15s. it
wasn’t ruinously expensive. But he has been driven to
change his tailor to get away from the moan that Aus-
tralian cloth “ won’t keep its color.”]

A few nicks from “ Ideas for Inventors,” by G.
G. Turri, of Melb. Harvey got £lO,OOO, in one cheque, for
his invention for making aerated water. Norman Miles, the
inventor ofperforatedpostage stamp edges, got £5,000. The
gum at the back brought £4,000 to Jacob Morris, another
inventor. Thousands of pounds await men or women who
can invent something to prevent the unauthorised re-
filling of bottles, a music leaf turner, anti-fraud cheque
book, ditto scrip, anti-fraud envelope, convertible furni-
ture, combined letter-sheets and envelopes, steamship
buffer, safety purses and pocket-books, gas-burner not to
waste, removable leaf ledgers, electric light regulator,
railway ticket cases, cork extractor, ice machine for £2,
stamp'affixers. Turri says he lias in hand an American
automatic telephone. Browne, of Sheffield, a poor cutler’s
apprentice, invented the railway buffers. The money-,
counting machine counts coins and wraps them in pack-
ages. A girl can thus count and wrap 25,000 coins per
hour. Westinghouse, as a mechanic, tried in vain to sell his
brake for £6OO. In 12 years he made out of it £4,000,000.

“Two Seas”: I bought five acres of land at
£3 3s. per acre, and after transfer was completed received
an offer to purchase at £5 per acre. Had the acres been
bullocks I should have jumped at the offer, having no
lawyer to reckon with ; but being land I had to decline to
deal because the cost of transfer had added £2 per acre to
my purchase. I can buy £20,000 worth of beef without
being compelled to call in one of the accursed tribe of
lawyers—but not an acre of land.

Another industry has started in Sydney. A
bottle-oh parades with a hot pie can in his cart, crying
“ ’Ot pies for empty bottles,” and doing a roaring trade.
One hopeful was seen streaking out of the house with a
bottle half-full of pickles. He emptied the pickles into
the gutter before dealing for a hot blob of mystery.

“ Drifter ” : Said “ Bill the Spinner ” “ Two-
up is the fairest game in the world. lam fifty quid
behind it because I haven’t the biains to stick to my
system. No ! It’s simplicity itself! You can work it
either in quids or shillings. F’rinstance, say I have three
quid ? I split it up inter thirty two-bob pieces an’ put it
in a box. That’s my bank. Well, I take two bob each
night. I slip inter the school early as possible—l notice
the raids are generally made with the staff of city traps
that have just been relieved at 10 o’clock —have my bet
and get out lively. Say I win ! That’s four bob. I leave
it on the mat and give it another chance. I win again.
That’s eight. Then again—sixteen. Now I go for the
last—that’s a thirty-two-bob win. Then I’ve got my call.
Four bets running and you win back losses for 15 nights.
If you can’t win four running in fifteen tries it’s because
your luck stinks. Well ! I button up on twenty-five
bob and give the five a chance. You .mustn t be
afraid to bet. When you have a bit of velvet to the
good make it hot. When I win the few quid I make my
mistake. I feel rich and start to knock it down ; have a
night out or so, and p’raps splash into a new suit of
clothes. It goes ; blime ! how it does go ! Then my bank
gets nothing to make up losses. It’s the best system
possible if a man will only stick to it. I have been
studying it for years, and sifted it down to that system
as the best chance. Blime! I’ll be at ’em withitagan
when I raise a few quid ! ” And the sphnner tossed two
invisible coins in the air, and they fell to the floor and
won him an imaginary heap of gold.
THE REJECTED BABY.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Wanted, a couple without encumbrance.” —Common
advertisement.
“ A baby’s feet, like sea-shells pink.”
So sang the poet.
And mothers used to. read and think,
“ How could lie know it ? ”
Those were the days wh;n babies re'gne f '
Quite undisputed,
For they who the reverse maintained
Were soon confuted.
The stork was sel lom from the door,
The cradle idle.
Falk never thought that having more
Was suicidal. y 1
The creed that taught this course was wise
Is n j .. recanted,
And people boldly advertise
“ Babies not wanted.”
We kneel before the gods of gold,
And sing their praises,
Like Israel in days of oil,
With heart-felt phrases.
We sacrifice the hopes, the dreams,
% Tiie future, maybe ;
The heart, the brain—and now, it seems,
They claim the baby.

“ Two Seas ” on a land-subiect :
,
Here is a point worth noting by intending buyers of
settlement leases. The holder of S.L. taken up before
the new Act can get his term of 28 years extended to 40
years, subject to periodical re-appraisements. A settle-
ment lease .not brought under the 1903 Act must be re-
appraised for the remainder of the 28 years whenever it
is transferred to a new owner. Thus it is worth no more
than the value of the improvements on it, plus its advan-
tages as a going concern. An actual case in point will
show how this works out. A squatter in the North
wished to purchase a S.L. for his son, and the vendor
applied to the Board for the necessaiy permission to
transfer (before the 1903 Act). The land was very good
and the rent low. The price was £2OOO, but they couldn’t,
by any amount of stretching, make the improvements
worth more than £lOOO. It was evident, therefore, that
the buyer was paying £lOOO solely on account of the low
lioUq i h T rfee j >?y Hie State. Had the land been under the
1903 Act and liable for re-appraisement; the price paid by
the buyer would have been evidence in favor of fixing the
rental at double or treble the amount previously placed
upon it. Quite right, too, from the State’s point of view
as landlord. But how different is this re-appraisement of
S.L.’s to the tender treatment meted out to the condi-
tional purchasers when they transfer \ But the big man
is not, as a rule, interested in S.L.’s, which makes all the
difference.
