“ Vigilans ” sums up concerning the Terri-
tory which the Commonwealth will have to take
in hand before long :
The true causes of the decadence of the Northern
Territory are “ cumshaw ” —the toll paid by the yellows
to the whites—and dummying for Chows. As long as
these pernicious habits continue the country will
stagnate. Add to these, immense areas held for specula-
tive purposes, and bad management of the mines, and
you have the thing in a nutshell.
Bennet Burleigh on the Japanese, in his
recently published “ Empire of the East, or Japan
and Russia at War, 1904-5” :
The commercial morality of the Japanese is a non-
negotiahle cipher. Ask any British merchant who knows
them, and let him answer. They pirate nearly all our
wares copying them even to the names of the firms and
trade-marks, and unblushingly sell these goods in their
open markets, and foreigners have taken no change out
of their law courts. “ Superior Sheffield cutlery,” “ Hunt-
ley and Palmer’s biscuits,” and “ Hubbuck’s paints,” and
so on, and on ad nauseam. All labelled and trade-
marked, yet but contemptible cheats made without war-
rant in Japan. Shoals of verifying cases can be cited.
And we are, as allies, to take these people at their word,
that they will spend a penny or rally to our aid in the
event of certain contingencies?
“ Squidge,” about a woman :
The brassy cheek of woman passeth all understanding.
I was waiting in a big drapery store in Melbourne, when
a tall, blonde woman strode in, and, with a scornful
glance at the girl behind the counter, demanded “A
knot of white tape ! ” The girl got the tape, wrapped it
up, and made out a bill for onepenee. Then the blonde
lady undid a multitude of purses and bags and extracted
a crossed cheque for £B. This she hurled on the counter,
as if it were a base aspersion which she repudiated with
bitterness. The Miss picked it up and meekly carried it
off to the cashier. Presently she came back. “This
cheque is crossed. You’ll have to endorse it, please,” she
said. But the blonde vision regarded her like Oaesar
looking at a microbe. “ No,” she said, “ I don’t have to
sign. Pa told me it was unnecessary.” She wrangled
over the matter for five minutes, and then grumblingly
affixed her name, got her £7- 19s. lid., did not forget her
knot of tape, and strode out into the street. _Oould a
man have done that ? And if he could, is there a man’s
clothing shop that would put up with him ?
Australia would be a great country if it
had as much grit as it has sand —the grit to pay
for works out of revenue, to tax the loafing land-
lord either out of existence or into activity, and to
put up the Customs tariff high enough to shut out
every foreign article which Australia can make for
itself. In fact, it is because it has so much sand
that it needs this much grit.
“L.P.N.” : A Sunday-school teacher in a
suburban portion of Kalgoorlie was dilating on
the Biblical story of David’s unholy love for Uriah
the Hittite’s wife, and .asked the question : Now
can any of you tell me where Uriah was when
David came to his house and took his wife?”
“On night shift!” replied the son and heir of a
local miner.

The Ballad of Mary.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HER rose-leaved progress held a throng
Of men who strove her smiles to win ;
The world rejoiced in wine and song
That Mary came to help it sin.
The Euxine knew her love succinct;
To madness was Massilia hurled
Before her passion ; so she linked
A chain of kisses round the world.
Stern Sparta fell, its curbs forswore
To gratify her ways and whims;
Cold Corinth’s marble glowed before
The fire that moved her lips and limbs.
The silken vows of Athens earned
The sweet dominion of her thrall;
With summer fervor she returned
The rude embraces of the Gaul.
To her each haughty captain bare
His head, to patient wait her beck
And wind her strands of amber hair
As shackles round his willing neck.
By poets round the tideless sea
In passioned verse her praise was sung ;
She paid their alien minstrelsy
In love that talks in every tongue.
Where domes of mighty temples shone.
The priests ignored their gods’ behests
To offer votive kisses on
The domes that were her gleaming breasts.
One day the stadium saw her woo
Some gladiator bold and brown,
With teasing chase until he threw
His net to bring her laughing down.
The next, her blue mesmeric eyes
Hose to the consul’s throne above,
Or thrilled some chief, to signalise
The great democracy of love.
Gay, irresponsible and free,
Capricious as the warming wind,
Whose rhythmic ripples swayed the sea,
Impenitent she sinned and sinned.
At last, the world grew dull and dead,
The homing instinct burst to fire,
What time her seaman lover spread
His painted sails and steered for Tyre.
They decked the deck with flaming blooms,
Iresh plucked from every wondrous port,
on rugs from Persia’s looms
Throughout that voyage, all too short.
A lover pilot dashed to wade
Ashore beneath a precious load ;
A lover soldier gladly made
Her escort on the troublous road.
With laughing eyes, content, elate,
In radiant garb and flashing gem,
She urged her steed, and passed the gate,
The gate of old Jerusalem.
Soon joined a merry Pagan crowd
To seek her aid the priests to tease,
With ribald mirth and laughter loud
They mocked the canting Pharisees.
When, through the casement, morning crept
To paint her shining tresses gold,
The jaded sinners snored and slept
’Midst where the spent amphoras rolled.
So passed the days in joyous sin
Until, by tedium overcome,
They sought the squalid stews wherein
A Preacher led the city’s scum.
They vied in banter to deride
Those pale, pure, peaceful pioneers,
And saw not Mary at their side
Unsmiling, silent, through their jeers.
Her eyes were on the Preacher’s eyes,
Hungered her ears for every word,
Her heart was fighting to surmise
What meant the doctrines strange she heard.
With scoffing tired, her comrades passed
To seek fresh spirits like their own ;
But Mary, watching to the last,
Listened and left and dreamed alone.
She passed from out the Pagan view ;
None felt the circle of her arms ;
Her lovers found them lovers new,
And sin itself lost half its charms.
They mourned her lost, but toiled in stealth
And found her home, alone, apart,
But neither courage, wit nor wealth
Nor manliness could tempt her heart.
Their revelry no more she knew ;
Her torrid heart seemed hard and cold ;
The memories all hateful grew
Of fiery night-long loves of old.
But where the Preacher’s puzzling text
Was spoken, Mary found her place ;
(She cared not how His words perplexed
So she might gaze upon His face.
The love that once entranced the earth
With largesse, boundless, wanton, wild,
Had gathered to reveal its worth
And centre on the Preacher mild.
Yet loveliness, that once had brought
Upon their knees the leaders famed,
Drew from the Preacher not the thought
The beggar and the leper claimed.
She spoke. Upon the dusty street,
Pulsating, cast her body fair ;
Devoted, blind, she washed His feet
And dried them with her golden air.
Unmoved, the glance she cast above,
He saw. She shrank as from a knife,
To hear Him grant, instead of love,
The bauble of Eternal Life.
No score of sins did slie compound ;
Relentless, the atonement ran ;
Through life she wept that she had found
A God when she had sought a man.
VIC.
C.N.
A Night in the East.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE most interesting personage I met in
London was a sandwich-man. I met
him in Cranbourne-street; a starved little
fellow with twinkling eyes that lost the twinkle
when I talked “fog,” and he thought of a chil-
blained December. Fashionable London talks
Rome and Cairo and Palermo and the Riviera
then; but for the sandwich-man the winter means
nine hours a day of rheumatism and chills, and
walking in gutter-slush. Sunday weather is
notoriously the best of the week, but on those
days he must not walk, because church people
object to reading on a sandwich - board that
“Bertie’s Baking Powder Assassinates Bugs,”
though they do not mind the statement on a
permanent hoarding.
There are 2500 sandwich-men in London —all
failures, for this is the last job on earth—the dead
finish. They get 7s. a week for six days—if the
day begins in rain they lose the day and its
precious fourteenpence. (Seven shillings is the
fixed rate—they will never get above it, they will
never find better occupation, and they must never
beg.
It was seeing him that started me on that night
of horror in the East—-a night of blue-handed,
leprous, white-faced people, of grey, vile-smelling
shadows, of men and women moving furtively in
the fog—a phantasmagoria of misery.
We had dined at a club in Northumberland
Avenue—a place loaded with nightmare marbles ;
the stair-columns in pork-brawn, and the balus-
trade of imitation Roquefort cheese ; and after that
there was some happy and unwise drinking at
every club and pub. in Fleet-street; so that at 11
o’clock I was listening to an old match-woman’s
troubles in Wine Office Court, and at five minutes
past 11 was dancing a saraband with the afore-
said old woman in the rain of Fleet-street. A
policeman moved on my partner, although he had
no objection to my dancing, as I wore the tall
hat of a blameful life. With a fine, vinous
hatred of this injustice, the Anti-Christian—who
was my friend from Australia, via Barcelona,
Cordoba, Cadiz, and Ayr, and had whistled the
music for the dance—and myself, determined to
go East, and see how the poor lived.
It was a crowded night of unhappy experiences—
everywhere the leper-faced people, and the stench
of poverty. Billingsgate has lost its one-time dis-
tinctive bad language—the people I saw there
were too cold to swear. A night of horror it was
—yet with much sardonic humor in it; a night of
tramping from beer-shop to beer-shop in Shore-
ditch, Whitechapel and Bethnal Green, attended
by a retinue as ragged as Falstaff’s army; that
pretended to follow us because it liked us, but
really pursued us for the long still dank cold
mysterious penny beers we paid for, with some-
thing that looked like death at the bottom of
every pot. This, of course, was not moral
philanthropy—we were merely curious and not
philanthropic, and as we two could not hope to
regenerate these awful millions, the next kindest
thing was to give to a score or so of the homeless
ones nepenthe for a night.
Old Petticoat Lane, we found, had converted
itself to Middlesex-street, and Ratcliffe Highway
is St. George’s-road; but there are others —Went-
worth-street and Dorset-street, for instance, and
the pen halts at these.
All that night a bulky man in morning dress,
and a slighter man in tweeds, penetrated the
rabbit-runs in that great warren of dingy brick,
followed by the Legion of the Damned.
It was a brave night, and dawn found us very
merry. But the dawn brought little difference to
the atmosphere—all objects were indefinite as if
they were reflections in a dim glass.
We were in the city at dawn, but at seven
o’clock we crossed to Surrey yet again, and met
thousands of work-girls on the way to slavery.
And life seemed such a fine thing that with the
best intentions in the world I stopped the traffic
and said to the girls—nearly all of them white-
faced, and some of them pretty :
“ Where are you going, girls ?”
“Gawnter wairk, mister.”
“Not to-day—let there be one merry day.
Come with me, any fifty of you, and we’ll go to
Chingford and see some trees.’’
Some laughed and went on. Others frowned
and ran away. One pretty one—her prettiness
making her more suspicious than the others—
said :
“You mi-end whatcher sayin’; I’ll tell the
p’leece.”
This calling the p’leece seems to be their one
stand-by—although the calling of him would have
been futile. Why these suspicions ? Surely we
had made our innocence patent by inviting fifty
of them. Otherwise we must have appeared as
whales in sheep’s clothing.
Back to the city and into Whitechapel again.
The time was but half-past seven; yet three
women, two of them with an emaciated baby
apiece in arms, were drinking beer in a noisome
public-house. When the children cried they were
given sips of beer to “ keep them good.” We left
that “public” and walked for twenty minutes
through dark wastes of houses, built of the gloomy
London brick. There were women everywhere—
drunken, bleared women ; old baggy women,
waddling agglomerations of fat; women with
horrible pouchy faces, pendent chins like dewlaps,
cheeks like bacon flitches. And children every-
where, and not one happy, well-cared-for child
among the lot.
As if to give a jocularity to all this bitterness,
an organ-grinder around the corner began to play
“Home, Sweet Home,” and the children rushed
to him and we followed. After he had
played twenty bars of that dreary, fraudulent
song, which suggests that roaming ’mid pleasures
and palaces has not half the joys that “Home”
has, even in Whitechapel, the joke became too
horrible, and I stopped the man and bade him
play something lively, and in two minutes all the
little girls of the gutter were footing it to the tune
of “Susie” from “ The Girl up There.” “Grand-
father’s Clock ” is wildly hilarious compared with
a moral atrocity like “ Home, Sweet Home.”
The organ-grinder was one of the most miser-
able wretches I have ever seen. His face was as
white and weak and despairing as that of a
criminal I saw hanged at Ballarat in 1891, and
he had wept himself dry, and had taken two days
to do it.
In such a place as the East End of London
the poverty is so terrible that it prompts
strange shifts, which from their mere unexpected-
ness would be funny if they were not so tragic.
Therefore imagine the poverty of the people who
provide poverty with its music and its art.
So there is a lower depth than the last deep.
These people are all more or less inside the starva-
tion-line. What life was possible to this organ-
grinder, who offered luxury to people who are
mostly unable to buy the indispensable? His
position in the depth was best illustrated by the
fact that here he was, at seven of a foggy morn-
ing, beginning his day in the Four Streets of
Poverty—Hunger, Cold, Waiting, and Con-
tumelj.
He was not the only representative of the fine
arts in the street. A little further on, before a
public-house, its lighted bars trying to banish the
morning darkness, two draggle-tailed young
women with violins, and a weary little man with
a flute, were playing “The Honeysuckle and the
Bee,” with much pathos. They told us that they
started out at six every morning; and we asked
them to drink.
The man ordered “gin hot with,” whatever
that may be. The ladies very genteely asked for
“Pork wine.”
“What wine?” said I.
“Pork Wine.”
“Port Wine?” That was for the pleasure of
hearing them say it again.
“Yes, please—Pork Wine.”
There appears to be a big class in London that
cannot pronounce its t’s.
The fog thinned into a moist haze, and, restless
and tireless, we crossed the river again.
At 9 o’clock we were at a boot-cleaner’s box on
the Surrey side of the river, having our patent
leathers made beautiful before venturing to
Piccadilly, and our pale retinue was with us still.
A policeman came and put them all aside as if
they were yesterday’s Times, saying, “ Move on
there —move on there. Have you never seen
two gentlemen drunk before.” At that, finding
our disguise had been penetrated, we distributed
largesse and drove from the miasma of Surrey into
the fog of Middlesex.
Later, I visited Islington and all the sordidness
of North London, to assure myself that all the
poverty is not in the East; but nightmares are
unpleasant to remember.
How anybody can be sufficiently callous to sing
“Rule Britannia” in the midst of this awful
English poverty, I know not ; but to do the
English justice, the blasphemous anthem is sung
mostly by German Jews. Almost every day I
read in newspapers of families sleeping six in a
room—bare of furniture. There was a case yes-
terday of a family of nine, who had lived on bread
and water for many months ; the father had been
workless for seven months; one of the seven
children was dying of consumption, and a new
baby was expected.
Five million people in England and Wales are
in receipt of charitable relief —over ten million
are within the starvation mark. Eight thousand
landlords receive in rents £3s,ooo,ooo—the rents
of 580 peers amount to £14,000.000; and 35,000
men hold all the land against a population of over
40,000,000. And the average Englishman is callous
to all this.
“Don’t give her anything,” said a very respec-
table householder of Bloomsbury to me as I helped
a beggar-woman. “ You mustn’t encourage them.
I pay my poor-rates—and there’s the workhouse.”
But the workhouse is only gaol.
I found the last straw in a pawnbroker’s window
near Aldgate; and the last straw was a great bowl
filled with sets of false teeth, offered for sale as
“ great bargains.” The Teeth of the Dead ! Ugh !

Rheumatic and Qouty Affections.
(Br “Origin.”)
The unnatural retention of uric acid and other urinary and biliary poisons in the blood produces a
group of complaints which inflict upon humanity long continued suffering and intense pain. The
most common of these complaints are rheumatism, gout, lumbago, sciatica, and neuralgia. The kid-
neys and liver are the organs upon which Nature has imposed the task of extracting from the blood
certain matter which is being continuously manufactured in the body, owing to the wasting of the
tissues. It is as necessary to life that the wasting of the tissues must proceed uninterruptedly as it is
that the substance of the body must be regularly renewed by the food we eat, the water we drink, and
the air we breathe. It is equally necessary to health that the waste matter should be expelled from
the body continuously, for its presence in the blood entails disease or death.
The treatment of rheumatic and gouty affections by the outward application of liniments, oint-
ments and embrocations is seldom productive of much lasting benefit. They may afford temporary
relief in some cases, but they do not reach the seat of the disorder. The only way in which health can
be permanently restored, and pain permanently removed, is to take measures to ensure the regular
action of the kidneys and the liver. When these organs are performing-their allotted task naturally
and freely, the uric acid, and other urinary and biliary poisons, pass from the system through the
ordinary channels, and any suffering caused by the presence of such poisons in the blood is at an end.
Owing to its specific action, Warner’s Safe Cure, every moment during the past twenty-five years,
has been demonstrating its remarkable power of restoring' the kidneys and liver to health and activity.
Warner’s Safe Cure not only cures Bright’s Disease and other specific diseases of those vital organs,
but rheumatism, gout, gravel, stone, bladder troubles, indigestion, biliousness, anaemia, impure blood,
and all disorders caused by the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons, speedily yield
to the influence of the medicine, simply because of its healing and stimulating influence upon the
kidneys and liver. Cures thus effected are permanent, simply because they are natural.
A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some urine, passed the
first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is then
cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural color, or has particles floating about in i,t
the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be taken to restore their vigor, or
Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric poisoning will result. [2.

The Song of the Earth Spirit.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN the field of the far heaven changes
Through the day’s broken bars,
O’er the peaks of the mystical ranges
I arise with the stars.
But I pass in a wreath o’ white vapor
When the shadows are done,
Where the arms of the sloping hills taper
To the reach of the sun.
All the wild winds are huddled together,
Lying low in their lair;
Falls the down of a lone eagle’s feather
Like a stone in the air.
Oh, Beloved ! my bosom is dreary
As the lilt of a song,
When the heart of the singer is weary,
And the rhythm is long.
Thy somnolent voice as I listen,
O’er the leagues of the turf
Floats to me, and white thy limbs glisten
On the crest of the surf.
Is there aught neath the wide sky’s embowment
Like thy mighty unrest ?
Oh, to lean for one lingering moment
On thy turbulent breast!
How I long for thy waters to hold me
As they lap at my feet,
To flow over and fondle and fold me
Till our joy be complete !
N.S.W.
LOLA RIDGE.
A Study in Slops.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT is week-end. We—wives long familiar-
ised with the formula “’e ain’t workin’,”
and makers of stock garments for Mrs.
Dunn and the warehouse —drive our machines
furiously.
With fingers almost under the needle, we cram
the feeder’s greedy maw with “parts.” A leap of
the machine, an instant’s thunderous whirring, a
dexterous twist or two, and the oddly-shaped
“pieces” of common print emerge, transformed
into fashionable sleeves and “ cross-overs,” ready
for “ putting in.”
Twining round our feet are strings of belts —to
the uninitiated, hopelessly entangled with other
strings of yokes, cuffs, and trimmings.
Out of this seeming confusion we will evolve
garments, cheap and tasteful, and, at a glance,
well and truly made. It is the buyers who will
prove them the shams they are and reveal the
rottenness of the stitching, the lonely thread that
has the entire responsibility of the button, and that
shameless mockery, the button-hole. The arm-
hole’s agonising grip and the draughty amplitude
of the neck they will also view with sorrow.
These things concern us not. Sufficient to know
that we are “in a rush” and have no time for
niceties.
In the kitchen-dining-room, where we work,
Muddle sits enthroned. The dusty floor is
covered with clippings, the shoddy suite heaped
with “ stock.” Every corner has its rolls of uncut
prints f every wall its paper patterns. Sus-
pended from hooks hang ill-cut specimens of
our handicraft in the shape of dressing-gowns
calculated to render even a Juno ungainly.
The “what-not,” its photos and nick-nacks
prone, is swamped under goods ready and waiting
for the carrier, who tarrieth.
Mrs. Dunn, also working at high pressure, folds
and completes the dozens. The screech of tearing
calico cleaves the air. In the scullery the magpie
hoarsely croaks and demands provender.
Mrs. Dunn lacks method. She seldom “tidies
up,” and I suspect the week’s dishes are done simul-
taneously with the week’s washing on the Day of
Rest. At present the table bears squalid remains
of breakfast. The loaf has unmistakably been rent
in twain, the saucers each have had a generous
overflow, the cloth a liberal baptism, and every-
where is evidence that the breakfasters have
“ pigged ” in haste.
Space being needed for the work, the remnants
of the feast have been pushed nearer and nearer
the table’s edge, thereby jeopardising the crockery,
also the melancholy cruet, the daily appearance of
which is purely a matter of form, for the most
sanguine never expect to extract condiments from
its bottles. .
Seated on the sofa, amid crumbs, pms and pat-
terns, a cute little girl, Mrs. Dunn’s Gladys,
nimbly sews on tickets, and gives her mother
much gratuitous advice. From the pantry, black
from a sojourn among the saucepans, the lodger’s
baby crawls. . . , .
His mother is busy preparing her mother for the
weekly visit to the hospital. Down the stair
floats the invalid’s indignant protest against the
bath, an inhuman ordeal prescribed by the doctor,
and the only blot on an otherwise enjoyable out-
ing.. “ They ’ave no ’eart,” she shrills, “arskin’ a
person o’ my years to strip this bitter weather.
My Gord, it’ll gimme death, so it will.”
That Mrs. Dunn is no Feter, to deny her Lord,
the texts adorning the walls (framed in pmk
muslin, the property of the warehouse), and
mostly swaying by one pin, abundantly testify.
On the lodger hurrying down for mother’s stock-
ings, left drying in the oven, she is expostulated
with concerning mother’s language. “ Not that I
want to be ’ard,” says Mrs. Dunn, “for I’m a
widder myself, but I’m a registered factory, and
as such should ’ave respeck.” “Lor,” replies the
lodger, “she don’t mean no ’arm ; it’s an ’abit of
’ers” I b’lieve she’s light-’eaded meself. A fair
noosance she’s gettin’—wish th orspital d take
ei She hurries upstairs, and Mrs. Dunn turns
again to the folding, threatens the supercilious
Gladys with the flat-iron, and takes the last pin
from “Thou art my staff,” which slithers silently
behind the safe, there to lie till next cleaning day.
From upstairs come squeals of “My Gord,
mind my laig !”
Amid the heat and hurry and rush Mrs. Dunn,
now cutting, now folding, always talking, keeps
up an intermittent search, peculiar to the week-
end, for an odd blouse, mislaid, and now needed
to complete the order.
She makes raids on our work, up-ending the
piles of “pieces,” searching distractedly in im-
possible places, such as the machine-drawers or
the meat-safe, even insisting that we all stand up
several times to prove that we are not sitting on
it. Needless to say, ice are blamed for its disap-
pearance, and she threatens all and sundry with
the “bag.” But, knowing that her bark is worse
than her bite, we have little fear of her bitterness.
When she becomes too eloquent, we defend our-
selves by drowning her voice in the roar of our
machines.
Always in a hurry, her unbuttoned boots
clopping, Mrs. Dunn runs everywhere with in-
elegant bobbing steps that get her “no forrader.”
A knock at the door announcing a probable
dun, she runs to open it. She also runs into the
sharp edge of the hall-stand en route, whilst
chanting a commentary on the slackness of trade
and the scarcity of money, intended to warn the
tax-gatherer that her pocket is empty.
Her precautions are wasted, however. It is no
claimant for cash, merely a poor out-door “hand,”
who, sodden with rain and sullen with labor, is
now made to “sit up” over her unpunctuality.
“ Oh, Mrs. What-is-it, what a hour to bring
those suits. You noo they was wanted. What-
ever kep’ you ? What yer say ? Didn’t tell yer
they was wanted ? Well, I thought you ’ad the—
what is it ? ’’—(“Nous ” suggests Gladys). “ Yes,”
resumes her mother, catching the word imper
fectly, “ the mouse to know that, any’ow.”
We save the “hand” from further questioning
by a concerted roar of the machines, and Mrs.
Dunn grumbles on, unheeded.
“Any’ow, it’s me pays the thingumbob,” she
says. “ ’Ow’m Ito get to the ware’ouse ? ” (glanc-
ing at a clock with but one hand) “ and the
carrier late, and the pressing not done, and”—
here she runs for the iron, and, forgetting her
religious scruples, cries, “Damn that fire, it’s
going out again, and the 4 woody ’ not bin. ’ She
charges vindictively at the embers. The poker
leaves its smutty trail on her fingers, to be pre-
sently transferred to the pile of “ nights ” she is
folding.
Anxious to propitiate, and to divert attention
from her own shortcomings, the “ hand” casually
remarks that “ that woody won’t give more’n a
week’s tick to nobody.” The ruse is not success-
ful. Mrs. Dunn’s temper, being of the quality
that swallows camels all the week and strains at
gnats on Friday, takes offence at what she regards
as a slur on her solvency.
So the “hand,” realising that danger is in the
air, sulkily submits without further parley certain
show pieces for inspection. Luck is clearly against
her, for Mrs. Dunn, spitefully on the alert, rejects
these and pounces upon 'others that, for reasons of
her own, the “hand” had been trying to keep
dark. Mrs. Dunn’s zeal is rewarded by finding
these shamelessly “slopped.” Lop-sided garments
they are, suitable perhaps for nightmares, and of
a cut that will never “set” on mortal boy, and
she almost weeps as she sees that the unwritten
law that demands an appearance at least of good
workmanship has been wholly ignored.
Taking up one of the suits she now appears in
the character of a homely magician. A smart
shake of it produces a shower of little gilt buttons.
At a touch of her smutty fingers collars fall
asunder, and anchors leave their moorings, while
at one glance of her magic eye a luxuriant crop of
tags and machine oil appears as by a miracle.
She raves dismally of the wrath to come when
the ware’ouse shall have cast its eye on such
botches, orders the “hand” to smooth things
over as best she can, and, jawing still, turns again
to the fire.
Gingerly she opens a cupboard, the shelves of
which are running over with a heterogeneous
collection of odds and ends. Careful as she is,
most of the contents seize the opportunity to
slide out and deposit themselves on the floor.
From out the muddle she extracts a cake of fire-
kindlers, stands it on the embers, adds a generous
toss of kerosene, leans the iron against the flame,
and leaves it to its fate.
The lodger who, if she had “her rights,”
would be Mrs. Higgs, comes down to curl her
hair.
She takes sides, silently with the embittered
“hand,” who, still smarting under rebuke, winks
at the expense of the employer of labor. That
lady’s back being towards them, Mrs. Higgs in-
dulges in insulting gesture, and points a derisive
finger at the skimpy coverings of Mrs. Dunn’s
bulging limbs. Upon Gladys observing her
manoeuvres, the finger of derision becomes one of
admonition pointed at the unoffending baby, as
he appears momentarily at the pantry door, and
so, through sheer presence of mind, the situation
is saved. .
It grows late, and we are all pressed into
service as folders.
Mrs. Dunn clumps round giving orders, putting
finishing touches and still looking for the blouse.
“ The carrier ’ll soon be ’ere now, Mrs. ’lggs,”
she says. “You better get ’er down. ’E’s
always in a devil of a ’urry.”
Mrs. ’lggs sees the wisdom of this advice, and
presently the invalid is lowered by the aid of
many hands. Her gigantic white limbs appear-
ing, as it seems, from space nearly deprive baby of
his reason, and it is not till they are followed by
the familiar garments, spotted bonnet and bleached
sheep face that constitute the “ gammer ” he
knows, that he breathes again.
Mrs. Dunn, running, dressing and improvising
a pair of garters simultaneously, becomes “that
faint ” as to necessitate the outlay of threepence
on a glass of ale (per Gladys), a purchase highly
commended by Gammer, till seeing Mrs. Dunn
drink it on her own in the scullery, she piously
gives thanks that she is not greedy.
Gladys, always on the watch, tells of the car-
rier’s approach. And her mother, buoyed up by
beer, defies any but an angel to find the missing
blouse and consigns it to perdition, and the red-
faced carrier entering just then she has perforce
to abandon the search.
He seizes the monster parcels and heaves them
into the van. He returns for Gammer, who has
been towed up the passage. What inducement
she has offered for the lift I know not. Sufficient
that with many groans and prayers for her “laig’s”
safety she is safely hoisted, and with a wild cry
she disappears into the interior.
Mrs. Higgs, donning a hat like a racecourse,
smilingly and nimbly mounts beside the driver.
“Look at ’er,” ironically remarks the “hand.”
“You’d think she was Lady’Opeton—an ad a
’usband,” she adds aggrievedly.
In the doorway Mrs. Dunn mumbles good-byes
over her false teeth, and, pinning on her hair as
she stands, watches them drive off in triumph.
The little excitement is over. We return to
our slavery. T _
Shortly afterwards a juvenile Mrs. Dunn,
stayed, busked and buttoned, runs away to the
warehouse, and we are left alone.
The “ hand ” referring bitterly to Mrs. Dunn’s
rakish appearance, wonders “’ow she does it.”
Then she takes pity on the baby, “ deserted, pore
child,” rummages in the cot for his clothing, and
brings forth the missing blouse.

WILFLEY v. CARD CONCENTRATOR.
N
The judgment in the recent law suit does not apply to the original Card Concentrator, but only to two experimen al
Table Tops that were made for the purpose of trial during the absence of Mr. Guthridge from Australia, and before
the working of the Table was properly understood. We are, therefore, continuing the manufacture and sale of these
machines, and the mining industry will not be deprived of this valuable invention. According o e sworn evi ence,
the “ Card” has 50 per cent. “ greater capacity and makes higher concentrates and poorer tailings than the Wiltley.
With the * Card ' there is practically no loss of lead (mineral), as there is with the Wilney. . , ,
The “ Card ” Table may be seen on application, when evidence of its great superiority will be lurnishea.
GUTHRIDGE, Ltd., Sole Agents, 17 BOND STREET, SYDNEY,
